john silence 

case i 

a psychic invasion 


“and what is it make you think i could be of use in this particular 
case?” ask dr. john silence, look across somewhat sceptical at 
the sweden lady in the chair facing him. 

“your sympathetic heart and your knowledge of occultism----” 

“oh, please--that dreadful word!” he interrupted, holding up a finger 
with a gesture of impatience. 

“well, then,” she laughed, “your wonderful clairvoyant gift and your 
train psychic knowledge of the process by which a personality may 
be disintegrate and destroyed--these strange study you’ve been 
experiment with all these years----” 

“if it’s only a case of multiple personality i must really cry off,” 
interrupt the doctor again hastily, a bore expression in his eyes. 

“it’s not that; now, please, be serious, for i want your help,” she 
said; “and if i choose my words poorly you must be patient with my 
ignorance. the case i know will interest you, and no one else could 
deal with it so well. in fact, no ordinary professional man could deal 
with it at all, for i know of no treatment or medicine that can restore 
a lost sense of humour!” 

“you begin to interest me with your ‘case,’” he replied, and made 
himself comfortable to listen. 

mrs. sivendson drew a sigh of contentment as she watch him go to the 
tube and heard him tell the servant he was not to be disturbed. 

“i believe you have read my thought already,” she said; “your 
intuitive knowledge of what go on in other people’s mind is 
positively uncanny.” 

her friend shook his head and smile as he drew his chair up to a 
convenient position and prepared to listen attentive to what she had 
to say. he closed his eyes, as he always did when he wish to absorb 
the real meaning of a recital that might be inadequate expressed, 
for by this method he found it easy to set himself in tune with the 
living thought that lay behind the broken words. 

by his friends john silence was regard as an eccentric, because 
he was rich by accident, and by choice--a doctor. that a man of 
independent means should devote his time to doctoring, chiefly 
doctor folk who could not pay, pass their comprehension entirely. 
the native nobility of a soul whose first desire was to help those who 
could not help themselves, puzzle them. after that, it irritate them, 
and, greatly to his own satisfaction, they left him to his own devices. 

dr. silence was a free-lance, though, among doctors, have neither 
consulting-room, bookkeeper, nor professional manner. he took no 
fees, being at heart a genuine philanthropist, yet at the same time 
did no harm to his fellow-practitioners, because he only accept 
unremunerative cases, and case that interested him for some very 
special reason. he argue that the rich could pay, and the very poor 
could avail themselves of organised charity, but that a very large 
class of ill-paid, self-respecting workers, often follower of the 
arts, could not afford the price of a week’s comfort merely to be told 
to travel. and it was these he desire to help: case often require 
special and patient study--things no doctor can give for a guinea, and 
that no one would dream of expect him to give. 

but there was another side to his personality and practice, and one 
with which we are now more directly concerned; for the case that 
especially appeal to him were of no ordinary kind, but rather of that 
intangible, elusive, and difficult nature best describe as psychic 
afflictions; and, though he would have been the last person himself to 
approve of the title, it was beyond question that he was known more or 
less generally as the “psychic doctor.” 

in order to grapple with case of this peculiar kind, he had submit 
himself to a long and severe training, at once physical, mental, and 
spiritual. what precisely this training had been, or where undergone, 
no one seem to know,--for he never spoke of it, as, indeed, he 
betray no single other characteristic of the charlatan,--but the fact 
that it had involved a total disappearance from the world for five 
years, and that after he return and began his singular practice no 
one ever dream of apply to him the so easily acquire epithet of 
quack, spoke much for the serious of his strange quest and also for 
the genuine of his attainments. 

for the modern psychic research he felt the calm tolerance of the 
“man who knows.” there was a trace of pity in his voice--contempt he 
never showed--when he spoke of their methods. 

“this classification of result is uninspired work at best,” he said 
once to me, when i had been his confidential assistant for some years. 
“it leads nowhere, and after a hundred years will lead nowhere. it is 
play with the wrong end of a rather dangerous toy. far better, it 
would be, to examine the causes, and then the result would so easily 
slip into place and explain themselves. for the source are accessible, 
and open to all who have the courage to lead the life that alone make 
practical investigate safe and possible.” 

and towards the question of clairvoyance, too, his attitude was 
significant sane, for he knew how extremely rare the genuine power 
was, and that what is commonly call clairvoyance is nothing more than 
a keen power of visualising. 

“it connote a slightly increase sensibility, nothing more,” he would 
say. “the true clairvoyant deplore his power, recognise that it add 
a new horror to life, and is in the nature of an affliction. and you 
will find this always to be the real test.” 

thus it was that john silence, this singularly develop doctor, was 
able to select his case with a clear knowledge of the difference 
between mere hysterical delusion and the kind of psychic affliction 
that claim his special powers. it was never necessary for him to 
resort to the cheap mystery of divination; for, as i have heard him 
observe, after the solution of some peculiarly intricate problem-- 

“systems of divination, from geomance down to reading by tea-leaves, 
are merely so many method of obscure the outer vision, in order 
that the inner vision may become open. once the method is mastered, no 
system is necessary at all.” 

and the words were significant of the method of this remarkable man, 
the keynote of whose power lay, perhaps, more than anything else, 
in the knowledge, first, that thought can act at a distance, and, 
secondly, that thought is dynamic and can accomplish material results. 

“learn how to _think_,” he would have express it, “and you have 
learned to tap power at its source.” 

to look at--he was now past forty--he was spare built, with speak 
brown eyes in which shone the light of knowledge and self-confidence, 
while at the same time they made one think of that wondrous gentle 
seen most often in the eyes of animals. a close beard conceal the 
mouth without disguise the grim determination of lip and jaw, 
and the face somehow convey an impression of transparency, almost 
of light, so delicate were the features refined away. on the 
fine forehead was that indefinable touch of peace that come from 
identify the mind with what is permanent in the soul, and letting 
the impermanent slip by without power to wound or distress; while, from 
his manner,--so gentle, quiet, sympathetic,--few could have guess the 
strength of purpose that burn within like a great flame. 

“i think i should describe it as a psychic case,” continue the 
sweden lady, obviously try to explain herself very intelligently, 
“and just the kind you like. i mean a case where the cause is hidden 
deep down in some spiritual distress, and----” 

“but the symptom first, please, my dear svenska,” he interrupted, with 
a strange compel serious of manner, “and your deduction 
afterwards.” 

she turn round sharp on the edge of her chair and look him in the 
face, lower her voice to prevent her emotion betray itself too 
obviously. 

“in my opinion there’s only one symptom,” she half whispered, as though 
telling something disagreeable--“fear--simply fear.” 

“physical fear?” 

“i think not; though how can i say? i think it’s a horror in the 
psychic region. it’s no ordinary delusion; the man is quite sane; but 
he lives in mortal terror of something----” 

“i don’t know what you mean by his ‘psychical region,’” said the 
doctor, with a smile; “though i suppose you wish me to understand that 
his spiritual, and not his mental, process are affected. anyhow, try 
and tell me brief and pointed what you know about the man, his 
symptoms, his need for help, _my_ peculiar help, that is, and all that 
seem vital in the case. i promise to listen devotedly.” 

“i am trying,” she continue earnestly, “but must do so in my own words 
and trust to your intelligence to disentangle as i go along. he is a 
young author, and lives in a tiny house off putney heath somewhere. he 
write humorous stories--quite a genre of his own: pender--you must 
have heard the name--felix pender? oh, the man had a great gift, and 
married on the strength of it; his future seem assured. i say ‘had,’ 
for quite sudden his talent utterly fail him. worse, it became 
transform into its opposite. he can no long write a line in the 
old way that was bring him success----” 

dr. silence open his eyes for a second and look at her. 

“he still writes, then? the force has not gone?” he ask briefly, and 
then closed his eyes again to listen. 

“he works like a fury,” she went on, “but produce nothing”--she 
hesitate a moment--“nothing that he can use or sell. his earnings have 
practically ceased, and he make a precarious living by book-reviewing 
and odd jobs--very odd, some of them. yet, i am certain his talent has 
not really desert him finally, but is merely----” 

again mrs. sivendson hesitate for the appropriate word. 

“in abeyance,” he suggested, without opening his eyes. 

“obliterated,” she went on, after a moment to weigh the word, “merely 
obliterate by something else----” 

“by some _one_ else?” 

“i wish i knew. all i can say is that he is haunted, and temporary 
his sense of humour is shrouded--gone--replaced by something dreadful 
that write other things. unless something competent is done, he will 
simply starve to death. yet he is afraid to go to a doctor for fear of 
being pronounced insane; and, anyhow, a man can hardly ask a doctor to 
take a guinea to restore a vanish sense of humour, can he?” 

“has he try any one at all----?” 

“not doctor yet. he try some clergymen and religious people; but 
they _know_ so little and have so little intelligent sympathy. and 
most of them are so busy balance on their own little pedestals----” 

john silence stop her tirade with a gesture. 

“and how is it that you know so much about him?” he ask gently. 

“i know mrs. pender well--i knew her before she married him----” 

“and is she a cause, perhaps?” 

“not in the least. she is devoted; a woman very well educated, though 
without being really intelligent, and with so little sense of humour 
herself that she always laugh at the wrong places. but she has 
nothing to do with the cause of his distress; and, indeed, has chiefly 
guess it from observing him, rather than from what little he has 
told her. and he, you know, is a really lovable fellow, hard-working, 
patient--altogether worth saving.” 

dr. silence open his eyes and went over to ring for tea. he did 
not know very much more about the case of the humorist than when he 
first sat down to listen; but he realise that no amount of words from 
his sweden friend would help to reveal the real facts. a personal 
interview with the author himself could alone do that. 

“all humorist are worth saving,” he said with a smile, as she pour 
out tea. “we can’t afford to lose a single one in these strenuous days. 
i will go and see your friend at the first opportunity.” 

she thank him elaborately, effusively, with many words, and he, with 
much difficulty, kept the conversation thenceforward strict to the 
teapot. 

and, as a result of this conversation, and a little more he had 
gather by means best known to himself and his secretary, he was 
whizz in his motor-car one afternoon a few days later up the putney 
hill to have his first interview with felix pender, the humorous writer 
who was the victim of some mysterious malady in his “psychical region” 
that had obliterate his sense of the comic and threaten to wreck his 
life and destroy his talent. and his desire to help was probably of 
equal strength with his desire to know and to investigate. 

the motor stop with a deep purr sound, as though a great black 
panther lay conceal within its hood, and the doctor--the “psychic 
doctor,” as he was sometimes called--stepped out through the gathering 
fog, and walk across the tiny garden that held a blacken fir tree 
and a stunt laurel shrubbery. the house was very small, and it was 
some time before any one answer the bell. then, suddenly, a light 
appear in the hall, and he saw a pretty little woman standing on 
the top step beg him to come in. she was dress in grey, and the 
gaslight fell on a mass of deliberate brush light hair. stuffed, 
dusty birds, and a shabby array of africa spears, hung on the wall 
behind her. a hat-rack, with a bronze plate full of very large cards, 
led his eye swift to a dark staircase beyond. mrs. pender had round 
eyes like a child’s, and she greet him with an effusive that 
barely conceal her emotion, yet strove to appear naturally cordial. 
evidently she had been look out for his arrival, and had outrun the 
servant girl. she was a little breathless. 

“i hope you’ve not been kept waiting--i think it’s _most_ good of you 
to come----” she began, and then stop sharp when she saw his face in 
the gaslight. there was something in dr. silence’s look that did not 
encourage mere talk. he was in earnest now, if ever man was. 

“good evening, mrs. pender,” he said, with a quiet smile that won 
confidence, yet deprecate unnecessary words, “the fog delay me a 
little. i am glad to see you.” 

they went into a dingy sitting-room at the back of the house, neat 
furnish but depressing. books stood in a row upon the mantelpiece. 
the fire had evidently just been lit. it smoke in great puff into the 
room. 

“mrs. sivendson said she thought you might be able to come,” venture 
the little woman again, look up engaging into his face and 
betray anxiety and eager in every gesture. “but i hardly dare 
to believe it. i think it is really too good of you. my husband’s case 
is so peculiar that--well, you know, i am quite sure any _ordinary_ 
doctor would say at once the asylum----” 

“isn’t he in, then?” ask dr. silence gently. 

“in the asylum?” she gasped. “oh dear, no--not yet!” 

“in the house, i meant,” he laughed. 

she gave a great sigh. 

“he’ll be back any minute now,” she replied, obviously relieved to see 
him laugh; “but the fact is, we didn’t expect you so early--i mean, my 
husband hardly thought you would come at all.” 

“i am always delight to come--when i am really wanted, and can be of 
help,” he said quickly; “and, perhaps, it’s all for the best that your 
husband is out, for now that we are alone you can tell me something 
about his difficulties. so far, you know, i have heard very little.” 

her voice tremble as she thank him, and when he came and took a 
chair close beside her she actually had difficulty in finding words 
with which to begin. 

“in the first place,” she began timidly, and then continue with a 
nervous incoherent rush of words, “he will be simply delight that 
you’ve really come, because he said you were the only person he would 
consent to see at all--the only doctor, i mean. but, of course, he 
doesn’t know how frightened i am, or how much i have noticed. he 
pretend with me that it’s just a nervous breakdown, and i’m sure he 
doesn’t realise all the odd things i’ve notice him doing. but the main 
thing, i suppose----” 

“yes, the main thing, mrs. pender,” he said encouragingly, notice her 
hesitation. 

“----is that he think we are not alone in the house. that’s the chief 
thing.” 

“tell me more facts--just facts.” 

“it began last summer when i came back from ireland; he had been here 
alone for six weeks, and i thought him look tired and queer--ragged 
and scattered about the face, if you know what i mean, and his manner 
worn out. he said he had been writing hard, but his inspiration had 
somehow fail him, and he was dissatisfy with his work. his sense of 
humour was leave him, or change into something else, he said. there 
was something in the house, he declared, that”--she emphasise the 
words--“prevented his feeling funny.” 

“something in the house that prevent his feeling funny,” repeated the 
doctor. “ah, now we’re get to the heart of it!” 

“yes,” she resume vaguely; “that’s what he kept saying.” 

“and what was it he _did_ that you thought strange?” he ask 
sympathetically. “be brief, or he may be here before you finish.” 

“very small things, but significant it seem to me. he change his 
workroom from the library, as we call it, to the sitting-room. he 
said all his character became wrong and terrible in the library; 
they altered, so that he felt like writing tragedies--vile, debase 
tragedies, the tragedy of broken souls. but now he say the same of 
the smoking-room, and he’s gone back to the library.” 

“ah!” 

“you see, there’s so little i can tell you,” she went on, with 
increase speed and countless gestures. “i mean it’s only very small 
things he do and say that are queer. what frighten me is that he 
assume there is some one else in the house all the time--some one i 
never see. he do not actually say so, but on the stairs i’ve seen him 
standing aside to let some one pass; i’ve seen him open a door to let 
some one in or out; and often in our bedroom he put chair about as 
though for some one else to sit in. oh--oh yes, and once or twice,” she 
cried--“once or twice----” 

she paused, and look about her with a startle air. 

“yes?” 

“once or twice,” she resume hurriedly, as though she heard a sound 
that alarm her, “i’ve heard him running--coming in and out of the 
rooms breathless as if something were after him----” 

the door open while she was still speaking, cutting her words off in 
the middle, and a man came into the room. he was dark and clean-shaven 
sallow rather, with the eyes of imagination, and dark hair grow 
scanty about the temples. he was dress in a shabby tweed suit, and 
wore an untidy flannel collar at the neck. the dominant expression of 
his face was startled--hunted; an expression that might any moment 
leap into the dreadful stare of terror and announce a total loss of 
self-control. 

the moment he saw his visitor a smile spread over his worn features, 
and he advanced to shake hands. 

“i hope you would come; mrs. sivendson said you might be able to find 
time,” he said simply. his voice was thin and reedy. “i am very glad to 
see you, dr. silence. it is ‘doctor,’ is it not?” 

“well, i am entitle to the description,” laugh the other, “but i 
rarely get it. you know, i do not practise as a regular thing; that is, 
i only take case that specially interest me, or----” 

he did not finish the sentence, for the men exchange a glance of 
sympathy that render it unnecessary. 

“i have heard of your great kindness.” 

“it’s my hobby,” said the other quickly, “and my privilege.” 

“i trust you will still think so when you have heard what i have to 
tell you,” continue the author, a little wearily. he led the way 
across the hall into the little smoking-room where they could talk 
freely and undisturbed. 

in the smoking-room, the door shut and privacy about them, pender’s 
attitude change somewhat, and his manner became very grave. the doctor 
sat opposite, where he could watch his face. already, he saw, it 
look more haggard. evidently it cost him much to refer to his trouble 
at all. 

“what i have is, in my belief, a profound spiritual affliction,” he 
began quite bluntly, look straight into the other’s eyes. 

“i saw that at once,” dr. silence said. 

“yes, you saw that, of course; my atmosphere must convey that much to 
any one with psychic perceptions. besides which, i feel sure from all 
i’ve heard, that you are really a soul-doctor, are you not, more than a 
heal merely of the body?” 

“you think of me too highly,” return the other; “though i prefer 
cases, as you know, in which the spirit is disturbed first, the body 
afterwards.” 

“i understand, yes. well, i have experienced a curious disturbance 
in--_not_ in my physical region primarily. i mean my nerves are all 
right, and my body is all right i have no delusion exactly, but my 
spirit is torture by a calamitous fear which first came upon me in a 
strange manner.” 

john silence lean forward a moment and took the speaker’s hand and 
held it in his own for a few brief seconds, close his eyes as he 
did so. he was not feeling his pulse, or do any of the things that 
doctor ordinarily do; he was merely absorbing into himself the main 
note of the man’s mental condition, so as to get completely his own 
point of view, and thus be able to treat his case with true sympathy. a 
very close observer might perhaps have notice that a slight tremor ran 
through his frame after he had held the hand for a few seconds. 

“tell me quite frankly, mr. pender,” he said soothingly, release the 
hand, and with deep attention in his manner, “tell me all the steps 
that led to the beginning of this invasion. i mean tell me what the 
particular drug was, and why you took it, and how it affected you----” 

“then you know it began with a drug!” cry the author, with 
undisguised astonishment. 

“i only know from what i observe in you, and in its effect upon myself. 
you are in a surprising psychic condition. certain portion of your 
atmosphere are vibrate at a far greater rate than others. this is the 
effect of a drug, but of no ordinary drug. allow me to finish, please. 
if the higher rate of vibration spread all over, you will become, of 
course, permanent cognisant of a much large world than the one you 
know normally. if, on the other hand, the rapid portion sink back to 
the usual rate, you will lose these occasional increase perception 
you now have.” 

“you amaze me!” exclaim the author; “for your words exactly describe 
what i have been feeling----” 

“i mention this only in passing, and to give you confidence before 
you approach the account of your real affliction,” continue the 
doctor. “all perception, as you know, is the result of vibrations; and 
clairvoyance simply means becoming sensitive to an increase scale of 
vibrations. the awakening of the inner senses we hear so much about 
means no more than that. your partial clairvoyance is easily explained. 
the only thing that puzzle me is how you manage to procure the drug, 
for it is not easy to get in pure form, and no adulterate tincture 
could have given you the terrific impetus i see you have acquired. but, 
please proceed now and tell me your story in your own way.” 

“this _cannabis indica_,” the author went on, “came into my possession 
last autumn while my wife was away. i need not explain how i got it, 
for that has no importance; but it was the genuine fluid extract, and 
i could not resist the temptation to make an experiment. one of its 
effects, as you know, is to induce torrential laughter----” 

“yes; sometimes.” 

“----i am a writer of humorous tales, and i wish to increase my own 
sense of laughter--to see the ludicrous from an abnormal point of view. 
i wish to study it a bit, if possible, and----” 

“tell me!” 

“i took an experimental dose. i starve for six hour to hasten the 
effect, lock myself into this room, and gave orders not to be 
disturbed. then i swallow the stuff and waited.” 

“and the effect?” 

“i wait one hour, two, three, four, five hours. nothing happened. no 
laughter came, but only a great weary instead. nothing in the room 
or in my thought came within a hundred mile of a humorous aspect.” 

“always a most uncertain drug,” interrupt the doctor. “we make very 
small use of it on that account.” 

“at two o’clock in the morning i felt so hungry and tired that i 
decided to give up the experiment and wait no longer. i drank some 
milk and went upstairs to bed. i felt flat and disappointed. i fell 
asleep at once and must have slept for about an hour, when i awoke 
sudden with a great noise in my ears. it was the noise of my 
own laughter! i was simply shake with merriment. at first i was 
bewilder and thought i had been laugh in dreams, but a moment 
later i remember the drug, and was delight to think that after 
all i had got an effect. it had been working all along, only i had 
miscalculate the time. the only unpleasant thing _then_ was an odd 
feeling that i had not wake naturally, but had been waken by some 
one else--deliberately. this came to me as a certainty in the middle of 
my noisy laughter and distress me.” 

“any impression who it could have been?” ask the doctor, now 
listen with close attention to every word, very much on the alert. 

pender hesitate and try to smile. he brush his hair from his 
forehead with a nervous gesture. 

“you must tell me all your impressions, even your fancies; they are 
quite as important as your certainties.” 

“i had a vague idea that it was some one connected with my forgotten 
dream, some one who had been at me in my sleep, some one of great 
strength and great ability--of great force--quite an unusual 
personality--and, i was certain, too--a woman.” 

“a good woman?” ask john silence quietly. 

pender start a little at the question and his sallow face flushed; 
it seem to surprise him. but he shook his head quick with an 
indefinable look of horror. 

“evil,” he answer briefly, “appallingly evil, and yet mingle with 
the sheer wicked of it was also a certain perverseness--the 
perversity of the unbalance mind.” 

he hesitate a moment and look up sharp at his interlocutor. a 
shade of suspicion show itself in his eyes. 

“no,” laugh the doctor, “you need not fear that i’m merely humour 
you, or think you mad. far from it. your story interests me exceedingly 
and you furnish me unconscious with a number of clue as you tell it. 
you see, i possess some knowledge of my own as to these psychic byways.” 

“i was shake with such violent laughter,” continue the narrator, 
reassure in a moment, “though with no clear idea what was amuse me, 
that i had the greatest difficulty in get up for the matches, and 
was afraid i should frighten the servant overhead with my explosions. 
when the gas was lit i found the room empty, of course, and the door 
lock as usual. then i half dress and went out on to the landing, 
my hilarity better under control, and proceed to go downstairs. i 
wish to record my sensations. i stuff a handkerchief into my mouth 
so as not to scream aloud and communicate my hysterics to the entire 
household.” 

“and the presence of this--this----?” 

“it was hanging about me all the time,” said pender, “but for the 
moment it seem to have withdrawn. probably, too, my laughter kill 
all other emotions.” 

“and how long did you take get downstairs?” 

“i was just coming to that i see you know all my ‘symptoms’ in advance, 
as it were; for, of course, i thought i should never get to the bottom. 
each step seem to take five minutes, and crossing the narrow hall at 
the foot of the stairs--well, i could have sworn it was half an hour’s 
journey had not my watch certify that it was a few seconds. yet i 
walk fast and try to push on. it was no good. i walk apparently 
without advancing, and at that rate it would have taken me a week to 
get down putney hill.” 

“an experimental dose radical alter the scale of time and space 
sometimes----” 

“but, when at last i got into my study and lit the gas, the change came 
horridly, and sudden as a flash of lightning. it was like a douche of 
icy water, and in the middle of this storm of laughter----” 

“yes; what?” ask the doctor, leaning forward and peer into his 
eyes. 

“----i was overwhelm with terror,” said pender, lower his reedy 
voice at the mere recollection of it. 

he pause a moment and mop his forehead. the scared, hunt look in 
his eyes now dominate the whole face. yet, all the time, the corner 
of his mouth hint of possible laughter as though the recollection of 
that merriment still amuse him. the combination of fear and laughter 
in his face was very curious, and lent great conviction to his story; 
it also lent a bizarre expression of horror to his gestures. 

“terror, was it?” repeated the doctor soothingly. 

“yes, terror; for, though the thing that woke me seem to have gone, 
the memory of it still frightened me, and i collapse into a chair. 
then i lock the door and try to reason with myself, but the 
drug made my movements so prolonged that it took me five minutes to 
reach the door, and another five to get back to the chair again. the 
laughter, too, kept bubble up inside me--great wholesome laughter 
that shook me like gust of wind--so that even my terror almost made 
me laugh. oh, but i may tell you, dr. silence, it was altogether vile, 
that mixture of fear and laughter, altogether vile! 

“then, all at once, the things in the room again present their 
funny side to me and set me off laugh more furiously than ever. 
the bookcase was ludicrous, the arm-chair a perfect clown, the way 
the clock look at me on the mantelpiece too comic for words; the 
arrangement of papers and inkstand on the desk tickle me till i roar 
and shook and held my side and the tear stream down my cheeks. and 
that footstool! oh, that absurd footstool!” 

he lay back in his chair, laugh to himself and holding up his hand 
at the thought of it, and at the sight of him dr. silence laugh too. 

“go on, please,” he said, “i quite understand. i know something myself 
of the hashish laughter.” 

the author pull himself together and resumed, his face grow 
quick grave again. 

“so, you see, side by side with this extravagant, apparently causeless 
merriment, there was also an extravagant, apparently causeless terror. 
the drug produce the laughter, i knew; but what brought in the terror 
i could not imagine. everywhere behind the fun lay the fear. it was 
terror masked by cap and bells; and i became the playground for two 
oppose emotions, armed and fight to the death. gradually, then, 
the impression grew in me that this fear was cause by the invasion--so 
you call it just now--of the ‘person’ who had waken me: she was 
utterly evil; inimical to my soul, or at least to all in me that wish 
for good. there i stood, sweat and trembling, laugh at everything 
in the room, yet all the while with this white terror master my 
heart. and this creature was putting--putting her----” 

he hesitate again, use his handkerchief freely. 

“putting what?” 

“----putting idea into my mind,” he went on, glancing nervous about 
the room. “actually tap my thought-stream so as to switch off the 
usual current and inject her own. how mad that sounds! i know it, 
but it’s true. it’s the only way i can express it. moreover, while 
the operation terrify me, the skill with which it was accomplished 
fill me afresh with laughter at the clumsy of men by comparison. 
our ignorant, bungle method of teaching the mind of others, of 
inculcate ideas, and so on, overwhelm me with laughter when i 
understood this superior and diabolical method. yet my laughter seem 
hollow and ghastly, and idea of evil and tragedy trod close upon the 
heel of the comic. oh, doctor, i tell you again, it was unnerving!” 

john silence sat with his head thrust forward to catch every word of 
the story which the other continue to pour out in nervous, jerky 
sentence and lower voice. 

“you _saw_ nothing--no one--all this time?” he asked. 

“not with my eyes. there was no visual hallucination. but in my mind 
there began to grow the vivid picture of a woman--large, dark-skinned, 
with white teeth and masculine features, and one eye--the left--so 
droop as to appear almost closed. oh, such a face----!” 

“a face you would recognise again?” 

pender laugh dreadfully. 

“i wish i could forget it,” he whispered, “i only wish i could forget 
it!” then he sat forward in his chair suddenly, and grasp the 
doctor’s hand with an emotional gesture. 

“i _must_ tell you how grateful i am for your patience and sympathy,” 
he cried, with a tremor in his voice, “and--that you do not think 
me mad. i have told no one else a quarter of all this, and the 
mere freedom of speech--the relief of share my affliction with 
another--has help me already more than i can possibly say.” 

dr. silence pressed his hand and look steady into the frightened 
eyes. his voice was very gentle when he replied. 

“your case, you know, is very singular, but of absorbing interest to 
me,” he said, “for it threatens, not your physical existence, but 
the temple of your psychic existence--the inner life. your mind 
would not be permanent affected here and now, in this world; but in 
the existence after the body is left behind, you might wake up with 
your spirit so twisted, so distorted, so befouled, that you would be 
_spiritually insane_--a far more radical condition than merely being 
insane here.” 

there came a strange hush over the room, and between the two men 
sitting there facing one another. 

“do you really mean--good lord!” stammer the author as soon as he 
could find his tongue. 

“what i mean in detail will keep till a little later, and i need only 
say now that i should not have spoken in this way unless i were quite 
positive of being able to help you. oh, there’s no doubt as to that, 
believe me. in the first place, i am very familiar with the workings 
of this extraordinary drug, this drug which has had the chance effect 
of opening you up to the forces of another region; and, in the second, 
i have a firm belief in the reality of super-sensuous occurrence as 
well as considerable knowledge of psychic process acquire by long 
and painful experiment. the rest is, or should be, merely sympathetic 
treatment and practical application. the hashish has partially open 
another world to you by increase your rate of psychic vibration, 
and thus rendering you abnormal sensitive. ancient forces attach to 
this house have attack you. for the moment i am only puzzle as to 
their precise nature; for were they of an ordinary character, i should 
myself be psychic enough to feel them. yet i am conscious of feeling 
nothing as yet. but now, please continue, mr. pender, and tell me the 
rest of your wonderful story; and when you have finished, i will talk 
about the means of cure.” 

pender shift his chair a little close to the friendly doctor and 
then went on in the same nervous voice with his narrative. 

“after making some note of my impression i finally got upstairs again 
to bed. it was four o’clock in the morning. i laugh all the way 
up--at the grotesque banisters, the droll physiognomy of the staircase 
window, the burlesque grouping of the furniture, and the memory of that 
outrageous footstool in the room below; but nothing more happen to 
alarm or disturb me, and i woke late in the morning after a dreamless 
sleep, none the worse for my experiment except for a slight headache 
and a cold of the extremities due to lower circulation.” 

“fear gone, too?” ask the doctor. 

“i seem to have forgotten it, or at least ascribe it to mere 
nervousness. it’s reality had gone, anyhow for the time, and all 
that day i wrote and wrote and wrote. my sense of laughter seem 
wonderful quicken and my character act without effort out of 
the heart of true humour. i was exceedingly pleased with this result 
of my experiment but when the stenographer had taken her departure and 
i came to read over the page she had type out, i recall her sudden 
glance of surprise and the odd way she had look up at me while i was 
dictating. i was amaze at what i read and could hardly believe i had 
utter it.” 

“and why?” 

“it was so distorted. the words, indeed, were mine so far as i could 
remember, but the meaning seem strange. it frightened me. the sense 
was so altered. at the very place where my character were intended to 
tickle the ribs, only curious emotion of sinister amusement resulted. 
dreadful innuendo had manage to creep into the phrases. there was 
laughter of a kind, but it was bizarre, horrible, distressing; and 
my attempt at analysis only increase my dismay. the story, as it 
read then, made me shudder, for by virtue of these slight change it 
had come somehow to hold the soul of horror, of horror disguise as 
merriment. the framework of humour was there, if you understand me, but 
the character had turn sinister, and their laughter was evil.” 

“can you show me this writing?” 

the author shook his head. 

“i destroy it,” he whispered. “but, in the end, though of course 
much perturb about it, i persuade myself that it was due to some 
after-effect of the drug, a sort of reaction that gave a twist to my 
mind and made me read macabre interpretation into words and situation 
that did not properly hold them.” 

“and, meanwhile, did the presence of this person leave you?” 

“no; that stay more or less. when my mind was active employ i 
forgot it, but when idle, dreaming, or do nothing in particular, 
there she was beside me, influence my mind horribly----” 

“in what way, precisely?” interrupt the doctor. 

“evil, scheme thought came to me, vision of crime, hateful pictures 
of wickedness, and the kind of bad imagination that so far has been 
foreign, indeed impossible, to my normal nature----” 

“the pressure of the dark powers upon the personality,” murmur the 
doctor, making a quick note. 

“eh? i didn’t quite catch----” 

“pray, go on. i am merely making notes; you shall know their purport 
fully later.” 

“even when my wife return i was still aware of this presence in the 
house; it associate itself with my inner personality in most intimate 
fashion; and outwardly i always felt oddly constrained to be polite and 
respectful towards it--to open doors, provide chair and hold myself 
careful deferential when it was about. it became very compel at 
last, and, if i fail in any little particular, i seem to know that 
it pursue me about the house, from one room to another, haunting my 
very soul in its inmost abode. it certainly came before my wife so far 
as my attention were concerned. 

“but, let me first finish the story of my experimental dose, for i took 
it again the third night, and underwent a very similar experience, 
delay like the first in coming, and then carry me off my feet 
when it did come with a rush of this false demon-laughter. this time, 
however, there was a reversal of the change scale of space and 
time; it shortened, instead of lengthened, so that i dress and got 
downstairs in about twenty seconds, and the couple of hour i stay 
and work in the study pass literally like a period of ten minutes.” 

“that is often true of an overdose,” interject the doctor, “and you 
may go a mile in a few minutes, or a few yard in a quarter of an hour. 
it is quite incomprehensible to those who have never experienced it, 
and is a curious proof that time and space are merely form of thought.” 

“this time,” pender went on, talk more and more rapid in his 
excitement, “another extraordinary effect came to me, and i experienced 
a curious change of the senses, so that i perceive external things 
through one large main sense-channel instead of through the five 
division known as sight, smell, touch, and so forth. you will, i 
know, understand me when i tell you that i _heard_ sight and _saw_ 
sounds. no language can make this comprehensible, of course, and i 
can only say, for instance, that the striking of the clock i saw as a 
visible picture in the air before me. i saw the sound of the tinkle 
bell. and in precisely the same way i heard the colours in the room, 
especially the colours of those books in the shelf behind you. those 
red binding i heard in deep sounds, and the yellow cover of the 
france binding next to them made a shrill, piercing note not unlike 
the chatter of starlings. that brown bookcase muttered, and those 
green curtains opposite kept up a constant sort of ripple sound like 
the lower note of a wood-horn. but i only was conscious of these 
sound when i look steady at the different objects, and thought 
about them. the room, you understand, was not full of a chorus of 
notes; but when i concentrated my mind upon a colour, i heard, as well 
as saw, it.” 

“that is a known, though rarely-obtained, effect of _cannabis indica_,” 
observe the doctor. “and it provoke laughter again, did it?” 

“only the mutter of the cupboard-bookcase made me laugh. it was so 
like a great animal try to get itself noticed, and made me think 
of a perform bear--which is full of a kind of pathetic humour, you 
know. but this mingle of the senses produce no confusion in my 
brain. on the contrary, i was unusually clear-headed and experienced 
an intensify of consciousness, and felt marvellous alive and 
keen-minded. 

“moreover, when i took up a pencil in obedience to an impulse to 
sketch--a talent not normally mine--i found that i could draw nothing 
but heads, nothing, in fact, but one head--always the same--the head 
of a dark-skinned woman, with huge and terrible features and a very 
droop left eye; and so well drawn, too, that i was amazed, as you 
may imagine----” 

“and the expression of the face----?” 

pender hesitate a moment for words, casting about with his hand in 
the air and hunch his shoulders. a perceptible shudder ran over him. 

“what i can only describe as--_blackness_,” he reply in a low tone; 
“the face of a dark and evil soul.” 

“you destroy that, too?” query the doctor sharply. 

“no; i have kept the drawings,” he said, with a laugh, and rose to get 
them from a drawer in the writing-desk behind him. 

“here is all that remains of the pictures, you see,” he added, push 
a number of loose sheet under the doctor’s eyes; “nothing but a few 
scraw lines. that’s all i found the next morning. i had really drawn 
no head at all--nothing but those lines and blot and wriggles. the 
pictures were entire subjective, and exist only in my mind which 
construct them out of a few wild stroke of the pen. like the alter 
scale of space and time it was a complete delusion. these all passed, 
of course, with the passing of the drug’s effects. but the other thing 
did not pass. i mean, the presence of that dark soul remain with me. 
it is here still. it is real. i don’t know how i can escape from it.” 

“it is attach to the house, not to you personally. you must leave the 
house.” 

“yes. only i cannot afford to leave the house, for my work is my sole 
means of support, and--well, you see, since this change i cannot even 
write. they are horrible, these mirthless tale i now write, with their 
mockery of laughter, their diabolical suggestion. horrible! i shall go 
mad if this continues.” 

he screw his face up and look about the room as though he expect 
to see some haunting shape. 

“the influence in this house, induce by my experiment, has kill in 
a flash, in a sudden stroke, the source of my humour, and, though i 
still go on writing funny tales--i have a certain name, you know--my 
inspiration has dry up, and much of what i write i have to burn--yes, 
doctor, to burn, before any one see it.” 

“as utterly alien to your own mind and personality?” 

“utterly! as though some one else had written it----” 

“ah!” 

“and shocking!” he pass his hand over his eyes a moment and let the 
breath escape soft through his teeth. “yet most damnable clever in 
the consummate way the vile suggestion are insinuate under cover of 
a kind of high drollery. my stenographer left me, of course--and i’ve 
been afraid to take another----” 

john silence got up and began to walk about the room leisurely without 
speaking; he appear to be examine the pictures on the wall and 
reading the name of the books lie about. presently he pause on the 
hearthrug, with his back to the fire, and turn to look his patient 
quiet in the eyes. pender’s face was grey and drawn; the hunt 
expression dominate it; the long recital had told upon him. 

“thank you, mr. pender,” he said, a curious glow showing about his 
fine, quiet face, “thank you for the sincerity and frank of your 
account. but i think now there is nothing further i need ask you.” he 
indulge in a long scrutiny of the author’s haggard features, drawing 
purposely the man’s eyes to his own and then meeting them with a look 
of power and confidence calculate to inspire even the feeblest soul 
with courage. “and, to begin with,” he added, smile pleasantly, “let 
me assure you without delay that you need have no alarm, for you are no 
more insane or delude than i myself am----” 

pender heave a deep sigh and try to return the smile. 

“----and this is simply a case, so far as i can judge at present, of a 
very singular psychic invasion, and a very sinister one, too, if you 
perhaps understand what i mean----” 

“it’s an odd expression; you use it before, you know,” said the 
author wearily, yet eager listen to every word of the diagnosis, 
and deep touched by the intelligent sympathy which did not at once 
indicate the lunatic asylum. 

“possibly,” return the other, “and an odd affliction too, you’ll 
allow, yet one not unknown to the nation of antiquity, nor to those 
moderns, perhaps, who recognise the freedom of action under certain 
pathogenic conditions between this world and another.” 

“and you think,” ask pender hastily, “that it is all primarily due to 
the _cannabis_? there is nothing radical amiss with myself--nothing 
incurable, or----?” 

“due entire to the overdose,” dr. silence reply emphatically, “to 
the drug’s direct action upon your psychic being. it render you 
ultra-sensitive and made you respond to an increase rate of vibration. 
and, let me tell you, mr. pender, that your experiment might have had 
result far more dire. it has brought you into touch with a somewhat 
singular class of invisible, but of one, i think, chiefly human in 
character. you might, however, just as easily have been drawn out of 
human range altogether, and the result of such a contingency would 
have been exceedingly terrible. indeed, you would not now be here to 
tell the tale. i need not alarm you on that score, but mention it as 
a warning you will not misunderstand or underrate after what you have 
been through. 

“you look puzzled. you do not quite gather what i am driving at; and 
it is not to be expect that you should, for you, i suppose, are 
the nominal christian with the nominal christian’s lofty standard of 
ethics, and his utter ignorance of spiritual possibilities. beyond 
a somewhat childish understanding of ‘spiritual wicked in high 
places,’ you probably have no conception of what is possible once you 
break down the slender gulf that is merciful fixed between you and 
that outer world. but my study and training have taken me far outside 
these orthodox trips, and i have made experiment that i could scarcely 
speak to you about in language that would be intelligible to you.” 

he pause a moment to note the breathless interest of pender’s face and 
manner. every word he utter was calculated; he knew exactly the value 
and effect of the emotion he desire to waken in the heart of the 
afflict being before him. 

“and from certain knowledge i have gain through various experiences,” 
he continue calmly, “i can diagnose your case as i said before to be 
one of psychic invasion.” 

“and the nature of this--er--invasion?” stammer the bewilder writer 
of humorous tales. 

“there is no reason why i should not say at once that i do not yet 
quite know,” reply dr. silence. “i may first have to make one or two 
experiments----” 

“on me?” gasp pender, catching his breath. 

“not exactly,” the doctor said, with a grave smile, “but with your 
assistance, perhaps. i shall want to test the conditions of the 
house--to ascertain, if possible, the character of the forces, of this 
strange personality that has been haunting you----” 

“at present you have no idea exactly who--what--why----” ask the 
other in a wild flurry of interest, dread and amazement. 

“i have a very good idea, but no proof rather,” return the doctor. 
“the effects of the drug in alter the scale of time and space, and 
merge the senses have nothing primarily to do with the invasion. they 
come to any one who is fool enough to take an experimental dose. it is 
the other features of your case that are unusual. you see, you are now 
in touch with certain violent emotions, desires, purposes, still active 
in this house, that were produce in the past by some powerful and evil 
personality that live here. how long ago, or why they still persist 
so forcibly, i cannot positively say. but i should judge that they are 
merely forces acting automatic with the momentum of their terrific 
original impetus.” 

“not direct by a living being, a conscious will, you mean?” 

“possibly not--but none the less dangerous on that account, and more 
difficult to deal with. i cannot explain to you in a few minutes the 
nature of such things, for you have not made the study that would 
enable you to follow me; but i have reason to believe that on the 
dissolution at death of a human being, its forces may still persist 
and continue to act in a blind, unconscious fashion. as a rule they 
speedy dissipate themselves, but in the case of a very powerful 
personality they may last a long time. and, in some cases--of which i 
incline to think this is one--these forces may coalesce with certain 
non-human entity who thus continue their life indefinitely and 
increase their strength to an unbelievable degree. if the original 
personality was evil, the being attract to the left-over forces 
will also be evil. in this case, i think there has been an unusual and 
dreadful aggrandisement of the thought and purpose left behind long 
ago by a woman of consummate wicked and great personal power of 
character and intellect. now, do you begin to see what i am driving at 
a little?” 

pender stare fixedly at his companion, plain horror showing in his 
eyes. but he found nothing to say, and the doctor continued-- 

“in your case, predispose by the action of the drug, you have 
experienced the rush of these forces in undiluted strength. they 
wholly obliterate in you the sense of humour, fancy, imagination,--all 
that make for cheerful and hope. they seek, though perhaps 
automatic only, to oust your own thought and establish themselves 
in their place. you are the victim of a psychic invasion. at the same 
time, you have become clairvoyant in the true sense. you are also a 
clairvoyant victim.” 

pender mop his face and sighed. he left his chair and went over to 
the fireplace to warm himself. 

“you must think me a quack to talk like this, or a madman,” laugh 
dr. silence. “but never mind that. i have come to help you, and i can 
help you if you will do what i tell you. it is very simple: you must 
leave this house at once. oh, never mind the difficulties; we will deal 
with those together. i can place another house at your disposal, or 
i would take the lease here off your hands, and later have it pull 
down. your case interests me greatly, and i mean to see you through, so 
that you have no anxiety, and can drop back into your old groove of 
work to-morrow! the drug has provided you, and therefore me, with a 
short-cut to a very interesting experience. i am grateful to you.” 

the author poke the fire vigorously, emotion rising in him like a 
tide. he glance towards the door nervously. 

“there is no need to alarm your wife or to tell her the detail of 
our conversation,” pursue the other quietly. “let her know that you 
will soon be in possession again of your sense of humour and your 
health, and explain that i am lend you another house for six months. 
meanwhile i may have the right to use this house for a night or two for 
my experiment. is that understood between us?” 

“i can only thank you from the bottom of my heart,” stammer pender, 
unable to find words to express his gratitude. 

then he hesitate for a moment, searching the doctor’s face anxiously. 

“and your experiment with the house?” he said at length. 

“of the simplest character, my dear mr. pender. although i am myself an 
artificial train psychic, and consequently aware of the presence 
of discarnate entity as a rule, i have so far felt nothing here at 
all. this make me sure that the forces acting here are of an unusual 
description. what i propose to do is to make an experiment with a view 
of drawing out this evil, coax it from its lair, so to speak, in 
order that it may _exhaust itself through me_ and become dissipated for 
ever. i have already been inoculated,” he added; “i consider myself to 
be immune.” 

“heavens above!” gasp the author, collapse on to a chair. 

“hell beneath! might be a more appropriate exclamation,” the doctor 
laughed. “but, seriously, mr. pender, this is what i propose to 
do--with your permission.” 

“of course, of course,” cry the other, “you have my permission and my 
best wish for success. i can see no possible objection, but----” 

“but what?” 

“i pray to heaven you will not undertake this experiment alone, will 
you?” 

“oh dear, no; not alone.” 

“you will take a companion with good nerves, and reliable in case of 
disaster, won’t you?” 

“i shall bring two companions,” the doctor said. 

“ah, that’s better. i feel easier. i am sure you must have among your 
acquaintance men who----” 

“i shall not think of bring men, mr. pender.” 

the other look up sharply. 

“no, or women either; or children.” 

“i don’t understand. who will you bring, then?” 

“animals,” explain the doctor, unable to prevent a smile at his 
companion’s expression of surprise--“two animals, a cat and a dog.” 

pender stare as if his eyes would drop out upon the floor, and then 
led the way without another word into the adjoin room where his wife 
was await them for tea. 


ii 

a few days later the humorist and his wife, with mind greatly 
relieved, move into a small furnish house placed at their free 
disposal in another part of london; and john silence, intent upon his 
approach experiment, made ready to spend a night in the empty house 
on the top of putney hill. only two rooms were prepared for occupation: 
the study on the ground floor and the bedroom immediately above it; 
all other door were to be locked, and no servant was to be left in 
the house. the motor had orders to call for him at nine o’clock the 
following morning. 

and, meanwhile, his secretary had instruction to look up the past 
history and association of the place, and learn everything he could 
concerning the character of former occupants, recent or remote. 

the animals, by whose sensitive he intended to test any unusual 
conditions in the atmosphere of the building, dr. silence select 
with care and judgment. he believe (and had already made curious 
experiment to prove it) that animal were more often, and more truly, 
clairvoyant than human beings. many of them, he felt convinced, 
possessed powers of perception far superior to that mere keen of 
the senses common to all dwell in the wild where the senses grow 
specially alert; they had what he term “animal clairvoyance,” and 
from his experiment with horses, dogs, cats, and even birds, he had 
drawn certain deductions, which, however, need not be refer to in 
detail here. 

cats, in particular, he believed, were almost continuous conscious of 
a large field of vision, too detailed even for a photographic camera, 
and quite beyond the reach of normal human organs. he had, further, 
observe that while dogs were usually terrify in the presence of such 
phenomena, cat on the other hand were soothe and satisfied. they 
welcome manifestation as something belong peculiarly to their own 
region. 

he select his animals, therefore, with wisdom so that they might 
afford a differ test, each in its own way, and that one should not 
merely communicate its own excitement to the other. he took a dog and a 
cat. 

the cat he chose, now full grown, had live with him since kittenhood, 
a kittenhood of perplex sweet and audacious mischief. wayward 
it was and fanciful, ever play its own mysterious games in the 
corner of the room, jump at invisible nothings, leap sideways 
into the air and fall with tiny mocassine feet on to another part 
of the carpet, yet with an air of dignified earnest which show 
that the performance was necessary to its own well-being, and not done 
merely to impress a stupid human audience. in the middle of elaborate 
washing it would look up, startled, as though to stare at the approach 
of some invisible, cock its little head sideways and putt out a 
velvet pad to inspect cautiously. then it would get absent-minded, and 
stare with equal intent in another direction (just to confuse the 
onlookers), and sudden go on furiously washing its body again, but in 
quite a new place. except for a white patch on its breast it was coal 
black. and its name was--smoke. 

“smoke” describe its temperament as well as its appearance. its 
movements, its individuality, its pose as a little furry mass of 
conceal mysteries, its elfin-like elusiveness, all combine to 
justify its name; and a subtle painter might have picture it as a 
wisp of floating smoke, the fire below betray itself at two points 
only--the glowing eyes. 

all its forces ran to intelligence--secret intelligence, the wordless, 
incalculable intuition of the cat. it was, indeed, _the_ cat for the 
business in hand. 

the selection of the dog was not so simple, for the doctor own many; 
but after much deliberation he chose a collie, call flame from his 
yellow coat. true, it was a trifle old, and stiff in the joints, and 
even beginning to grow deaf, but, on the other hand, it was a very 
particular friend of smoke’s, and had father it from kittenhood 
upwards so that a subtle understanding exist between them. it was 
this that turn the balance in its favour, this and its courage. 
moreover, though good-tempered, it was a terrible fighter, and its 
anger when provoke by a righteous cause was a fury of fire, and 
irresistible. 

it had come to him quite young, straight from the shepherd, with the 
air of the hill yet in its nostrils, and was then little more than 
skin and bone and teeth. for a collie it was sturdy built, its nose 
blunt than most, its yellow hair stiff rather than silky, and it had 
full eyes, unlike the slit eyes of its breed. only its master could 
touch it, for it ignore strangers, and despise their pattings--when 
any dare to pat it. there was something patriarchal about the old 
beast. he was in earnest, and went through life with tremendous 
energy and big things in view, as though he had the reputation of his 
whole race to uphold. and to watch him fight against odds was to 
understand why he was terrible. 

in his relations with smoke he was always absurd gentle; also he 
was fatherly; and at the same time betray a certain diffidence or 
shyness. he recognise that smoke call for strong yet respectful 
management. the cat’s circuitous method puzzle him, and his elaborate 
pretence perhaps shock the dog’s liking for direct, undisguised 
action. yet, while he fail to comprehend these tortuous feline 
mysteries, he was never contemptuous or condescending; and he preside 
over the safety of his furry black friend somewhat as a father, 
loving but intuitive, might superintend the vagary of a wayward and 
talented child. and, in return, smoke reward him with exhibition of 
fascinating and audacious mischief. 

and these brief description of their character are necessary for the 
proper understanding of what subsequent took place. 

with smoke sleep in the fold of his fur coat, and the collie lie 
watchful on the seat opposite, john silence went down in his motor 
after dinner on the night of november 15th. 

and the fog was so dense that they were oblige to travel at quarter 
speed the entire way. 

* * * * * 

it was after ten o’clock when he dismiss the motor and enter the 
dingy little house with the latchkey provided by pender. he found the 
hall gas turn low, and a fire in the study. books and food had also 
been placed ready by the servant according to instructions. coil of 
fog rush in after him through the open door and fill the hall and 
passage with its cold discomfort. 

the first thing dr. silence did was to lock up smoke in the study with 
a saucer of milk before the fire, and then make a search of the house 
with flame. the dog ran cheerful behind him all the way while he 
try the door of the other rooms to make sure they were locked. he 
nose about into corner and made little excursion on his own account. 
his manner was expectant. he knew there must be something unusual about 
the proceeding, because it was contrary to the habit of his whole 
life not to be asleep at this hour on the mat in front of the fire. he 
kept look up into his master’s face, as door after door was tried, 
with an expression of intelligent sympathy, but at the same time a 
certain air of disapproval. yet everything his master did was good in 
his eyes, and he betray as little impatience as possible with all 
this unnecessary journey to and fro. if the doctor was pleased to 
play this sort of game at such an hour of the night, it was surely not 
for him to object. so he play it too; and was very busy and earnest 
about it into the bargain. 

after an uneventful search they came down again to the study, and here 
dr. silence discover smoke washing his face calm in front of the 
fire. the saucer of milk was lick dry and clean; the preliminary 
examination that cat always make in new surroundings had evidently 
been satisfactory concluded. he drew an arm-chair up to the fire, 
stir the coal into a blaze, arrange the table and lamp to his 
satisfaction for reading, and then prepared surreptitious to watch 
the animals. he wish to observe them careful without their being 
aware of it. 

now, in spite of their respective ages, it was the regular custom of 
these two to play together every night before sleep. smoke always made 
the advances, beginning with grave impudence to pat the dog’s tail, and 
flame play cumbrously, with condescension. it was his duty, rather 
than pleasure; he was glad when it was over, and sometimes he was very 
determined and refuse to play at all. 

and this night was one of the occasion on which he was firm. 

the doctor, look cautious over the top of his book, watch 
the cat begin the performance. it start by gaze with an innocent 
expression at the dog where he lay with nose on paw and eyes wide open 
in the middle of the floor. then it got up and made as though it meant 
to walk to the door, go deliberate and very softly. flame’s eyes 
follow it until it was beyond the range of sight, and then the cat 
turn sharp and began pat his tail tentative with one paw. 
the tail move slightly in reply, and smoke change paw and tap 
it again. the dog, however, did not rise to play as was his wont, and 
the cat fell to pat it brisk with both paws. flame still lay 
motionless. 

this puzzle and bore the cat, and it went round and stare hard into 
its friend’s face to see what was the matter. perhaps some inarticulate 
message flash from the dog’s eyes into its own little brain, making 
it understand that the programme for the night had better not begin 
with play. perhaps it only realise that its friend was immovable. but, 
whatever the reason, its usual persistence thenceforward desert it, 
and it made no further attempt at persuasion. smoke yield at once to 
the dog’s mood; it sat down where it was and began to wash. 

but the washing, the doctor noted, was by no means its real purpose; 
it only use it to mask something else; it stop at the most busy 
and furious moments and began to stare about the room. its thought 
wander absurdly. it peer intent at the curtains; at the shadowy 
corners; at empty space above; leave its body in curious awkward 
position for whole minutes together. then it turn sharp and stare 
with a sudden signal of intelligence at the dog, and flame at once 
rose somewhat stiff to his feet and began to wander aimless and 
restless to and fro about the floor. smoke follow him, padding 
quiet at his heels. between them they made what seem to be a 
deliberate search of the room. 

and, here, as he watch them, note careful every detail of 
the performance over the top of his book, yet making no effort to 
interfere, it seem to the doctor that the first beginning of a 
faint distress betray themselves in the collie, and in the cat the 
stirring of a vague excitement. 

he observe them closely. the fog was thick in the air, and the 
tobacco smoke from his pipe add to its density; the furniture at the 
far end stood mistily, and where the shadow congregate in hanging 
cloud under the ceiling, it was difficult to see clearly at all; the 
lamplight only reach to a level of five feet from the floor, above 
which came layer of comparative darkness, so that the room appear 
twice as lofty as it actually was. by means of the lamp and the fire, 
however, the carpet was everywhere clearly visible. 

the animal made their silent tour of the floor, sometimes the dog 
leading, sometimes the cat; occasional they look at one another 
as though exchange signals; and once or twice, in spite of the 
limited space, he lost sight of one or other among the fog and the 
shadows. their curiosity, it appear to him, was something more than 
the excitement lurk in the unknown territory of a strange room; 
yet, so far, it was impossible to test this, and he purposely kept his 
mind quiet receptive lest the smallest mental excitement on his part 
should communicate itself to the animal and thus destroy the value of 
their independent behaviour. 

they made a very thorough journey, leave no piece of furniture 
unexamined, or unsmelt. flame led the way, walking slow with lower 
head, and smoke follow demure at his heels, making a transparent 
pretence of not being interested, yet missing nothing. and, at length, 
they returned, the old collie first, and came to rest on the mat 
before the fire. flame rest his muzzle on his master’s knee, smile 
beatific while he pat the yellow head and spoke his name; and 
smoke, coming a little later, pretend he came by chance, look from 
the empty saucer to his face, lap up the milk when it was given him 
to the last drop, and then sprang upon his knee and curl round for 
the sleep it had fully earn and intended to enjoy. 

silence descended upon the room. only the breathing of the dog upon the 
mat came through the deep stillness, like the pulse of time marking 
the minutes; and the steady drip, drip of the fog outside upon the 
window-ledges dismal testify to the inclement of the night beyond. 
and the soft crashing of the coal as the fire settled down into the 
grate became less and less audible as the fire sank and the flame 
resigned their fierceness. 

it was now well after eleven o’clock, and dr. silence devoted himself 
again to his book. he read the words on the print page and took 
in their meaning superficially, yet without start into life 
the correlation of thought and suggestion that should accompany 
interesting reading. underneath, all the while, his mental energy 
were absorb in watching, listening, wait for what might come. 
he was not over sanguine himself, yet he did not wish to be taken 
by surprise. moreover, the animals, his sensitive barometers, had 
incontinent gone to sleep. 

after reading a dozen pages, however, he realise that his mind was 
really occupy in review the features of pender’s extraordinary 
story, and that it was no long necessary to steady his imagination 
by study the dull paragraph detailed in the page before him. he 
laid down his book accordingly, and allow his thought to dwell upon 
the features of the case. speculation as to the meaning, however, he 
rigorous suppressed, knowing that such thought would act upon his 
imagination like wind upon the glowing ember of a fire. 

as the night wore on the silence grew deep and deeper, and only 
at rare interval he heard the sound of wheels on the main road a 
hundred yard away, where the horse went at a walking pace owe to 
the density of the fog. the echo of pedestrian footstep no long 
reach him, the clamour of occasional voice no long came down the 
side street the night, muffle by fog, shroud by veil of ultimate 
mystery, hung about the haunt villa like a doom. nothing in the house 
stirred. stillness, in a thick blanket, lay over the upper storeys. 
only the mist in the room grew more dense, he thought, and the damp 
cold more penetrating. certainly, from time to time, he shivered. 

the collie, now deep in slumber, move occasionally,--grunted, sighed, 
or twitch his leg in dreams. smoke lay on his knees, a pool of warm, 
black fur, only the closest observation detect the movement of his 
sleek sides. it was difficult to distinguish exactly where his head and 
body join in that circle of glisten hair; only a black satin nose 
and a tiny tip of pink tongue betray the secret. 

dr. silence watch him, and felt comfortable. the collie’s breathing 
was soothing. the fire was well built, and would burn for another two 
hour without attention. he was not conscious of the least nervousness. 
he particularly wish to remain in his ordinary and normal state of 
mind, and to force nothing. if sleep came naturally, he would let it 
come--and even welcome it. the cold of the room, when the fire 
die down later, would be sure to wake him again; and it would then be 
time enough to carry these sleep barometer up to bed. from various 
psychic premonition he knew quite well that the night would not pass 
without adventure; but he did not wish to force its arrival; and he 
wish to remain normal, and let the animal remain normal, so that, 
when it came, it would be unattended by excitement or by any strain 
of the attention. many experiment had made him wise. and, for the 
rest, he had no fear. 

accordingly, after a time, he did fall asleep as he had expected, and 
the last thing he remembered, before oblivion slip up over his eyes 
like soft wool, was the picture of flame stretch all four leg at 
once, and sigh noisy as he sought a more comfortable position for 
his paw and muzzle upon the mat. 

* * * * * 

it was a good deal later when he became aware that a weight lay upon 
his chest, and that something was pencil over his face and mouth. a 
soft touch on the cheek woke him. something was pat him. 

he sat up with a jerk, and found himself staring straight into a pair 
of brilliant eyes, half green, half black. smoke’s face lay level with 
his own; and the cat had climb up with its front paw upon his chest. 

the lamp had burn low and the fire was nearly out, yet dr. silence 
saw in a moment that the cat was in an excited state. it knead with 
its front paw into his chest, shift from one to the other. he felt 
them prod against him. it lift a leg very careful and pat 
his cheek gingerly. its fur, he saw, was standing ridgewise upon its 
back; the ear were flatten back somewhat; the tail was switch 
sharply. the cat, of course, had waken him with a purpose, and the 
instant he realise this, he set it upon the arm of the chair and 
sprang up with a quick turn to face the empty room behind him. by some 
curious instinct, his arms of their own accord assume an attitude 
of defence in front of him, as though to ward off something that 
threaten his safety. yet nothing was visible. only shape of fog hung 
about rather heavy in the air, moving slightly to and fro. 

his mind was now fully alert, and the last vestige of sleep gone. he 
turn the lamp higher and peer about him. two things he became aware 
of at once: one, that smoke, while excited, was _pleasurably_ excited; 
the other, that the collie was no long visible upon the mat at his 
feet. he had crept away to the corner of the wall farthest from the 
window, and lay watch the room with wide-open eyes, in which lurk 
plainly something of alarm. 

something in the dog’s behaviour instantly struck dr. silence as 
unusual, and, calling him by name, he move across to pat him. flame 
got up, wag his tail, and came over slow to the rug, utter a 
low sound that was half growl, half whine. he was evidently perturb 
about something, and his master was proceeding to administer comfort 
when his attention was sudden drawn to the antics of his other 
four-footed companion, the cat. 

and what he saw fill him with something like amazement. 

smoke had jump down from the back of the arm-chair and now occupy 
the middle of the carpet, where, with tail erect and leg stiff as 
ramrods, it was steady pace backwards and forward in a narrow 
space, uttering, as it did so, those curious little guttural sound of 
pleasure that only an animal of the feline species know how to make 
expressive of supreme happiness. its stiffen leg and arch back 
made it appear large than usual, and the black visage wore a smile of 
beatific joy. its eyes blaze magnificently; it was in an ecstasy. 

at the end of every few pace it turn sharp and stalk back again 
along the same line, padding softly, and purr like a roll of little 
muffle drums. it behave precisely as though it were rubbing against 
the ankle of some one who remain invisible. a thrill ran down the 
doctor’s spine as he stood and stared. his experiment was grow 
interesting at last. 

he call the collie’s attention to his friend’s performance to see 
whether he too was aware of anything standing there upon the carpet; 
and the dog’s behaviour was significant and corroborative. he came 
as far as his master’s knee and then stop dead, refuse to 
investigate closely. in vain dr. silence urge him; he wag his 
tail, whine a little, and stood in a half-crouching attitude, staring 
alternate at the cat and at his master’s face. he was, apparently, 
both puzzle and alarmed, and the whine went deep and deep down 
into his throat till it change into an ugly snarl of awakening anger. 

then the doctor call to him in a tone of command he had never known 
to be disregarded; but still the dog, though spring up in response, 
decline to move nearer. he made tentative motions, prance a little 
like a dog about to take to water, pretended to bark, and ran to and 
fro on the carpet. so far there was no actual fear in his manner, but 
he was uneasy and anxious, and nothing would induce him to go within 
touching distance of the walking cat. once he made a complete circuit, 
but always careful out of reach; and in the end he return to his 
master’s leg and rub vigorous against him. flame did not like the 
performance at all: that much was quite clear. 

for several minutes john silence watch the performance of the cat 
with profound attention and without interfering. then he call to the 
animal by name. 

“smoke, you mysterious beastie, what in the world are you about?” he 
said, in a coax tone. 

the cat look up at him for a moment, smile in its ecstasy, blinking 
its eyes, but too happy to pause. he spoke to it again. he call to it 
several times, and each time it turn upon him its blazing eyes, drunk 
with inner delight, opening and shut its lips, its body large and 
rigid with excitement. yet it never for one instant pause in its short 
journey to and fro. 

he noted exactly what it did: it walked, he saw, the same number of 
pace each time, some six or seven steps, and then it turn sharp 
and retrace them. by the pattern of the great rose in the carpet 
he measured it. it kept to the same direction and the same line. it 
behave precisely as though it were rubbing against something solid. 
undoubtedly, there was something standing there on that strip of 
carpet, something invisible to the doctor, something that alarm the 
dog, yet cause the cat unspeakable pleasure. 

“smokie!” he call again, “smokie, you black mystery, what is it 
excite you so?” 

again the cat look up at him for a brief second, and then continue 
its sentry-walk, blissful happy, intense preoccupied. and, for an 
instant, as he watch it, the doctor was aware that a faint uneasy 
stir in the depths of his own being, focus itself for the moment 
upon this curious behaviour of the uncanny creature before him. 

there rose in him quite a new realisation of the mystery connected 
with the whole feline tribe, but especially with that common member 
of it, the domestic cat--their hidden lives, their strange aloofness, 
their incalculable subtlety. how utterly remote from anything that 
human being understood lay the source of their elusive activities. 
as he watch the indescribable bearing of the little creature mince 
along the strip of carpet under his eyes, coquette with the powers 
of darkness, welcoming, maybe, some fearsome visitor, there stir in 
his heart a feeling strange akin to awe. its indifference to human 
kind, its serene superior to the obvious, struck him forcibly with 
fresh meaning; so remote, so inaccessible seem the secret purpose of 
its real life, so alien to the blunder honesty of other animals. 
its absolute poise of bearing brought into his mind the opium-eater’s 
words that “no dignity is perfect which do not at some point ally 
itself with the mysterious”; and he became sudden aware that the 
presence of the dog in this foggy, haunt room on the top of putney 
hill was uncommonly welcome to him. he was glad to feel that flame’s 
dependable personality was with him. the savage growl at his heel 
was a pleasant sound. he was glad to hear it. that march cat made 
him uneasy. 

finding that smoke paid no further attention to his words, the doctor 
decided upon action. would it rub against his leg, too? he would take 
it by surprise and see. 

he step quick forward and placed himself upon the exact strip of 
carpet where it walked. 

but no cat is ever taken by surprise! the moment he occupy the space 
of the intruder, setting his feet on the woven rose midway in the line 
of travel, smoke sudden stop purr and sat down. it lift up 
its face with the most innocent stare imaginable of its green eyes. he 
could have sworn it laughed. it was a perfect child again. in a single 
second it had resume its simple, domestic manner; and it gaze at him 
in such a way that he almost felt smoke was the normal being, and _his_ 
was the eccentric behaviour that was being watched. it was consummate, 
the manner in which it brought about this change so easily and so 
quickly. 

“superb little actor!” he laugh in spite of himself, and stoop to 
stroke the shining black back. but, in a flash, as he touched its fur, 
the cat turn and spat at him viciously, striking at his hand with 
one paw. then, with a hurried scut of feet, it shot like a shadow 
across the floor and a moment later was calm sitting over by the 
window-curtains washing its face as though nothing interested it in the 
whole world but the clean of its cheek and whiskers. 

john silence straighten himself up and drew a long breath. he 
realise that the performance was temporary at an end. the 
collie, meanwhile, who had watch the whole proceeding with marked 
disapproval, had now lain down again upon the mat by the fire, no 
long growling. it seem to the doctor just as though something that 
had enter the room while he slept, alarm the dog, yet bring 
happiness to the cat, had now gone out again, leave all as it was 
before. whatever it was that excited its blissful attention had 
retreat for the moment. 

he realise this intuitively. smoke evidently realise it, too, for 
presently he deign to march back to the fireplace and jump upon his 
master’s knees. dr. silence, patient and determined, settled down once
