more to his book. the animal soon slept; the fire blaze cheerfully; 
and the cold fog from outside pour into the room through every 
available chink and cranny. 

for a long time silence and peace reign in the room and dr. silence 
avail himself of the quiet to make careful note of what had 
happened. he enter for future use in other case an exhaustive 
analysis of what he had observed, especially with regard to the 
effect upon the two animals. it is impossible here, nor would it be 
intelligible to the reader unversed in the knowledge of the region 
known to a scientific train psychic like dr. silence, to 
detail these observations. but to him it was clear, up to a certain 
point--and for the rest he must still wait and watch. so far, at least, 
he realise that while he slept in the chair--that is, while his will 
was dormant--the room had suffer intrusion from what he recognise 
as an intense active force, and might later be forced to acknowledge 
as something more than merely a blind force, namely, a distinct 
personality. 

so far it had affected himself scarcely at all, but had act directly 
upon the simple organism of the animals. it stimulate keen the 
centre of the cat’s psychic being, induce a state of instant 
happiness (intensifying its consciousness probably in the same way 
a drug or stimulant intensify that of a human being); whereas it 
alarm the less sensitive dog, cause it to feel a vague apprehension 
and distress. 

his own sudden action and exhibition of energy had serve to disperse 
it temporarily, yet he felt convinced--the indication were not lacking 
even while he sat there making notes--that it still remain near to 
him, conditional if not spatially, and was, as it were, gathering 
force for a second attack. 

and, further, he intuitive understood that the relations between the 
two animal had undergone a subtle change: that the cat had become 
immeasurable superior, confident, sure of itself in its own peculiar 
region, whereas flame had been weaken by an attack he could not 
comprehend and knew not how to reply to. though not yet afraid, he was 
defiant--ready to act against a fear that he felt to be approaching. he 
was no long fatherly and protective towards the cat. smoke held the 
key to the situation; and both he and the cat knew it. 

thus, as the minutes passed, john silence sat and waited, keen on 
the alert, wonder how soon the attack would be renewed, and at what 
point it would be divert from the animal and direct upon himself. 

the book lay on the floor beside him, his note were complete. with one 
hand on the cat’s fur, and the dog’s front paw rest against his 
feet, the three of them doze comfortable before the hot fire while the 
night wore on and the silence deepen towards midnight. 

it was well after one o’clock in the morning when dr. silence turn 
the lamp out and light the candle preparatory to go up to bed. 
then smoke sudden woke with a loud sharp purr and sat up. it neither 
stretched, wash nor turned: it listened. and the doctor, watch it, 
realise that a certain indefinable change had come about that very 
moment in the room. a swift readjust of the forces within the four 
wall had taken place--a new disposition of their personal equations. 
the balance was destroyed, the former harmony gone. smoke, most 
sensitive of barometers, had been the first to feel it, but the dog was 
not slow to follow suit, for on look down he noted that flame was no 
long asleep. he was lie with eyes wide open, and that same instant 
he sat up on his great haunch and began to growl. 

dr. silence was in the act of taking the match to re-light the lamp 
when an audible movement in the room behind made him pause. smoke 
leap down from his knee and move forward a few pace across the 
carpet. then it stop and stare fixedly; and the doctor stood up on 
the rug to watch. 

as he rose the sound was repeated, and he discover that it was not 
in the room as he first thought, but outside, and that it came from 
more directions than one. there was a rushing, sweeping noise against 
the window-panes, and simultaneous a sound of something brush 
against the door--out in the hall. smoke advanced sedate across the 
carpet, twitch his tail, and sat down within a foot of the door. the 
influence that had destroy the harmonious conditions of the room had 
apparently move in advance of its cause. clearly, something was about 
to happen. 

for the first time that night john silence hesitated; the thought of 
that dark narrow hall-way, choke with fog, and destitute of human 
comfort, was unpleasant. he became aware of a faint creep of his 
flesh. he knew, of course, that the actual opening of the door was not 
necessary to the invasion of the room that was about to take place, 
since neither door nor windows, nor any other solid barrier could 
interpose an obstacle to what was seek entrance. yet the opening of 
the door would be significant and symbolic, and he distinct shrank 
from it. 

but for a moment only. smoke, turning with a show of impatience, 
recall him to his purpose, and he move past the sitting, watch 
creature, and deliberate open the door to its full width. 

what subsequent happened, happen in the feeble and flicker light 
of the solitary candle on the mantelpiece. 

through the open door he saw the hall, dim lit and thick with fog. 
nothing, of course, was visible--nothing but the hat-stand, the africa 
spear in dark lines upon the wall and the high-backed wooden chair 
standing grotesque underneath on the oilcloth floor. for one instant 
the fog seem to move and thicken oddly; but he set that down to the 
score of the imagination. the door had open upon nothing. 

yet smoke apparently thought otherwise, and the deep growl of the 
collie from the mat at the back of the room seem to confirm his 
judgment. 

for, proud and self-possessed, the cat had again risen to his feet, 
and have advanced to the door, was now usher some one slow into 
the room. nothing could have been more evident. he pace from side to 
side, bowing his little head with great _empressement_ and holding his 
stiffen tail aloft like a flagstaff. he turn this way and that, 
mince to and fro, and showing sign of supreme satisfaction. he was 
in his element. he welcome the intrusion, and apparently reckon that 
his companions, the doctor and the dog, would welcome it likewise. 

the intruder had return for a second attack. 

dr. silence move slow backwards and took up his position on the 
hearthrug, key himself up to a condition of concentrated attention. 

he noted that flame stood beside him, facing the room, with body 
motionless, and head moving swift from side to side with a curious 
sway movement. his eyes were wide open, his back rigid, his neck and 
jaws thrust forward, his leg tense and ready to leap. savage, ready 
for attack or defence, yet dreadfully puzzle and perhaps already a 
little cowed, he stood and stared, the hair on his spine and side 
positively bristle outwards as though a wind play through them. in 
the dim firelight he look like a great yellow-haired wolf, silent, 
eyes shoot dark fire, exceedingly formidable. it was flame, the 
terrible. 

smoke, meanwhile, advanced from the door towards the middle of the 
room, adopt the very slow pace of an invisible companion. a few 
feet away it stop and began to smile and blink its eyes. there 
was something deliberate coax in its attitude as it stood there 
undecided on the carpet, clearly wish to effect some sort of 
introduction between the intruder and its canine friend and ally. 
it assume its most winning manners, purring, smiling, look 
persuasive from one to the other, and making quick tentative steps 
first in one direction and then in the other. there had always exist 
such perfect understanding between them in everything. surely flame 
would appreciate smoke’s intentions now, and acquiesce. 

but the old collie made no advances. he bare his teeth, lift 
his lip till the gum showed, and stood stockstill with fixed eyes 
and heave sides. the doctor move a little farther back, watch 
intent the smallest movement, and it was just then he divine 
sudden from the cat’s behaviour and attitude that it was not only a 
single companion it had usher into the room, but _several_. it kept 
crossing over from one to the other, look up at each in turn. it 
sought to win over the dog to friendly with them all. the original 
intruder had come back with reinforcements. and at the same time he 
further realise that the intruder was something more than a blind 
acting force, impersonal though destructive. it was a personality, and 
moreover a great personality. and it was accompany for the purpose 
of assistance by a host of other personalities, minor in degree, but 
similar in kind. 

he brace himself in the corner against the mantelpiece and waited, 
his whole being rouse to defence, for he was now fully aware that 
the attack had spread to include himself as well as the animals, 
and he must be on the alert. he strained his eyes through the foggy 
atmosphere, try in vain to see what the cat and dog saw; but the 
candlelight threw an uncertain and flicker light across the room and 
his eyes discern nothing. on the floor smoke move soft in front of 
him like a black shadow, his eyes gleam as he turn his head, still 
try with many insinuate gesture and much purr to bring about 
the introduction he desired. 

but it was all in vain. flame stood rivet to one spot, motionless as 
a figure carve in stone. 

some minutes passed, during which only the cat moved, and then there 
came a sharp change. flame began to back towards the wall. he move 
his head from side to side as he went, sometimes turning to snap at 
something almost behind him. _they_ were advance upon him, try to 
surround him. his distress became very marked from now onwards, and 
it seem to the doctor that his anger merge into genuine terror and 
became overwhelm by it. the savage growl sound perilous like a 
whine, and more than once he try to dive past his master’s legs, as 
though hunting for a way of escape. he was try to avoid something 
that everywhere block the way. 

this terror of the indomitable fighter impress the doctor enormously; 
yet also painfully; stirring his impatience; for he had never before 
seen the dog show sign of give in, and it distress him to witness 
it. he knew, however, that he was not give in easily, and understood 
that it was really impossible for him to gauge the animal’s sensation 
properly at all. what flame felt, and saw, must be terrible indeed to 
turn him all at once into a coward. he face something that made him 
afraid of more than his life merely. the doctor spoke a few quick words 
of encouragement to him, and stroke the bristle hair. but without 
much success. the collie seem already beyond the reach of comfort 
such as that, and the collapse of the old dog follow indeed very 
speedy after this. 

and smoke, meanwhile, remain behind, watch the advance, but not 
join in it; sitting, pleased and expectant, considering that all 
was go well and as it wished. it was knead on the carpet with 
its front paws--slowly, laboriously, as though its feet were dip in 
treacle. the sound its claw made as they caught in the thread was 
distinct audible. it was still smiling, blinking, purring. 

sudden the collie utter a poignant short bark and leap heavy to 
one side. his bare teeth trace a line of white through the gloom. 
the next instant he dashed past his master’s legs, almost upset his 
balance, and shot out into the room, where he went blunder wildly 
against wall and furniture. but that bark was significant; the doctor 
had heard it before and knew what it meant: for it was the cry of the 
fighter against odds and it meant that the old beast had found his 
courage again. possibly it was only the courage of despair, but at any 
rate the fight would be terrific. and dr. silence understood, too, 
that he dare not interfere. flame must fight his own enemy in his 
own way. 

but the cat, too, had heard that dreadful bark; and it, too, had 
understood. this was more than it had bargain for. across the dim 
shadow of that haunt room there must have pass some secret signal 
of distress between the animals. smoke stood up and look swift 
about him. he utter a piteous meow and trot smart away into the 
greater dark by the windows. what his object was only those endow 
with the spirit-like intelligence of cat might know. but, at any rate, 
he had at last range himself on the side of his friend. and the little 
beast meant business. 

at the same moment the collie manage to gain the door. the doctor saw 
him rush through into the hall like a flash of yellow light. he shot 
across the oilcloth, and tore up the stairs, but in another second he 
appear again, flying down the steps and landing at the bottom in a 
tumble heap, whining, cringing, terrified. the doctor saw him slink 
back into the room again and crawl round by the wall towards the cat. 
was, then, even the staircase occupied? did _they_ stand also in the 
hall? was the whole house crowded from floor to ceiling? 

the thought came to add to the keen distress he felt at the sight of 
the collie’s discomfiture. and, indeed, his own personal distress had 
increase in a marked degree during the past minutes, and continue 
to increase steady to the climax. he recognise that the drain on 
his own vitality grew steadily, and that the attack was now direct 
against himself even more than against the defeat dog, and the too 
much deceive cat. 

it all seem so rapid and uncalculated after that--the event that 
took place in this little modern room at the top of putney hill between 
midnight and sunrise--that dr. silence was hardly able to follow and 
remember it all. it came about with such uncanny swift and terror; 
the light was so uncertain; the movements of the black cat so difficult 
to follow on the dark carpet, and the doctor himself so weary and taken 
by surprise--that he found it almost impossible to observe accurately, 
or to recall afterwards precisely what it was he had seen or in what 
order the incident had taken place. he never could understand what 
defect of vision on his part made it seem as though the cat had 
duplicate itself at first, and then increase indefinitely, so that 
there were at least a dozen of them dart silent about the floor, 
leap soft on to chair and tables, passing like shadow from the 
open door to the end of the room, all black as sin, with brilliant 
green eyes flash fire in all directions. it was like the reflection 
from a score of mirror placed round the wall at different angles. 
nor could he make out at the time why the size of the room seem to 
have altered, grown much larger, and why it extend away behind him 
where ordinarily the wall should have been. the snarl of the enrage 
and terrify collie sound sometimes so far away; the ceiling seem 
to have raise itself so much higher than before, and much of the 
furniture had change in appearance and shift marvellously. 

it was all so confuse and confusing, as though the little room he knew 
had become merge and transform into the dimensions of quite another 
chamber, that came to him, with its host of cat and its strange 
distances, in a sort of vision. 

but these change came about a little later, and at a time when his 
attention was so concentrated upon the proceedings of smoke and the 
collie, that he only observe them, as it were, subconsciously. and the 
excitement, the flicker candlelight, the distress he felt for the 
collie, and the distort atmosphere of fog were the poorest possible 
ally to careful observation. 

at first he was only aware that the dog was repeating his short 
dangerous bark from time to time, snap vicious at the empty 
air, a foot or so from the ground. once, indeed, he sprang upwards 
and forwards, working furiously with teeth and paws, and with a noise 
like wolf fighting, but only to dash back the next minute against 
the wall behind him. then, after lie still for a bit, he rose to a 
crouch position as though to spring again, snarl horrible and 
making short half-circles with lower head. and smoke all the while 
meow piteous by the window as though try to draw the attack upon 
himself. 

then it was that the rush of the whole dreadful business seem to turn 
aside from the dog and direct itself upon his own person. the collie 
had made another spring and fallen back with a crash into the corner, 
where he made noise enough in his savage rage to waken the dead before 
he fell to whine and then finally lay still. and directly afterwards 
the doctor’s own distress became intolerable acute. he had made a half 
movement forward to come to the rescue when a veil that was dense 
than mere fog seem to drop down over the scene, drape room, walls, 
animal and fire in a mist of dark and fold also about his own 
mind. other form move silent across the field of vision, form 
that he recognise from previous experiments, and welcome not. unholy 
thought began to crowd into his brain, sinister suggestion of evil 
present themselves seductively. ice seem to settle about his heart, 
and his mind trembled. he began to lose memory--memory of his identity, 
of where he was, of what he ought to do. the very foundations of his 
strength were shaken. his will seem paralysed. 

and it was then that the room fill with this horde of cats, all dark 
as the night, all silent, all with lamp eyes of green fire. the 
dimensions of the place alter and shifted. he was in a much large 
space. the whine of the dog sound far away, and all about him the 
cat flew busy to and fro, silent play their tearing, rush 
game of evil, weave the pattern of their dark purpose upon the floor. 
he strove hard to collect himself and remember the words of power he 
had made use of before in similar dread position where his dangerous 
practice had sometimes led; but he could recall nothing consecutively; 
a mist lay over his mind and memory; he felt daze and his forces 
scattered. the deep within were too trouble for heal power to come 
out of them. 

it was glamour, of course, he realise afterwards, the strong glamour 
thrown upon his imagination by some powerful personality behind the 
veil; but at the time he was not sufficient aware of this and, as 
with all true glamour, was unable to grasp where the true end and 
the false began. he was caught momentarily in the same vortex that 
had sought to lure the cat to destruction through its delight, and 
threaten utterly to overwhelm the dog through its terror. 

there came a sound in the chimney behind him like wind boom and 
tear its way down. the window rattled. the candle flicker and 
went out. the glacial atmosphere closed round him with the cold of 
death, and a great rush sound swept by overhead as though the 
ceiling had lift to a great height. he heard the door shut. far 
away it sounded. he felt lost, shelterless in the depths of his soul. 
yet still he held out and resist while the climax of the fight came 
near and nearer.... he had step into the stream of forces awaken 
by pender and he knew that he must withstand them to the end or come to 
a conclusion that it was not good for a man to come to. something from 
the region of utter cold was upon him. 

and then quite suddenly, through the confuse mists about him, there 
slow rose up the personality that had been all the time direct 
the battle. some force enter his being that shook him as the 
tempest shakes a leaf, and close against his eyes--clean level with 
his face--he found himself staring into the wreck of a vast dark 
countenance, a countenance that was terrible even in its ruin. 

for ruin it was, and terrible it was, and the mark of spiritual 
evil was brand everywhere upon its broken features. eyes, face and 
hair rose level with his own, and for a space of time he never could 
properly measure, or determine, these two, a man and a woman, look 
straight into each other’s visage and down into each other’s hearts. 

and john silence, the soul with the good, unselfish motive, held his 
own against the dark discarnate woman whose motive was pure evil, and 
whose soul was on the side of the dark powers. 

it was the climax that touched the depth of power within him and began 
to restore him slow to his own. he was conscious, of course, of 
effort, and yet it seem no superhuman one, for he had recognise the 
character of his opponent’s power, and he call upon the good within 
him to meet and overcome it. the inner forces stir and tremble 
in response to his call. they did not at first come readily as was 
their habit, for under the spell of glamour they had already been 
diabolical lull into inactivity, but come they eventually did, 
rising out of the inner spiritual nature he had learned with so much 
time and pain to awaken to life. and power and confidence came with 
them. he began to breathe deep and regularly, and at the same time 
to absorb into himself the forces oppose to him, and to _turn them to 
his own account_. by cease to resist, and allow the deadly stream 
to pour into him unopposed, he use the very power supply by his 
adversary and thus enormously increase his own. 

for this spiritual alchemy he had learned. he understood that force 
ultimately is everywhere one and the same; it is the motive behind 
that make it good or evil; and his motive was entire unselfish. he 
knew--provided he was not first rob of self-control--how vicarious 
to absorb these evil radiation into himself and change them magical 
into his own good purposes. and, since his motive was pure and his soul 
fearless, they could not work him harm. 

thus he stood in the main stream of evil unwitting attract by 
pender, deflect its course upon himself; and after passing through 
the purify filter of his own unselfish these energy could only 
add to his store of experience, of knowledge, and therefore of power. 
and, as his self-control return to him, he gradual accomplished 
this purpose, even though tremble while he did so. 

yet the struggle was severe, and in spite of the freezing chill of the 
air, the perspiration pour down his face. then, by slow degrees, the 
dark and dreadful countenance faded, the glamour pass from his soul, 
the normal proportions return to wall and ceiling, the form melt 
back into the fog, and the whirl of rush shadow-cats disappear 
whence they came. 

and with the return of the consciousness of his own identity john 
silence was restore to the full control of his own will-power. in a 
deep, modulate voice he began to utter certain rhythmic sound that 
slow roll through the air like a rising sea, filling the room with 
powerful vibratory activity that whelme all irregularity of lesser 
vibration in its own swelling tone. he made certain sigils, gesture 
and movements at the same time. for several minutes he continue to 
utter these words, until at length the grow volume dominate the 
whole room and master the manifestation of all that oppose it. for 
just as he understood the spiritual alchemy that can transmute evil 
forces by raise them into higher channels, so he knew from long study 
the occult use of sound, and its direct effect upon the plastic region 
wherein the powers of spiritual evil work their fell purposes. harmony 
was restore first of all to his own soul, and thence to the room and 
all its occupants. 

and, after himself, the first to recognise it was the old dog lie 
in his corner. flame began sudden utter sound of pleasure, that 
“something” between a growl and a grunt that dogs make upon being 
restore to their master’s confidence. dr. silence heard the thumping 
of the collie’s tail against the ground. and the grunt and the thumping 
touched the depth of affection in the man’s heart, and gave him some 
inkling of what agony the dumb creature had suffered. 

next, from the shadow by the window, a somewhat shrill purr 
announce the restoration of the cat to its normal state. smoke was 
advance across the carpet. he seem very pleased with himself, and 
smile with an expression of supreme innocence. he was no shadow-cat, 
but real and full of his usual and perfect self-possession. he march 
along, pick his way delicately, but with a stately dignity that 
suggest his ancestry with the majesty of egypt. his eyes no long 
glared; they shone steady before him; they radiated, not excitement, 
but knowledge. clearly he was anxious to make amends for the mischief 
to which he had unwitting lent himself owe to his subtle and 
electric constitution. 

still utter his sharp high purr he march up to his master and 
rub vigorous against his legs. then he stood on his hind feet and 
paw his knee and stare beseeching up into his face. he turn his 
head towards the corner where the collie still lay, thumping his tail 
feeble and pathetically. 

john silence understood. he bent down and stroke the creature’s living 
fur, note the line of bright blue sparks that follow the motion of 
his hand down its back. and then they advanced together towards the 
corner where the dog was. 

smoke went first and put his nose gently against his friend’s muzzle, 
purr while he rubbed, and utter little soft sound of affection 
in his throat. the doctor lit the candle and brought it over. he 
saw the collie lie on its side against the wall; it was utterly 
exhausted, and foam still hung about its jaws. its tail and eyes 
respond to the sound of its name, but it was evidently very weak and 
overcome. smoke continue to rub against its cheek and nose and eyes, 
sometimes even standing on its body and knead into the thick yellow 
hair. flame reply from time to time by little lick of the tongue, 
most of them curious misdirected. 

but dr. silence felt intuitive that something disastrous had 
happened, and his heart was wrung. he stroke the dear body, feeling 
it over for bruise or broken bones, but finding none. he fed it with 
what remain of the sandwich and milk, but the creature clumsy 
upset the saucer and lost the sandwich between its paws, so that the 
doctor had to feed it with his own hand. and all the while smoke meow 
piteously. 

then john silence began to understand. he went across to the farther 
side of the room and call aloud to it. 

“flame, old man! come!” 

at any other time the dog would have been upon him in an instant, 
bark and leap to the shoulder. and even now he got up, though 
heavy and awkwardly, to his feet. he start to run, wag his 
tail more briskly. he collide first with a chair, and then ran 
straight into a table. smoke trot close at his side, try his very 
best to guide him. but it was useless. dr. silence had to lift him up 
into his own arms and carry him like a baby. for he was blind. 


iii 

it was a week later when john silence call to see the author in his 
new house, and found him well on the way to recovery and already busy 
again with his writing. the haunt look had left his eyes, and he 
seem cheerful and confident. 

“humour restored?” laugh the doctor, as soon as they were comfortable 
settled in the room overlook the park. 

“i’ve had no trouble since i left that dreadful place,” return pender 
gratefully; “and thanks to you----” 

the doctor stop him with a gesture. 

“never mind that,” he said, “we’ll discuss your new plan afterwards, 
and my scheme for relieve you of the house and helping you settle 
elsewhere. of course it must be pull down, for it’s not fit for any 
sensitive person to live in, and any other tenant might be afflict 
in the same way you were. although, personally, i think the evil has 
exhaust itself by now.” 

he told the astonish author something of his experience in it with 
the animals. 

“i don’t pretend to understand,” pender said, when the account was 
finished, “but i and my wife are intense relieved to be free of it 
all. only i must say i should like to know something of the former 
history of the house. when we took it six month ago i heard no word 
against it.” 

dr. silence drew a typewritten paper from his pocket. 

“i can satisfy your curiosity to some extent,” he said, running 
his eye over the sheets, and then replace them in his coat; “for 
by my secretary’s investigate i have been able to check certain 
information obtain in the hypnotic trance by a ‘sensitive’ who help 
me in such cases. the former occupant who haunt you appear to have 
been a woman of singularly atrocious life and character who finally 
suffer death by hanging, after a series of crime that appall the 
whole of england and only came to light by the merest chance. she came 
to her end in the year 1798, for it was not this particular house she 
live in, but a much large one that then stood upon the site it now 
occupies, and was then, of course, not in london, but in the country. 
she was a person of intellect, possessed of a powerful, train will, 
and of consummate audacity, and i am convinced avail herself of the 
resources of the lower magic to attain her ends. this go far to 
explain the virulence of the attack upon yourself, and why she is still 
able to carry on after death the evil practice that form her main 
purpose during life.” 

“you think that after death a soul can still conscious direct----” 
gasp the author. 

“i think, as i told you before, that the forces of a powerful 
personality may still persist after death in the line of their original 
momentum,” reply the doctor; “and that strong thought and purpose 
can still react upon suitable prepared brains long after their 
originate have pass away. 

“if you knew anything of magic,” he pursued, “you would know that 
thought is dynamic, and that it may call into existence form and 
pictures that may well exist for hundred of years. for, not far 
remove from the region of our human life, is another region where 
float the waste and drift of all the centuries, the limbo of the 
shell of the dead; a dense populate region cram with horror and 
abomination of all descriptions, and sometimes galvanise into active 
life again by the will of a train manipulator, a mind versed in the 
practice of lower magic. that this woman understood its vile commerce, 
i am persuaded, and the forces she set go during her life have 
simply been accumulate ever since, and would have continue to do so 
had they not been drawn down upon yourself, and afterwards discharge 
and satisfy through me. 

“anything might have brought down the attack, for, besides drugs, 
there are certain violent emotions, certain mood of the soul, certain 
spiritual fevers, if i may so call them, which directly open the inner 
being to a cognisance of this astral region i have mentioned. in your 
case it happen to be a peculiarly potent drug that did it. 

“but now, tell me,” he added, after a pause, hand to the perplexed 
author a pencil-drawing he had made of the dark countenance that had 
appear to him during the night on putney hill--“tell me if you 
recognise this face?” 

pender look at the drawing closely, greatly astonished. he shudder 
a little as he looked. 

“undoubtedly,” he said, “it is the face i kept try to draw--dark, 
with the great mouth and jaw, and the droop eye. that is the woman.” 

dr. silence then produce from his pocket-book an old-fashioned woodcut 
of the same person which his secretary had unearth from the record 
of the newgate calendar. the woodcut and the pencil drawing were two 
different aspect of the same dreadful visage. the men compare them 
for some moments in silence. 

“it make me thank god for the limitation of our senses,” said 
pender quietly, with a sigh; “continuous clairvoyance must be a sore 
affliction.” 

“it is indeed,” return john silence significantly, “and if all 
the people nowadays who claim to be clairvoyant were really so, the 
statistics of suicide and lunacy would be considerably higher than they 
are. it is little wonder,” he added, “that your sense of humour was 
clouded, with the mind-forces of that dead monster try to use your 
brain for their dissemination. you have had an interesting adventure, 
mr. felix pender, and, let me add, a fortunate escape.” 

the author was about to renew his thanks when there came a sound of 
scratch at the door, and the doctor sprang up quickly. 

“it’s time for me to go. i left my dog on the step, but i suppose----” 

before he had time to open the door, it had yield to the pressure 
behind it and flew wide open to admit a great yellow-haired collie. 
the dog, wag his tail and contort his whole body with delight, 
tore across the floor and try to leap up upon his owner’s breast. and 
there was laughter and happiness in the old eyes; for they were clear 
again as the day. 




case ii 

ancient sorcery 


i 

there are, it would appear, certain wholly unremarkable persons, with 
none of the characteristic that invite adventure, who yet once or 
twice in the course of their smooth lives undergo an experience so 
strange that the world catch its breath--and looks the other way! and 
it was case of this kind, perhaps, more than any other, that fell into 
the widespread net of john silence, the psychic doctor, and, appealing 
to his deep humanity, to his patience, and to his great quality of 
spiritual sympathy, led often to the revelation of problem of the 
strangest complexity, and of the profoundest possible human interest. 

matter that seem almost too curious and fantastic for belief he 
love to trace to their hidden sources. to unravel a tangle in the 
very soul of things--and to release a suffering human soul in the 
process--was with him a veritable passion. and the knot he untie 
were, indeed, often passing strange. 

the world, of course, ask for some plausible basis to which it can 
attach credence--something it can, at least, pretend to explain. 
the adventurous type it can understand: such people carry about 
with them an adequate explanation of their exciting lives, and their 
character obviously drive them into the circumstances which produce 
the adventures. it expect nothing else from them, and is satisfied. 
but dull, ordinary folk have no right to out-of-the-way experiences, 
and the world have been led to expect otherwise, is disappointed 
with them, not to say shocked. its complacent judgment has been rudely 
disturbed. 

“such a thing happen to _that_ man!” it cries--“a commonplace person 
like that! it is too absurd! there must be something wrong!” 

yet there could be no question that something did actually happen to 
little arthur vezin, something of the curious nature he describe to 
dr. silence. outwardly, or inwardly, it happen beyond a doubt, and in 
spite of the jeer of his few friends who heard the tale, and observe 
wise that “such a thing might perhaps have come to iszard, that 
crack-brained iszard, or to that odd fish minski, but it could never 
have happen to commonplace little vezin, who was fore-ordained to 
live and die according to scale.” 

but, whatever his method of death was, vezin certainly did not “live 
according to scale” so far as this particular event in his otherwise 
uneventful life was concerned; and to hear him recount it, and watch 
his pale delicate features change, and hear his voice grow soft 
and more hush as he proceeded, was to know the conviction that his 
halting words perhaps fail sometimes to convey. he live the thing 
over again each time he told it. his whole personality became muffle 
in the recital. it subdued him more than ever, so that the tale became 
a lengthy apology for an experience that he deprecated. he appear to 
excuse himself and ask your pardon for have dare to take part in so 
fantastic an episode. for little vezin was a timid, gentle, sensitive 
soul, rarely able to assert himself, tender to man and beast, and 
almost constitutionally unable to say no, or to claim many things that 
should rightly have been his. his whole scheme of life seem utterly 
remote from anything more exciting than missing a train or lose an 
umbrella on an omnibus. and when this curious event came upon him he 
was already more years beyond forty than his friends suspect or he 
care to admit. 

john silence, who heard him speak of his experience more than once, 
said that he sometimes left out certain detail and put in others; 
yet they were all obviously true. the whole scene was unforgettable 
cinematograph on to his mind. none of the detail were imagine 
or invented. and when he told the story with them all complete, the 
effect was undeniable. his appealing brown eyes shone, and much of the 
charming personality, usually so careful repressed, came forward and 
reveal itself. his modesty was always there, of course, but in the 
telling he forgot the present and allow himself to appear almost 
vivid as he live again in the past of his adventure. 

he was on the way home when it happened, crossing northern france from 
some mountain trip or other where he bury himself solitary-wise 
every summer. he had nothing but an unregistered bag in the rack, and 
the train was jam to suffocation, most of the passenger being 
unredeemed holiday english. he dislike them, not because they were 
his fellow-countrymen, but because they were noisy and obtrusive, 
obliterate with their big limb and tweed clothing all the quiet 
tint of the day that brought him satisfaction and enable him to melt 
into insignificance and forget that he was anybody. these english 
clash about him like a brass band, making him feel vague that he 
ought to be more self-assertive and obstreperous, and that he did not 
claim insistent enough all kind of things that he didn’t want and 
that were really valueless, such as corner seats, window up or down, 
and so forth. 

so that he felt uncomfortable in the train, and wish the journey were 
over and he was back again living with his unmarried sister in surbiton. 

and when the train stop for ten pant minutes at the little 
station in northern france, and he got out to stretch his leg on the 
platform, and saw to his dismay a further batch of the britain isle 
debouch from another train, it sudden seem impossible to him to 
continue the journey. even _his_ flabby soul revolted, and the idea of 
stay a night in the little town and go on next day by a slower, 
empty train, flash into his mind. the guard was already shouting 
“_en voiture_” and the corridor of his compartment was already packed 
when the thought came to him. and, for once, he act with decision and 
rush to snatch his bag. 

finding the corridor and steps impassable, he tap at the window 
(for he had a corner seat) and beg the frenchman who sat opposite 
to hand his luggage out to him, explain in his wretched france that 
he intended to break the journey there. and this elderly frenchman, he 
declared, gave him a look, half of warning, half of reproach, that to 
his die day he could never forget; hand the bag through the window 
of the moving train; and at the same time pour into his ear a long 
sentence, spoken rapid and low, of which he was able to comprehend 
only the last few words: “_à cause du sommeil et à cause des chats_.” 

in reply to dr. silence, whose singular psychic acute at once 
seize upon this frenchman as a vital point in the adventure, 
vezin admitted that the man had impress him favourable from the 
beginning, though without being able to explain why. they had sat 
facing one another during the four hour of the journey, and though 
no conversation had pass between them--vezin was timid about his 
stutter french--he confessed that his eyes were being continual 
drawn to his face, almost, he felt, to rudeness, and that each, by a 
dozen nameless little politenesse and attentions, had evince the 
desire to be kind. the men like each other and their personalities 
did not clash, or would not have clash had they chance to come to 
terms of acquaintance. the frenchman, indeed, seem to have exercise 
a silent protective influence over the insignificant little englishman, 
and without words or gesture betray that he wish him well and 
would gladly have been of service to him. 

“and this sentence that he hurl at you after the bag?” ask john 
silence, smile that peculiarly sympathetic smile that always melt 
the prejudice of his patient, “were you unable to follow it exactly?” 

“it was so quick and low and vehement,” explain vezin, in his small 
voice, “that i miss practically the whole of it. i only caught the 
few words at the very end, because he spoke them so clearly, and his 
face was bent down out of the carriage window so near to mine.” 

“_‘a cause du sommeil et à cause des chats’?_” repeated dr. silence, as 
though half speak to himself. 

“that’s it exactly,” said vezin; “which, i take it, means something 
like ‘because of sleep and because of the cats,’ doesn’t it?” 

“certainly, that’s how i should translate it,” the doctor observe 
shortly, evidently not wish to interrupt more than necessary. 

“and the rest of the sentence--all the first part i couldn’t 
understand, i mean--was a warning not to do something--not to stop in 
the town, or at some particular place in the town, perhaps. that was 
the impression it made on me.” 

then, of course, the train rush off, and left vezin standing on the 
platform alone and rather forlorn. 

the little town climb in straggle fashion up a sharp hill rising 
out of the plain at the back of the station, and was crown by the 
twin tower of the ruin cathedral peep over the summit. from the 
station itself it look uninterest and modern, but the fact was 
that the mediæval position lay out of sight just beyond the crest. and 
once he reach the top and enter the old streets, he step clean 
out of modern life into a bygone century. the noise and bustle of the 
crowded train seem days away. the spirit of this silent hill-town, 
remote from tourist and motor-cars, dream its own quiet life under 
the autumn sun, rose up and cast its spell upon him. long before he 
recognise this spell he act under it. he walk softly, almost on 
tiptoe, down the winding narrow street where the gable all but met 
over his head, and he enter the doorway of the solitary inn with a 
deprecate and modest demeanour that was in itself an apology for 
intrude upon the place and disturb its dream. 

at first, however, vezin said, he notice very little of all this. the 
attempt at analysis came much later. what struck him then was only the 
delightful contrast of the silence and peace after the dust and noisy 
rattle of the train. he felt soothe and stroke like a cat. 

“like a cat, you said?” interrupt john silence, quick catching him 
up. 

“yes. at the very start i felt that.” he laugh apologetically. “i 
felt as though the warmth and the still and the comfort made me 
purr. it seem to be the general mood of the whole place--then.” 

the inn, a rambling ancient house, the atmosphere of the old coach 
days still about it, apparently did not welcome him too warmly. he felt 
he was only tolerated, he said. but it was cheap and comfortable, and 
the delicious cup of afternoon tea he ordered at once made him feel 
really very pleased with himself for leave the train in this bold, 
original way. for to him it had seem bold and original. he felt 
something of a dog. his room, too, soothe him with its dark panelling 
and low irregular ceiling, and the long slope passage that led to it 
seem the natural pathway to a real chamber of sleep--a little dim 
cubby hole out of the world where noise could not enter. it look 
upon the courtyard at the back. it was all very charming, and made 
him think of himself as dress in very soft velvet somehow, and the 
floor seem padded, the wall provided with cushions. the sound of 
the street could not penetrate there. it was an atmosphere of absolute 
rest that surround him. 

on engaging the two-franc room he had interview the only person 
who seem to be about that sleepy afternoon, an elderly waiter with 
dundreary whiskers and a drowsy courtesy, who had amble lazy towards 
him across the stone yard; but on coming downstairs again for a little 
promenade in the town before dinner he encounter the proprietress 
herself. she was a large woman whose hands, feet, and features seem 
to swim towards him out of a sea of person. they emerged, so to speak. 
but she had great dark, vivacious eyes that counteract the bulk of 
her body, and betray the fact that in reality she was both vigorous 
and alert. when he first caught sight of her she was knitting in a low 
chair against the sunlight of the wall, and something at once made him 
see her as a great tabby cat, dozing, yet awake, heavy sleepy, and 
yet at the same time prepared for instantaneous action. a great mouser 
on the watch occur to him. 

she took him in with a single comprehensive glance that was polite 
without being cordial. her neck, he noticed, was extraordinarily supple 
in spite of its proportions, for it turn so easily to follow him, and 
the head it carry bowed so very flexibly. 

“but when she look at me, you know,” said vezin, with that little 
apologetic smile in his brown eyes, and that faint deprecate 
gesture of the shoulder that was characteristic of him, “the odd 
notion came to me that really she had intended to make quite a 
different movement, and that with a single bound she could have leap 
at me across the width of that stone yard and pounce upon me like some 
huge cat upon a mouse.” 

he laugh a little soft laugh, and dr. silence made a note in his book 
without interrupting, while vezin proceed in a tone as though he 
fear he had already told too much and more than we could believe. 

“very soft, yet very active she was, for all her size and mass, and i 
felt she knew what i was do even after i had pass and was behind 
her back. she spoke to me, and her voice was smooth and running. she 
ask if i had my luggage, and was comfortable in my room, and then 
add that dinner was at seven o’clock, and that they were very early 
people in this little country town. clearly, she intended to convey 
that late hour were not encouraged.” 

evidently, she contrived by voice and manner to give him the impression 
that here he would be “managed,” that everything would be arrange and 
plan for him, and that he had nothing to do but fall into the groove 
and obey. no decided action or sharp personal effort would be look 
for from him. it was the very reverse of the train. he walk quiet 
out into the street feeling soothe and peaceful. he realise that he 
was in a _milieu_ that suit him and stroke him the right way. it was 
so much easy to be obedient. he began to purr again, and to feel that 
all the town purr with him. 

about the street of that little town he meander gently, fall 
deep and deep into the spirit of repose that characterise it. with 
no special aim he wander up and down, and to and fro. the september 
sunshine fell slanting over the roofs. down winding alleyways, 
fringe with tumble gable and open casements, he caught fairylike 
glimpse of the great plain below, and of the meadow and yellow copse 
lie like a dream-map in the haze. the spell of the past held very 
potent here, he felt. 

the street were full of picturesque garb men and women, all busy 
enough, go their respective ways; but no one took any notice of him 
or turn to stare at his obviously english appearance. he was even 
able to forget that with his tourist appearance he was a false note in 
a charming picture, and he melt more and more into the scene, feeling 
delightful insignificant and unimportant and unselfconscious. it was 
like becoming part of a softly-coloured dream which he did not even 
realise to be a dream. 

on the eastern side the hill fell away more sharply, and the plain 
below ran off rather sudden into a sea of gathering shadow in which 
the little patch of woodland look like island and the stubble 
field like deep water. here he stroll along the old rampart of 
ancient fortification that once had been formidable, but now were 
only vision-like with their charming mingle of broken grey wall 
and wayward vine and ivy. from the broad coping on which he sat for a 
moment, level with the round tops of clip plane trees, he saw the 
esplanade far below lie in shadow. here and there a yellow sunbeam 
crept in and lay upon the fallen yellow leaves, and from the height 
he look down and saw that the townsfolk were walking to and fro in 
the cool of the evening. he could just hear the sound of their slow 
footfalls, and the murmur of their voice float up to him through the 
gap between the trees. the figures look like shadow as he caught 
glimpse of their quiet movements far below. 

he sat there for some time pondering, bath in the wave of murmur 
and half-lost echo that rose to his ears, muffle by the leaves of 
the plane trees. the whole town, and the little hill out of which it 
grew as naturally as an ancient wood, seem to him like a being lie 
there half asleep on the plain and croon to itself as it dozed. 

and, presently, as he sat lazy melting into its dream, a sound of 
horn and strings and wood instrument rose to his ears, and the town 
band began to play at the far end of the crowded terrace below to 
the accompaniment of a very soft, deep-throated drum. vezin was very 
sensitive to music, knew about it intelligently, and had even ventured, 
unknown to his friends, upon the composition of quiet melody with 
low-running chord which he play to himself with the soft pedal when 
no one was about. and this music floating up through the tree from an 
invisible and doubtless very picturesque band of the townspeople wholly 
charm him. he recognise nothing that they played, and it sound as 
though they were simply improvise without a conductor. no definitely 
marked time ran through the pieces, which end and began oddly after 
the fashion of wind through an Æolian harp. it was part of the place 
and scene, just as the die sunlight and faintly-breathing wind were 
part of the scene and hour, and the mellow note of old-fashioned 
plaintive horns, pierce here and there by the sharper strings, all 
half smother by the continuous boom of the deep drum, touched his 
soul with a curious potent spell that was almost too engrossing to be 
quite pleasant. 

there was a certain queer sense of bewitch in it all. the music 
seem to him oddly unartificial. it made him think of tree swept by 
the wind, of night breeze singing among wire and chimney-stacks, or 
in the rigging of invisible ships; or--and the simile leap up in his 
thought with a sudden sharp of suggestion--a chorus of animals, 
of wild creatures, somewhere in desolate place of the world, crying 
and singing as animal will, to the moon. he could fancy he heard the 
wailing, half-human cries of cat upon the tile at night, rising and 
fall with weird interval of sound, and this music, muffle by 
distance and the trees, made him think of a queer company of these 
creature on some roof far away in the sky, utter their solemn music 
to one another and the moon in chorus. 

it was, he felt at the time, a singular image to occur to him, yet it 
express his sensation pictorial better than anything else. the 
instrument play such impossible odd intervals, and the crescendo 
and diminuendo were so very suggestive of cat-land on the tile at 
night, rising swiftly, drop without warning to deep note again, 
and all in such strange confusion of discord and accords. but, at the 
same time a plaintive sweet result on the whole, and the discord 
of these half-broken instrument were so singular that they did not 
distress his musical soul like fiddle out of tune. 

he listen a long time, wholly surrender himself as his character 
was, and then stroll homewards in the dusk as the air grew chilly. 

“there was nothing to alarm?” put in dr. silence briefly. 

“absolutely nothing,” said vezin; “but you know it was all so 
fantastic and charming that my imagination was profound impressed. 
perhaps, too,” he continued, gently explanatory, “it was this stirring 
of my imagination that cause other impressions; for, as i walk back, 
the spell of the place began to steal over me in a dozen ways, though 
all intelligible ways. but there were other things i could not account 
for in the least, even then.” 

“incidents, you mean?” 

“hardly incidents, i think. a lot of vivid sensation crowded 
themselves upon my mind and i could trace them to no causes. it was 
just after sunset and the tumble old building trace magical outline 
against an opalescent sky of gold and red. the dusk was running down 
the twist streets. all round the hill the plain pressed in like a 
dim sea, its level rising with the darkness. the spell of this kind of 
scene, you know, can be very moving, and it was so that night. yet i 
felt that what came to me had nothing directly to do with the mystery 
and wonder of the scene.” 

“not merely the subtle transformation of the spirit that come with 
beauty,” put in the doctor, notice his hesitation. 

“exactly,” vezin went on, duly encourage and no long so fearful 
of our smile at his expense. “the impression came from somewhere 
else. for instance, down the busy main street where men and women were 
bustle home from work, shop at stalls and barrows, idle gossip 
in groups, and all the rest of it, i saw that i arouse no interest and 
that no one turn to stare at me as a foreigner and stranger. i was 
utterly ignored, and my presence among them excited no special interest 
or attention. 

“and then, quite suddenly, it dawn upon me with conviction that 
all the time this indifference and inattention were merely feigned. 
everybody as a matter of fact was watch me closely. every movement 
i made was known and observed. ignore me was all a pretence--an 
elaborate pretence.” 

he pause a moment and look at us to see if we were smiling, and then 
continued, reassured-- 

“it is useless to ask me how i notice this, because i simply cannot 
explain it. but the discovery gave me something of a shock. before i 
got back to the inn, however, another curious thing rose up strong in 
my mind and forced my recognition of it as true. and this, too, i may 
as well say at once, was equally inexplicable to me. i mean i can only 
give you the fact, as fact it was to me.” 

the little man left his chair and stood on the mat before the fire. 
his diffidence lessen from now onwards, as he lost himself again in 
the magic of the old adventure. his eyes shone a little already as he 
talked. 

“well,” he went on, his soft voice rising somewhat with his excitement, 
“i was in a shop when it came to me first--though the idea must have 
been at work for a long time subconscious to appear in so complete 
a form all at once. i was buy socks, i think,” he laughed, “and 
struggle with my dreadful french, when it struck me that the woman in 
the shop did not care two pin whether i bought anything or not. she 
was indifferent whether she made a sale or did not make a sale. she was 
only pretend to sell. 

“this sound a very small and fanciful incident to build upon what 
follows. but really it was not small. i mean it was the spark that lit 
the line of powder and ran along to the big blaze in my mind. 

“for the whole town, i sudden realised, was something other than i 
so far saw it. the real activity and interests of the people were 
elsewhere and otherwise than appeared. their true lives lay somewhere 
out of sight behind the scenes. their busy-ness was but the outward 
semblance that masked their actual purposes. they bought and sold, and 
ate and drank, and walk about the streets, yet all the while the main 
stream of their existence lay somewhere beyond my ken, underground, in 
secret places. in the shop and at the stalls they did not care whether 
i purchase their articles or not; at the inn, they were indifferent 
to my stay or going; their life lay remote from my own, spring 
from hidden, mysterious sources, coursing out of sight, unknown. it 
was all a great elaborate pretence, assume possibly for my benefit, 
or possibly for purpose of their own. but the main current of their 
energy ran elsewhere. i almost felt as an unwelcome foreign substance 
might be expect to feel when it has found its way into the human 
system and the whole body organise itself to eject it or to absorb it. 
the town was do this very thing to me. 

“this bizarre notion present itself forcibly to my mind as i walk 
home to the inn, and i began busy to wonder wherein the true life of 
this town could lie and what were the actual interests and activity 
of its hidden life. 

“and, now that my eyes were partly opened, i notice other things 
too that puzzle me, first of which, i think, was the extraordinary 
silence of the whole place. positively, the town was muffled. although 
the street were paved with cobble the people move about silently, 
softly, with pad feet, like cats. nothing made noise. all was 
hushed, subdued, muted. the very voice were quiet, low-pitched like 
purring. nothing clamorous, vehement or emphatic seem able to live 
in the drowsy atmosphere of soft dream that soothe this little 
hill-town into its sleep. it was like the woman at the inn--an outward 
repose screening intense inner activity and purpose. 

“yet there was no sign of lethargy or sluggish anywhere about it. 
the people were active and alert. only a magical and uncanny soft 
lay over them all like a spell.” 

vezin pass his hand across his eyes for a moment as though the memory 
had become very vivid. his voice had run off into a whisper so that 
we heard the last part with difficulty. he was telling a true thing 
obviously, yet something that he both like and hate telling. 

“i went back to the inn,” he continue presently in a loud voice, 
“and dined. i felt a new strange world about me. my old world of 
reality receded. here, whether i like it or no, was something new and 
incomprehensible. i regret have left the train so impulsively. 
an adventure was upon me, and i loathe adventure as foreign to my 
nature. moreover, this was the beginning apparently of an adventure 
somewhere deep within me, in a region i could not check or measure, 
and a feeling of alarm mingle itself with my wonder--alarm for the 
stability of what i had for forty years recognise as my ‘personality.’ 

“i went upstairs to bed, my mind teeming with thought that were 
unusual to me, and of rather a haunting description. by way of 
relief i kept thinking of that nice, prosaic noisy train and all 
those wholesome, bluster passengers. i almost wish i were with 
them again. but my dream took me elsewhere. i dream of cats, and 
soft-moving creatures, and the silence of life in a dim muffle world 
beyond the senses.” 


ii 

vezin stay on from day to day, indefinitely, much long than he 
had intended. he felt in a kind of dazed, somnolent condition. he did 
nothing in particular, but the place fascinate him and he could not 
decide to leave. decision were always very difficult for him and he 
sometimes wonder how he had ever brought himself to the point of 
leave the train. it seem as though some one else must have arrange 
it for him, and once or twice his thought ran to the swarthy frenchman 
who had sat opposite. if only he could have understood that long 
sentence ending so strange with “_à cause du sommeil et à cause des 
chats_.” he wonder what it all meant. 

meanwhile the hush soft of the town held him prisoner and he 
sought in his muddling, gentle way to find out where the mystery 
lay, and what it was all about. but his limited france and his 
constitutional hatred of active investigate made it hard for him to 
buttonhole anybody and ask questions. he was content to observe, and 
watch, and remain negative. 

the weather held on calm and hazy, and this just suit him. he 
wander about the town till he knew every street and alley. the 
people suffer him to come and go without let or hindrance, though 
it became clear to him every day that he was never free himself from 
observation. the town watch him as a cat watches a mouse. and he got 
no near to finding out what they were all so busy with or where the 
main stream of their activity lay. this remain hidden. the people 
were as soft and mysterious as cats. 

but that he was continual under observation became more evident from 
day to day. 

for instance, when he stroll to the end of the town and enter a 
little green public garden beneath the rampart and seat himself upon 
one of the empty bench in the sun, he was quite alone--at first. 
not another seat was occupied; the little park was empty, the path 
deserted. yet, within ten minutes of his coming, there must have been 
fully twenty person scattered about him, some strolling aimless 
along the gravel walks, staring at the flowers, and other seat on 
the wooden bench enjoy the sun like himself. none of them appear 
to take any notice of him; yet he understood quite well they had all 
come there to watch. they kept him under close observation. in the 
street they had seem busy enough, hurry upon various errands; yet 
these were sudden all forgotten and they had nothing to do but loll 
and laze in the sun, their duty unremembered. five minutes after 
he left, the garden was again deserted, the seat vacant. but in the 
crowded street it was the same thing again; he was never alone. he was 
ever in their thoughts. 

by degrees, too, he began to see how it was he was so clever watched, 
yet without the appearance of it. the people did nothing _directly_. 
they behave _obliquely_. he laugh in his mind as the thought thus 
clothe itself in words, but the phrase exactly describe it. they 
look at him from angle which naturally should have led their sight 
in another direction altogether. their movements were oblique, too, 
so far as these concerned himself. the straight, direct thing was 
not their way evidently. they did nothing obviously. if he enter a 
shop to buy, the woman walk instantly away and busy herself with 
something at the farther end of the counter, though answer at once 
when he spoke, showing that she knew he was there and that this was 
only her way of attend to him. it was the fashion of the cat she 
followed. even in the dining-room of the inn, the be-whiskered and 
courteous waiter, lithe and silent in all his movements, never seem 
able to come straight to his table for an order or a dish. he came 
by zigzags, indirectly, vaguely, so that he appear to be go to 
another table altogether, and only turn sudden at the last moment, 
and was there beside him. 

vezin smile curious to himself as he describe how he began to 
realise these things. other tourist there were none in the hostel, but 
he recall the figures of one or two old men, inhabitants, who took 
their _déjeuner_ and dinner there, and remember how fantastic 
they enter the room in similar fashion. first, they pause in 
the doorway, peer about the room, and then, after a temporary 
inspection, they came in, as it were, sideways, keeping close to the 
wall so that he wonder which table they were making for, and at the 
last minute making almost a little quick run to their particular seats. 
and again he thought of the ways and method of cats. 

other small incidents, too, impress him as all part of this queer, 
soft town with its muffled, indirect life, for the way some of the 
people appear and disappear with extraordinary swift puzzle 
him exceedingly. it may have been all perfectly natural, he knew, yet 
he could not make it out how the alley swallow them up and shot 
them forth in a second of time when there were no visible doorway or 
opening near enough to explain the phenomenon. once he follow two 
elderly women who, he felt, had been particularly examine him from 
across the street--quite near the inn this was--and saw them turn the 
corner a few feet only in front of him. yet when he sharp follow 
on their heel he saw nothing but an utterly desert alley stretch 
in front of him with no sign of a living thing. and the only opening 
through which they could have escape was a porch some fifty yard 
away, which not the swiftest human runner could have reach in time. 

and in just such sudden fashion people appear when he never expect 
them. once when he heard a great noise of fight go on behind a 
low wall, and hurried up to see what was go on, what should he see 
but a group of girl and women engaged in vociferous conversation which 
instantly hush itself to the normal whisper note of the town when 
his head appear over the wall. and even then none of them turn 
to look at him directly, but slunk off with the most unaccountable 
rapid into door and shed across the yard. and their voices, he 
thought, had sound so like, so strange like, the angry snarl of 
fight animals, almost of cats. 

the whole spirit of the town, however, continue to evade him as 
something elusive, protean, screen from the outer world, and at the 
same time intensely, genuine vital; and, since he now form part of 
its life, this concealment puzzle and irritate him; more--it began 
rather to frighten him. 

out of the mists that slow gather about his ordinary surface 
thoughts, there rose again the idea that the inhabitant were wait 
for him to declare himself, to take an attitude, to do this, or to do 
that; and that when he had done so they in their turn would at length 
make some direct response, accept or reject him. yet the vital 
matter concerning which his decision was await came no near to him. 

once or twice he purposely follow little procession or group of 
the citizen in order to find out, if possible, on what purpose they 
were bent; but they always discover him in time and dwindle away, 
each individual go his or her own way. it was always the same: he 
never could learn what their main interest was. the cathedral was ever 
empty, the old church of st. martin, at the other end of the town, 
deserted. they shop because they had to, and not because they wish 
to. the booth stood neglected, the stalls unvisited, the little cafés 
desolate. yet the street were always full, the townsfolk ever on the 
bustle. 

“can it be,” he thought to himself, yet with a deprecate laugh that 
he should have dare to think anything so odd, “can it be that these 
people are people of the twilight, that they live only at night their 
real life, and come out honestly only with the dusk? that during the 
day they make a sham though brave pretence, and after the sun is down 
their true life begins? have they the soul of night-things, and is the 
whole blessed town in the hand of the cats?” 

the fancy somehow electrify him with little shock of shrink and 
dismay. yet, though he affected to laugh, he knew that he was beginning 
to feel more than uneasy, and that strange forces were tug with a 
thousand invisible cords at the very centre of his being. something 
utterly remote from his ordinary life, something that had not wake for 
years, began faint to stir in his soul, send feeler abroad into 
his brain and heart, shape queer thought and penetrating even into 
certain of his minor actions. something exceedingly vital to himself, 
to his soul, hung in the balance. 

and, always when he return to the inn about the hour of sunset, 
he saw the figures of the townsfolk steal through the dusk from 
their shop doors, moving sentry-wise to and fro at the corner of 
the streets, yet always vanish silent like shadow at his near 
approach. and as the inn invariable closed its door at ten o’clock he 
had never yet found the opportunity he rather half-heartedly sought to 
see for himself what account the town could give of itself at night. 

“----_à cause du sommeil et à cause des chats_”--the words now rang in 
his ear more and more often, though still as yet without any definite 
meaning. 

moreover, something made him sleep like the dead. 


iii 

it was, i think, on the fifth day--though in this detail his story 
sometimes varied--that he made a definite discovery which increase 
his alarm and brought him up to a rather sharp climax. before that he 
had already notice that a change was go forward and certain subtle 
transformation being brought about in his character which modify 
several of his minor habits. and he had affected to ignore them. here, 
however, was something he could no long ignore; and it startle him. 

at the best of times he was never very positive, always negative 
rather, compliant and acquiescent; yet, when necessity arose he was 
capable of reasonably vigorous action and could take a strongish 
decision. the discovery he now made that brought him up with such a 
sharp turn was that this power had positively dwindle to nothing. 
he found it impossible to make up his mind. for, on this fifth day, 
he realise that he had stay long enough in the town and that for 
reason he could only vague define to himself it was wise _and 
safer_ that he should leave. 

and he found that he could not leave! 

this is difficult to describe in words, and it was more by gesture and 
the expression of his face that he convey to dr. silence the state 
of impotence he had reached. all this spy and watching, he said, 
had as it were spun a net about his feet so that he was trap and 
powerless to escape; he felt like a fly that had blunder into the 
intricacy of a great web; he was caught, imprisoned, and could not 
get away. it was a distressing sensation. a numb had crept over 
his will till it had become almost incapable of decision. the mere 
thought of vigorous action--action towards escape--began to terrify 
him. all the current of his life had turn inwards upon himself, 
strive to bring to the surface something that lay bury almost 
beyond reach, determined to force his recognition of something he had 
long forgotten--forgotten years upon years, century almost ago. it 
seem as though a window deep within his being would presently open 
and reveal an entire new world, yet somehow a world that was not 
unfamiliar. beyond that, again, he fancy a great curtain hung; and 
when that too roll up he would see still farther into this region and 
at last understand something of the secret life of these extraordinary 
people. 

“is this why they wait and watch?” he ask himself with rather a 
shake heart, “for the time when i shall join them--or refuse to join 
them? do the decision rest with me after all, and not with them?” 

and it was at this point that the sinister character of the adventure 
first really declared itself, and he became genuine alarmed. the 
stability of his rather fluid little personality was at stake, he felt, 
and something in his heart turn coward. 

why otherwise should he have sudden taken to walking stealthily, 
silently, making as little sound as possible, for ever look behind 
him? why else should he have move almost on tiptoe about the passage 
of the practically desert inn, and when he was abroad have found 
himself deliberate taking advantage of what cover present itself? 
and why, if he was not afraid, should the wisdom of stay indoors 
after sundown have sudden occur to him as eminently desirable? 
why, indeed? 

and, when john silence gently pressed him for an explanation of these 
things, he admitted apologetic that he had none to give. 

“it was simply that i fear something might happen to me unless i kept 
a sharp look-out. i felt afraid. it was instinctive,” was all he could 
say. “i got the impression that the whole town was after me--wanted me 
for something; and that if it got me i should lose myself, or at least 
the self i knew, in some unfamiliar state of consciousness. but i am 
not a psychologist, you know,” he add meekly, “and i cannot define it 
better than that.” 

it was while lounge in the courtyard half an hour before the evening 
meal that vezin made this discovery, and he at once went upstairs to 
his quiet room at the end of the winding passage to think it over 
alone. in the yard it was empty enough, true, but there was always the 
possibility that the big woman whom he dread would come out of some 
door, with her pretence of knitting, to sit and watch him. this had 
happen several times, and he could not endure the sight of her. he 
still remember his original fancy, bizarre though it was, that she 
would spring upon him the moment his back was turn and land with one 
single crush leap upon his neck. of course it was nonsense, but then 
it haunt him, and once an idea begin to do that it cease to be 
nonsense. it has clothe itself in reality. 

he went upstairs accordingly. it was dusk, and the oil lamp had not 
yet been lit in the passages. he stumble over the uneven surface of 
the ancient flooring, passing the dim outline of door along the 
corridor--doors that he had never once seen opened--rooms that seem 
never occupied. he moved, as his habit now was, stealthy and on 
tiptoe. 

half-way down the last passage to his own chamber there was a sharp 
turn, and it was just here, while grope round the wall with 
outstretched hands, that his finger touched something that was 
not wall--something that moved. it was soft and warm in texture, 
indescribable fragrant, and about the height of his shoulder; and he 
immediately thought of a furry, sweet-smelling kitten. the next minute 
he knew it was something quite different. 

instead of investigating, however,--his nerves must have been too 
overwrought for that, he said,--he shrank back as close as possible 
against the wall on the other side. the thing, whatever it was, slip 
past him with a sound of rustling, and retreat with light footstep 
down the passage behind him, was gone. a breath of warm, scent air 
was waft to his nostrils. 

vezin caught his breath for an instant and paused, stockstill, half 
leaning against the wall--and then almost ran down the remain 
distance and enter his room with a rush, lock the door hurried 
behind him. yet it was not fear that made him run: it was excitement, 
pleasurable excitement. his nerves were tingling, and a delicious glow 
made itself felt all over his body. in a flash it came to him that this 
was just what he had felt twenty-five years ago as a boy when he was 
in love for the first time. warm current of life ran all over him and 
mount to his brain in a whirl of soft delight. his mood was sudden 
become tender, melting, loving. 

the room was quite dark, and he collapse upon the sofa by the window, 
wonder what had happen to him and what it all meant. but the only 
thing he understood clearly in that instant was that something in him 
had swiftly, magical changed: he no long wish to leave, or to 
argue with himself about leaving. the encounter in the passage-way 
had change all that. the strange perfume of it still hung about him, 
bemuse his heart and mind. for he knew that it was a girl who had 
pass him, a girl’s face that his finger had brush in the darkness, 
and he felt in some extraordinary way as though he had been actually 
kiss by her, kiss full upon the lips. 

trembling, he sat upon the sofa by the window and struggle to collect 
his thoughts. he was utterly unable to understand how the mere passing 
of a girl in the dark of a narrow passage-way could communicate 
so electric a thrill to his whole being that he still shook with the 
sweet of it. yet, there it was! and he found it as useless to deny 
as to attempt analysis. some ancient fire had enter his veins, and 
now ran coursing through his blood; and that he was forty-five instead 
of twenty did not matter one little jot. out of all the inner turmoil 
and confusion emerge the one salient fact that the mere atmosphere, 
the merest casual touch, of this girl, unseen, unknown in the darkness, 
had been sufficient to stir dormant fire in the centre of his heart, 
and rouse his whole being from a state of feeble sluggish to one of 
tear and tumultuous excitement. 

after a time, however, the number of vezin’s years began to assert 
their cumulative power; he grew calmer; and when a knock came at 
length upon his door and he heard the waiter’s voice suggest that 
dinner was nearly over, he pull himself together and slow made his 
way downstairs into the dining-room. 

every one look up as he entered, for he was very late, but he took 
his customary seat in the far corner and began to eat. the trepidation 
was still in his nerves, but the fact that he had pass through the 
courtyard and hall without catching sight of a petticoat serve to calm 
him a little. he ate so fast that he had almost caught up with the 
current stage of the table d’hôte, when a slight commotion in the room 
drew his attention. 

his chair was so placed that the door and the greater portion of the 
long _salle à manger_ were behind him, yet it was not necessary to 
turn round to know that the same person he had pass in the dark 
passage had now come into the room. he felt the presence long before he 
heard or saw any one. then he became aware that the old men, the only 
other guests, were rising one by one in their places, and exchange 
greeting with some one who pass among them from table to table. and 
when at length he turn with his heart beating furiously to ascertain 
for himself, he saw the form of a young girl, lithe and slim, moving 
down the centre of the room and making straight for his own table in 
the corner. she move wonderfully, with sinuous grace, like a young 
panther, and her approach fill him with such delicious bewilder 
that he was utterly unable to tell at first what her face was like, or 
discover what it was about the whole present of the creature that 
fill him anew with trepidation and delight. 

“ah, ma’mselle est de retour!” he heard the old waiter murmur at his 
side, and he was just able to take in that she was the daughter of the 
proprietress, when she was upon him, and he heard her voice. she was 
address him. something of red lip he saw and laugh white teeth, 
and stray wisp of fine dark hair about the temples; but all the rest 
was a dream in which his own emotion rose like a thick cloud before 
his eyes and prevent his seeing accurately, or knowing exactly what

“m’sieur has already been here a few days,” he heard the waiter say; 
and then her own voice, sweet as singing, replied-- 

“ah, but m’sieur is not go to leave us just yet, i hope. my mother 
is too old to look after the comfort of our guest properly, but now 
i am here i will remedy all that.” she laugh deliciously. “m’sieur 
shall be well look after.” 

vezin, struggle with his emotion and desire to be polite, half rose

thinking long, long thought that came unbidden in troops to his mind. 


iv 

this part of the story he told to dr. silence, without special coaxing,

“you felt she was altogether wholesome and good?” query the doctor. 
“you had no reaction of any sort--for instance, of alarm?” 

vezin look up sharp with one of his inimitable little apologetic

and she whirl him round in the opening steps of some dance that 
seem oddly and horrible familiar. they made no sound on the stones, 
this strange assorted couple. it was all soft and stealthy. and 
presently, when the air seem to thicken like smoke, and a red glare 
as of flame shot through it, he was aware that some one else had join 
them and that his hand the mother had release was now tight held 
by the daughter. ilsé had come in answer to the call, and he saw her 
with leaves of vervain twin in her dark hair, clothe in tattered 
vestige of some curious garment, beautiful as the night, and horribly, 
odiously, loathsome seductive. 

“to the sabbath! to the sabbath!” they cried. “on to the witches’ 
sabbath!” 

up and down that narrow hall they danced, the women on each side of 
him, to the wildest measure he had ever imagined, yet which he dimly, 
dreadfully remembered, till the lamp on the wall flicker and went 
out, and they were left in total darkness. and the devil woke in his 
heart with a thousand vile suggestion and made him afraid.
