sudden they release his hand and he heard the voice of the mother 
cry that it was time, and they must go. which way they went he did 
not pause to see. he only realise that he was free, and he blunder 
through the dark till he found the stairs and then tore up them to 
his room as though all hell was at his heels. 

he flung himself on the sofa, with his face in his hands, and groaned. 
swift review a dozen ways of immediate escape, all equally 
impossible, he finally decided that the only thing to do for the moment 
was to sit quiet and wait. he must see what was go to happen. at 
least in the privacy of his own bedroom he would be fairly safe. the 
door was locked. he cross over and soft open the window which 
gave upon the courtyard and also permit a partial view of the hall 
through the glass doors. 

as he did so the hum and murmur of a great activity reach his ear 
from the street beyond--the sound of footstep and voice muffle by 
distance. he lean out cautious and listened. the moonlight was 
clear and strong now, but his own window was in shadow, the silver 
disc being still behind the house. it came to him irresistible that 
the inhabitant of the town, who a little while before had all been 
invisible behind closed doors, were now issue forth, busy upon some 
secret and unholy errand. he listen intently. 

at first everything about him was silent, but soon he became aware of 
movements go on in the house itself. rustling and cheep came 
to him across that still, moonlit yard. a concourse of living being 
sent the hum of their activity into the night. things were on the move 
everywhere. a biting, pungent odour rose through the air, coming he 
knew not whence. presently his eyes became glue to the window of 
the opposite wall where the moonshine fell in a soft blaze. the roof 
overhead, and behind him, was reflect clearly in the pane of glass, 
and he saw the outline of dark body moving with long footstep over 
the tile and along the coping. they pass swift and silently, 
shaped like immense cats, in an endless procession across the picture 
glass, and then appear to leap down to a lower level where he lost 
sight of them. he just caught the soft thud of their leaps. 
sometimes their shadow fell upon the white wall opposite, and then 
he could not make out whether they were the shadow of human being 
or of cats. they seem to change swift from one to the other. the 
transformation look horrible real, for they leap like human beings, 
yet change swift in the air immediately afterwards, and drop like 
animals. 

the yard, too, beneath him, was now alive with the creep movements 
of dark form all stealthy drawing towards the porch with the glass 
doors. they kept so close to the wall that he could not determine 
their actual shape, but when he saw that they pass on to the great 
congregation that was gathering in the hall, he understood that these 
were the creature whose leap shadow he had first seen reflect in 
the window-panes opposite. they were coming from all parts of the town, 
reach the appoint meeting-place across the roof and tiles, and 
spring from level to level till they came to the yard. 

then a new sound caught his ear, and he saw that the window all about 
him were being soft opened, and that to each window came a face. a 
moment later figures began drop hurried down into the yard. and 
these figures, as they lower themselves down from the windows, were 
human, he saw; but once safe in the yard they fell upon all fours and 
change in the swiftest possible second into--cats--huge, silent cats. 
they ran in stream to join the main body in the hall beyond. 

so, after all, the rooms in the house had not been empty and unoccupied. 

moreover, what he saw no long fill him with amazement. for he 
remember it all. it was familiar. it had all happen before just 
so, hundred of times, and he himself had taken part in it and known 
the wild madness of it all. the outline of the old building changed, 
the yard grew larger, and he seem to be staring down upon it from 
a much greater height through smoky vapours. and, as he looked, half 
remembering, the old pains of long ago, fierce and sweet, furiously 
assail him, and the blood stir horrible as he heard the call of 
the dance again in his heart and taste the ancient magic of ilsé 
whirl by his side. 

sudden he start back. a great lithe cat had leap soft up from 
the shadow below on to the sill close to his face, and was staring 
fixedly at him with the eyes of a human. “come,” it seem to say, 
“come with us to the dance! change as of old! transform yourself 
swift and come!” only too well he understood the creature’s soundless 
call. 

it was gone again in a flash with scarcely a sound of its pad feet 
on the stones, and then other drop by the score down the side of 
the house, past his very eyes, all change as they fell and dart 
away rapidly, softly, towards the gathering point. and again he felt 
the dreadful desire to do likewise; to murmur the old incantation, 
and then drop upon hand and knee and run swift for the great 
flying leap into the air. oh, how the passion of it rose within him 
like a flood, twist his very entrails, send his heart’s desire 
flaming forth into the night for the old, old dance of the sorcerer 
at the witches’ sabbath! the whirl of the stars was about him; once 
more he met the magic of the moon. the power of the wind, rush 
from precipice and forest, leap from cliff to cliff across the 
valleys, tore him away.... he heard the cries of the dance and their 
wild laughter, and with this savage girl in his embrace he dance 
furiously about the dim throne where sate the figure with the sceptre 
of majesty.... 

then, suddenly, all became hush and still, and the fever die down a 
little in his heart. the calm moonlight flood a courtyard empty and 
deserted. they had started. the procession was off into the sky. and he 
was left behind--alone. 

vezin tiptoe soft across the room and unlock the door. the murmur 
from the streets, grow momentarily as he advanced, met his ears. he 
made his way with the utmost caution down the corridor. at the head of 
the stairs he pause and listened. below him, the hall where they had 
gather was dark and still, but through open door and window on 
the far side of the building came the sound of a great throng moving 
farther and farther into the distance. 

he made his way down the creak wooden stairs, dread yet longing 
to meet some straggle who should point the way, but finding no one; 
across the dark hall, so lately throng with living, moving things, 
and out through the open front door into the street. he could not 
believe that he was really left behind, really forgotten, that he had 
been purposely permit to escape. it perplexed him. 

nervous he peer about him, and up and down the street; then, seeing 
nothing, advanced slow down the pavement. 

the whole town, as he went, show itself empty and deserted, as 
though a great wind had blown everything alive out of it. the door 
and window of the house stood open to the night; nothing stirred; 
moonlight and silence lay over all. the night lay about him like a 
cloak. the air, soft and cool, caress his cheek like the touch of a 
great furry paw. he gain confidence and began to walk quickly, though 
still keeping to the shadow side. nowhere could he discover the 
faintest sign of the great unholy exodus he knew had just taken place. 
the moon sail high over all in a sky, cloudless and serene. 

hardly realise where he was going, he cross the open market-place 
and so came to the ramparts, whence he knew a pathway descended to the 
high road and along which he could make good his escape to one of the 
other little town that lay to the northward, and so to the railway. 

but first he pause and gaze out over the scene at his feet where the 
great plain lay like a silver map of some dream country. the still 
beauty of it enter his heart, increase his sense of bewilder 
and unreality. no air stirred, the leaves of the plane tree stood 
motionless, the near detail were define with the sharp of day 
against dark shadows, and in the distance the field and wood melt 
away into haze and shimmer mistiness. 

but the breath caught in his throat and he stood stockstill as though 
transfix when his gaze pass from the horizon and fell upon the near 
prospect in the depth of the valley at his feet. the whole lower slope 
of the hill, that lay hid from the bright of the moon, were aglow, 
and through the glare he saw countless moving forms, shift thick and 
fast between the opening of the trees; while overhead, like leaves 
driven by the wind, he discern flying shape that hover dark one 
moment against the sky and then settled down with cries and weird 
singing through the branch into the region that was aflame. 

spellbound, he stood and stare for a time that he could not measure. 
and then, move by one of the terrible impulse that seem to control 
the whole adventure, he climb swift upon the top of the broad 
coping, and balanced a moment where the valley gape at his feet. but 
in that very instant, as he stood hovering, a sudden movement among the 
shadow of the house caught his eye, and he turn to see the outline 
of a large animal dart swift across the open space behind him, and 
land with a flying leap upon the top of the wall a little lower down. 
it ran like the wind to his feet and then rose up beside him upon the 
ramparts. a shiver seem to run through the moonlight, and his sight 
tremble for a second. his heart pulse fearfully. ilsé stood beside 
him, peer into his face. 

some dark substance, he saw, stain the girl’s face and skin, shining 
in the moonlight as she stretch her hand towards him; she was 
dress in wretched tattered garment that yet became her mightily; rue 
and vervain twin about her temples; her eyes glitter with unholy 
light. he only just control the wild impulse to take her in his arms 
and leap with her from their giddy perch into the valley below. 

“see!” she cried, point with an arm on which the rag flutter in 
the rising wind towards the forest aglow in the distance. “see where 
they await us! the wood are alive! already the great one are there, 
and the dance will soon begin! the salve is here! anoint yourself and 
come!” 

though a moment before the sky was clear and cloudless, yet even while 
she spoke the face of the moon grew dark and the wind began to toss 
in the crest of the plane tree at his feet. stray gust brought the 
sound of hoarse singing and crying from the lower slope of the hill, 
and the pungent odour he had already notice about the courtyard of the 
inn rose about him in the air. 

“transform, transform!” she cry again, her voice rising like a song. 
“rub well your skin before you fly. come! come with me to the sabbath, 
to the madness of its furious delight, to the sweet abandon of 
its evil worship! see! the great one are there, and the terrible 
sacrament prepared. the throne is occupied. anoint and come! anoint 
and come!” 

she grew to the height of a tree beside him, leap upon the wall with 
flaming eyes and hair strewn upon the night. he too began to change 
swiftly. her hand touched the skin of his face and neck, streak him 
with the burning salve that sent the old magic into his blood with the 
power before which fade all that is good. 

a wild roar came up to his ear from the heart of the wood, and the 
girl, when she heard it, leap upon the wall in the frenzy of her 
wicked joy. 

“satan is there!” she screamed, rush upon him and strive to draw 
him with her to the edge of the wall. “satan has come! the sacrament 
call us! come, with your dear apostate soul, and we will worship and 
dance till the moon die and the world is forgotten!” 

just saving himself from the dreadful plunge, vezin struggle to 
release himself from her grasp, while the passion tore at his reins and 
all but master him. he shriek aloud, not knowing what he said, and 
then he shriek again. it was the old impulses, the old awful habit 
instinctive finding voice; for though it seem to him that he merely 
shriek nonsense, the words he utter really had meaning in them, and 
were intelligible. it was the ancient call. and it was heard below. it 
was answered. 

the wind whistle at the skirt of his coat as the air round him 
darken with many flying form crowd upwards out of the valley. the 
crying of hoarse voice smote upon his ears, coming closer. stroke of 
wind buffet him, tear him this way and that along the crumble 
top of the stone wall; and ilsé clung to him with her long shining 
arms, smooth and bare, holding him fast about the neck. but not ilsé 
alone, for a dozen of them surround him, drop out of the air. 
the pungent odour of the anoint body stifle him, exciting him to 
the old madness of the sabbath, the dance of the witch and sorcerer 
do honour to the personify evil of the world. 

“anoint and away! anoint and away!” they cry in wild chorus about 
him. “to the dance that never dies! to the sweet and fearful fantasy of 
evil!” 

another moment and he would have yield and gone, for his will turn 
soft and the flood of passionate memory all but overwhelm him, 
when--so can a small thing alter the whole course of an adventure--he 
caught his foot upon a loose stone in the edge of the wall, and 
then fell with a sudden crash on to the ground below. but he fell 
towards the houses, in the open space of dust and cobble stones, and 
fortunately not into the gape depth of the valley on the farther side. 

and they, too, came in a tumble heap about him, like flies upon a 
piece of food, but as they fell he was release for a moment from 
the power of their touch, and in that brief instant of freedom there 
flash into his mind the sudden intuition that save him. before he 
could regain his feet he saw them scrabble awkward back upon the 
wall, as though bat-like they could only fly by drop from a height, 
and had no hold upon him in the open. then, seeing them perch there 
in a row like cat upon a roof, all dark and singularly shapeless, 
their eyes like lamps, the sudden memory came back to him of ilsé’s 
terror at the sight of fire. 

quick as a flash he found his match and lit the dead leaves that lay 
under the wall. 

dry and withered, they caught fire at once, and the wind carry the 
flame in a long line down the length of the wall, licking upwards as 
it ran; and with shriek and wailings, the crowded row of form upon 
the top melt away into the air on the other side, and were gone with 
a great rush and whirr of their body down into the heart of the 
haunt valley, leave vezin breathless and shaken in the middle of 
the desert ground. 

“ilsé!” he call feebly; “ilsé!” for his heart ache to think that she 
was really gone to the great dance without him, and that he had lost 
the opportunity of its fearful joy. yet at the same time his relief 
was so great, and he was so daze and trouble in mind with the whole 
thing, that he hardly knew what he was saying, and only cry aloud in 
the fierce storm of his emotion.... 

the fire under the wall ran its course, and the moonlight came out 
again, soft and clear, from its temporary eclipse. with one last 
shudder look at the ruin ramparts, and a feeling of horrid wonder 
for the haunt valley beyond, where the shape still crowded and flew, 
he turn his face towards the town and slow made his way in the 
direction of the hotel. 

and as he went, a great wail of cries, and a sound of howling, 
follow him from the gleam forest below, grow faint and 
faint with the burst of wind as he disappear between the houses. 


vi 

“it may seem rather abrupt to you, this sudden tame ending,” said 
arthur vezin, glancing with flushed face and timid eyes at dr. silence 
sitting there with his notebook, “but the fact is--er--from that moment 
my memory seem to have fail rather. i have no distinct recollection 
of how i got home or what precisely i did. 

“it appear i never went back to the inn at all. i only dim recollect 
racing down a long white road in the moonlight, past wood and 
villages, still and deserted, and then the dawn came up, and i saw the 
tower of a biggish town and so came to a station. 

“but, long before that, i remember pause somewhere on the road and 
look back to where the hill-town of my adventure stood up in the 
moonlight, and thinking how exactly like a great monstrous cat it lay 
there upon the plain, its huge front paw lie down the two main 
streets, and the twin and broken tower of the cathedral marking its 
torn ear against the sky. that picture stays in my mind with the 
utmost vivid to this day. 

“another thing remains in my mind from that escape--namely, the sudden 
sharp reminder that i had not paid my bill, and the decision i made, 
standing there on the dusty highroad, that the small baggage i had left 
behind would more than settle for my indebtedness. 

“for the rest, i can only tell you that i got coffee and bread at a 
café on the outskirts of this town i had come to, and soon after found 
my way to the station and caught a train later in the day. that same 
evening i reach london.” 

“and how long altogether,” ask john silence quietly, “do you think 
you stay in the town of the adventure?” 

vezin look up sheepishly. 

“i was coming to that,” he resumed, with apologetic wriggle of his 
body. “in london i found that i was a whole week out in my reckoning of 
time. i had stay over a week in the town, and it ought to have been 
september 15th,--instead of which it was only september 10th!” 

“so that, in reality, you had only stay a night or two in the inn?” 
query the doctor. 

vezin hesitate before replying. he shuffle upon the mat. 

“i must have gain time somewhere,” he said at length--“somewhere or 
somehow. i certainly had a week to my credit. i can’t explain it. i can 
only give you the fact.” 

“and this happen to you last year, since when you have never been 
back to the place?” 

“last autumn, yes,” murmur vezin; “and i have never dare to go 
back. i think i never want to.” 

“and, tell me,” ask dr. silence at length, when he saw that the 
little man had evidently come to the end of his words and had nothing 
more to say, “had you ever read up the subject of the old witchcraft 
practice during the middle ages, or been at all interested in the 
subject?” 

“never!” declared vezin emphatically. “i had never given a thought to 
such matter so far as i know----” 

“or to the question of reincarnation, perhaps?” 

“never--before my adventure; but i have since,” he reply 
significantly. 

there was, however, something still on the man’s mind that he wish 
to relieve himself of by confession, yet could with difficulty bring 
himself to mention; and it was only after the sympathetic tactful 
of the doctor had provided numerous opening that he at length avail 
himself of one of them, and stammer that he would like to show him 
the mark he still had on his neck where, he said, the girl had touched 
him with her anoint hands. 

he took off his collar after infinite fumble hesitation, and lower 
his shirt a little for the doctor to see. and there, on the surface of 
the skin, lay a faint reddish line across the shoulder and extend 
a little way down the back towards the spine. it certainly indicate 
exactly the position an arm might have taken in the act of embracing. 
and on the other side of the neck, slightly higher up, was a similar 
mark, though not quite so clearly defined. 

“that was where she held me that night on the ramparts,” he whispered, 
a strange light coming and go in his eyes. 

* * * * * 

it was some week later when i again found occasion to consult john 
silence concerning another extraordinary case that had come under my 
notice, and we fell to discuss vezin’s story. since hearing it, 
the doctor had made investigate on his own account, and one of his 
secretary had discover that vezin’s ancestor had actually live 
for generation in the very town where the adventure came to him. two 
of them, both women, had been try and convict as witches, and had 
been burn alive at the stake. moreover, it had not been difficult to 
prove that the very inn where vezin stay was built about 1700 upon 
the spot where the funeral pyre stood and the execution took place. 
the town was a sort of headquarters for all the sorcerer and witch 
of the entire region, and after conviction they were burnt there 
literally by scores. 

“it seem strange,” continue the doctor, “that vezin should have 
remain ignorant of all this; but, on the other hand, it was not the 
kind of history that successive generation would have been anxious to 
keep alive, or to repeat to their children. therefore i am inclined to 
think he still know nothing about it. 

“the whole adventure seem to have been a very vivid revival of the 
memory of an early life, cause by coming directly into contact 
with the living forces still intense enough to hang about the place, 
and, by a most singular chance too, with the very soul who had taken 
part with him in the event of that particular life. for the mother 
and daughter who impress him so strange must have been leading 
actors, with himself, in the scene and practice of witchcraft which 
at that period dominate the imagination of the whole country. 

“one has only to read the history of the times to know that 
these witch claim the power of transform themselves into 
various animals, both for the purpose of disguise and also to 
convey themselves swift to the scene of their imaginary orgies. 
lycanthropy, or the power to change themselves into wolves, was 
everywhere believe in, and the ability to transform themselves into 
cat by rubbing their body with a special salve or ointment provided 
by satan himself, found equal credence. the witchcraft trial abound in 
evidence of such universal beliefs.” 

dr. silence quote chapter and verse from many writer on the subject, 
and show how every detail of vezin’s adventure had a basis in the 
practice of those dark days. 

“but that the entire affair took place subjective in the man’s own 
consciousness, i have no doubt,” he went on, in reply to my questions; 
“for my secretary who has been to the town to investigate, discover 
his signature in the visitors’ book, and prove by it that he had 
arrive on september 8th, and left sudden without pay his bill. 
he left two days later, and they still were in possession of his dirty 
brown bag and some tourist clothes. i paid a few franc in settlement 
of his debt, and have sent his luggage on to him. the daughter was 
absent from home, but the proprietress, a large woman very much as he 
describe her, told my secretary that he had seem a very strange, 
absent-minded kind of gentleman, and after his disappearance she had 
fear for a long time that he had met with a violent end in the 
neighbouring forest where he use to roam about alone. 

“i should like to have obtain a personal interview with the daughter 
so as to ascertain how much was subjective and how much actually took 
place with her as vezin told it. for her dread of fire and the sight of 
burning must, of course, have been the intuitive memory of her former 
painful death at the stake, and have thus explain why he fancy more 
than once that he saw her through smoke and flame.” 

“and that mark on his skin, for instance?” i inquired. 

“merely the mark produce by hysterical brooding,” he replied, “like 
the stigmata of the _religieuses_, and the bruise which appear on 
the body of hypnotise subject who have been told to expect them. 
this is very common and easily explained. only it seem curious that 
these mark should have remain so long in vezin’s case. usually they 
disappear quickly.” 

“obviously he is still thinking about it all, brooding, and living it 
all over again,” i ventured. 

“probably. and this make me fear that the end of his trouble is not 
yet. we shall hear of him again. it is a case, alas! i can do little to 
alleviate.” 

dr. silence spoke grave and with sad in his voice. 

“and what do you make of the frenchman in the train?” i ask 
further--“the man who warn him against the place, _à cause du sommeil 
et à cause des chats_? surely a very singular incident?” 

“a _very_ singular incident indeed,” he made answer slowly, “and one i 
can only explain on the basis of a highly improbable coincidence----” 

“namely?” 

“that the man was one who had himself stay in the town and undergone 
there a similar experience. i should like to find this man and ask him. 
but the crystal is useless here, for i have no slightest clue to go 
upon, and i can only conclude that some singular psychic affinity, some 
force still active in his being out of the same past life, drew him 
thus to the personality of vezin, and enable him to fear what might 
happen to him, and thus to warn him as he did. 

“yes,” he presently continued, half talk to himself, “i suspect in 
this case that vezin was swept into the vortex of forces arise out of 
the intense activity of a past life, and that he live over again a 
scene in which he had often play a leading part century before. for 
strong action set up forces that are so slow to exhaust themselves, 
they may be said in a sense never to die. in this case they were not 
vital enough to render the illusion complete, so that the little man 
found himself caught in a very distressing confusion of the present 
and the past; yet he was sufficient sensitive to recognise that it 
was true, and to fight against the degrade of returning, even in 
memory, to a former and lower state of development. 

“ah yes!” he continued, crossing the floor to gaze at the darken 
sky, and seemingly quite oblivious of my presence, “subliminal 
up-rushes of memory like this can be exceedingly painful, and sometimes 
exceedingly dangerous. i only trust that this gentle soul may soon 
escape from this obsession of a passionate and tempestuous past. but i 
doubt it, i doubt it.” 

his voice was hush with sad as he spoke, and when he turn back 
into the room again there was an expression of profound yearning upon 
his face, the yearning of a soul whose desire to help is sometimes 
greater than his power. 




case iii 

the nemesis of fire 


i 

by some means which i never could fathom, john silence always contrived 
to keep the compartment to himself, and as the train had a clear run 
of two hour before the first stop, there was ample time to go over 
the preliminary fact of the case. he had telephone to me that very 
morning, and even through the disguise of the mile of wire the thrill 
of incalculable adventure had sound in his voice. 

“as if it were an ordinary country visit,” he called, in reply to my 
question; “and don’t forget to bring your gun.” 

“with blank cartridges, i suppose?” for i knew his rigid principles 
with regard to the taking of life, and guess that the gun were 
merely for some obvious purpose of disguise. 

then he thank me for coming, mention the train, snap down the 
receiver, and left me vibrate with the excitement of anticipation 
to do my packing. for the honour of accompany dr. john silence on 
one of his big case was what many would have considered an empty 
honour--and risky. certainly the adventure held all manner of 
possibilities, and i arrive at waterloo with the feelings of a man who 
is about to embark on some dangerous and peculiar mission in which the 
danger he expect to run will not be the ordinary danger to life and 
limb, but of some secret character difficult to name and still more 
difficult to cope with. 

“the manor house has a high sound,” he told me, as we sat with our 
feet up and talked, “but i believe it is little more than an overgrown 
farmhouse in the desolate heather country beyond d----, and its owner, 
colonel wragge, a retired soldier with a taste for books, lives there 
practically alone, i understand, with an elderly invalid sister. so you 
need not look forward to a lively visit, unless the case provide some 
excitement of its own.” 

“which is likely?” 

by way of reply he hand me a letter marked “private.” it was dated a 
week ago, and sign “yours faithfully, horace wragge.” 

“he heard of me, you see, through captain anderson,” the doctor 
explain modestly, as though his fame were not almost world-wide; “you 
remember that indian obsession case----” 

i read the letter. why it should have been marked private was difficult 
to understand. it was very brief, direct, and to the point. it refer 
by way of introduction to captain anderson, and then state quite 
simply that the writer need help of a peculiar kind and ask for a 
personal interview--a morning interview, since it was impossible for 
him to be absent from the house at night. the letter was dignified 
even to the point of abruptness, and it is difficult to explain how 
it manage to convey to me the impression of a strong man, shaken 
and perplexed. perhaps the restraint of the wording, and the mystery 
of the affair had something to do with it; and the reference to the 
anderson case, the horror of which lay still vivid in my memory, may 
have touched the sense of something rather ominous and alarming. but, 
whatever the cause, there was no doubt that an impression of serious 
peril rose somehow out of that white paper with the few lines of firm 
writing, and the spirit of a deep uneasy ran between the words and 
reach the mind without any visible form of expression. 

“and when you saw him----?” i asked, return the letter as the train 
rush clatter noisy through clapham junction. 

“i have not seen him,” was the reply. “the man’s mind was charged to 
the brim when he wrote that; full of vivid mental pictures. notice 
the restraint of it. for the main character of his case psychometry 
could be depend upon, and the scrap of paper his hand has touched 
is sufficient to give to another mind--a sensitive and sympathetic 
mind--clear mental pictures of what is go on. i think i have a very 
sound general idea of his problem.” 

“so there may be excitement after all?” 

john silence wait a moment before he replied. 

“something very serious is amiss there,” he said gravely, at length. 
“some one--not himself, i gather,--has been meddle with a rather 
dangerous kind of gunpowder. so--yes, there may be excitement, as you 
put it.” 

“and my duties?” i asked, with a decidedly grow interest. “remember, 
i am your ‘assistant.’” 

“behave like an intelligent confidential secretary. observe everything, 
without seeming to. say nothing--nothing that means anything. be 
present at all interviews. i may ask a good deal of you, for if my 
impression are correct this is----” 

he broke off suddenly. 

“but i won’t tell you my impression yet,” he resume after a moment’s 
thought. “just watch and listen as the case proceeds. form your 
own impression and cultivate your intuitions. we come as ordinary 
visitors, of course,” he added, a twinkle showing for an instant in his 
eye; “hence, the guns.” 

though disappointed not to hear more, i recognise the wisdom of 
his words and knew how valueless my impression would be once the 
powerful suggestion of have heard his own lay behind them. i likewise 
reflect that intuition join to a sense of humour was of more use to 
a man than double the quantity of mere “brains,” as such. 

before putt the letter away, however, he hand it back, telling me 
to place it against my forehead for a few moments and then describe any 
pictures that came spontaneous into my mind. 

“don’t deliberate look for anything. just imagine you see the inside 
of the eyelid, and wait for pictures that rise against its dark screen.” 

i follow his instructions, making my mind as nearly a blank as 
possible. but no vision came. i saw nothing but the lines of light 
that pass to and fro like the change of a kaleidoscope across the 
blackness. a momentary sensation of warmth came and went curiously. 

“you see--what?” he ask presently. 

“nothing,” i was oblige to admit disappointedly; “nothing but the 
usual flash of light one always sees. only, perhaps, they are more 
vivid than usual.” 

he said nothing by way of comment or reply. 

“and they group themselves now and then,” i continued, with painful 
candour, for i long to see the pictures he had spoken of, “group 
themselves into globe and round balls of fire, and the lines that 
flash about sometimes look like triangle and crosses--almost like 
geometric figures. nothing more.” 

i open my eyes again, and gave him back the letter. 

“it make my head hot,” i said, feeling somehow unworthy for not seeing 
anything of interest. but the look in his eyes arrest my attention at 
once. 

“that sensation of heat is important,” he said significantly. 

“it was certainly real, and rather uncomfortable,” i replied, hope 
he would expand and explain. “there was a distinct feeling of 
warmth--internal warmth somewhere--oppressive in a sense.” 

“that is interesting,” he remarked, putt the letter back in his 
pocket, and settle himself in the corner with newspaper and books. 
he vouchsafe nothing more, and i knew the useless of try to 
make him talk. following his example i settled likewise with magazine 
into my corner. but when i closed my eyes again to look for the 
flash lights and the sensation of heat, i found nothing but the 
usual phantasmagoria of the day’s events--faces, scenes, memories,--and 
in due course i fell asleep and then saw nothing at all of any kind. 

when we left the train, after six hours’ travelling, at a little 
wayside station standing without tree in a world of sand and heather, 
the late october shadow had already drop their sombre veil upon the 
landscape, and the sun dip almost out of sight behind the moorland 
hills. in a high dogcart, behind a fast horse, we were soon rattling 
across the undulate stretch of an open and bleak country, the keen 
air sting our cheek and the scent of pine and bracken strong 
about us. bare hill were faint visible against the horizon, and the 
coachman pointed to a bank of distant shadow on our left where he told 
us the sea lay. occasional stone farmhouses, standing back from the 
road among straggle fir trees, and large black barn that seem to 
shift past us with a movement of their own in the gloom, were the only 
sign of humanity and civilisation that we saw, until at the end of a 
bracing five mile the lights of the lodge gate flared before us and 
we plunge into a thick grove of pine tree that conceal the manor 
house up to the moment of actual arrival. 

colonel wragge himself met us in the hall. he was the typical army 
officer who had seen service, real service, and found himself in the 
process. he was tall and well built, broad in the shoulders, but lean 
as a greyhound, with grave eyes, rather stern, and a moustache turning 
grey. i judge him to be about sixty years of age, but his movements 
show a supple of strength and agile that contradict the 
years. the face was full of character and resolution, the face of a man 
to be depend upon, and the straight grey eyes, it seem to me, wore 
a veil of perplexed anxiety that he made no attempt to disguise. the 
whole appearance of the man at once clothe the adventure with gravity 
and importance. a matter that gave such a man cause for serious alarm, 
i felt, must be something real and of genuine moment. 

his speech and manner, as he welcome us, were like his letter, simple 
and sincere. he had a nature as direct and undeviate as a bullet. 
thus, he show plainly his surprise that dr. silence had not come 
alone. 

“my confidential secretary, mr. hubbard,” the doctor said, introduce 
me, and the steady gaze and powerful shake of the hand i then 
received were well calculated, i remember thinking, to drive home the 
impression that here was a man who was not to be trifle with, and 
whose perplexity must spring from some very real and tangible cause. 
and, quite obviously, he was relieved that we had come. his welcome was 
unmistakable genuine. 

he led us at once into a room, half library, half smoking-room, 
that open out of the low-ceilinged hall. the manor house gave the 
impression of a rambling and glorify farmhouse, solid, ancient, 
comfortable, and wholly unpretentious. and so it was. only the heat of 
the place struck me as unnatural. this room with the blazing fire may 
have seem uncomfortable warm after the long drive through the night 
air; yet it seem to me that the hall itself, and the whole atmosphere 
of the house, breathe a warmth that hardly belong to well-filled 
grate or the pipes of hot air and water. it was not the heat of the 
greenhouse; it was an oppressive heat that somehow got into the head 
and mind. it stir a curious sense of uneasy in me, and i caught 
myself thinking of the sensation of warmth that had emanate from the 
letter in the train. 

i heard him thank dr. silence for have come; there was no 
preamble, and the exchange of civility was of the briefest 
description. evidently here was a man who, like my companion, love 
action rather than talk. his manner was straightforward and direct. i 
saw him in a flash: puzzled, worried, harass into a state of alarm by 
something he could not comprehend; forced to deal with things he would 
have prefer to despise, yet facing it all with dogged serious 
and making no attempt to conceal that he felt secret ashamed of his 
incompetence. 

“so i cannot offer you much entertainment beyond that of my own 
company, and the queer business that has been go on here, and is 
still go on,” he said, with a slight inclination of the head towards 
me by way of including me in his confidence. 

“i think, colonel wragge,” reply john silence impressively, “that we 
shall none of us find the time hang heavy. i gather we shall have our 
hand full.” 

the two men look at one another for the space of some seconds, and 
there was an indefinable quality in their silence which for the first 
time made me admit a swift question into my mind; and i wonder a 
little at my rash in coming with so little reflection into a big 
case of this incalculable doctor. but no answer suggest itself, and 
to withdraw was, of course, inconceivable. the gate had closed behind 
me now, and the spirit of the adventure was already besiege my mind 
with its advance guard of a thousand little hope and fears. 

explain that he would wait till after dinner to discuss anything 
serious, as no reference was ever made before his sister, he led the 
way upstairs and show us personally to our rooms; and it was just as 
i was finish dressing that a knock came at my door and dr. silence 
entered. 

he was always what is call a serious man, so that even in moments of 
comedy you felt he never lost sight of the profound gravity of life, 
but as he came across the room to me i caught the expression of his 
face and understood in a flash that he was now in his most grave and 
earnest mood. he look almost troubled. i stop fumble with my 
black tie and stared. 

“it _is_ serious,” he said, speak in a low voice, “more so even than 
i imagined. colonel wragge’s control over his thought conceal a 
great deal in my psychometrise of the letter. i look in to warn you 
to keep yourself well in hand--generally speaking.” 

“haunted house?” i asked, conscious of a distinct shiver down my back. 

but he smile grave at the question. 

“haunted house of life more likely,” he replied, and a look came into 
his eyes which i had only seen there when a human soul was in the toils 
and he was thick in the fight of rescue. he was stir in the deeps. 

“colonel wragge--or the sister?” i ask hurriedly, for the gong was 
sounding. 

“neither directly,” he said from the door. “something far older, 
something very, very remote indeed. this thing has to do with the ages, 
unless i am mistaken greatly, the age on which the mists of memory 
have long lain undisturbed.” 

he came across the floor very quick with a finger on his lips, 
look at me with a peculiar searching of gaze. 

“are you aware yet of anything--odd here?” he ask in a whisper. 
“anything you cannot quite define, for instance. tell me, hubbard, for 
i want to know all your impressions. they may help me.” 

i shook my head, avoid his gaze, for there was something in the eyes 
that scared me a little. but he was so in earnest that i set my mind 
keen searching. 

“nothing yet,” i reply truthfully, wish i could confess to a real 
emotion; “nothing but the strange heat of the place.” 

he gave a little jump forward in my direction. 

“the heat again, that’s it!” he exclaimed, as though glad of my 
corroboration. “and how would you describe it, perhaps?” he ask 
quickly, with a hand on the door knob. 

“it doesn’t seem like ordinary physical heat,” i said, casting about in 
my thought for a definition. 

“more a mental heat,” he interrupted, “a glowing of thought and desire, 
a sort of feverish warmth of the spirit. isn’t that it?” 

i admitted that he had exactly describe my sensations. 

“good!” he said, as he open the door, and with an indescribable 
gesture that combine a warning to be ready with a sign of praise for 
my correct intuition, he was gone. 

i hurried after him, and found the two men wait for me in front of 
the fire. 

“i ought to warn you,” our host was saying as i came in, “that my 
sister, whom you will meet at dinner, is not aware of the real object 
of your visit. she is under the impression that we are interested in 
the same line of study--folklore--and that your research have led to 
my seek acquaintance. she come to dinner in her chair, you know. it 
will be a great pleasure to her to meet you both. we have few visitors.” 

so that on enter the dining-room we were prepared to find miss 
wragge already at her place, seat in a sort of bath-chair. she was 
a vivacious and charming old lady, with smile expression and bright 
eyes, and she chat all through dinner with unfailing spontaneity. 
she had that face, unlined and fresh, that some people carry through 
life from the cradle to the grave; her smooth plump cheek were 
all pink and white, and her hair, still dark, was divide into two 
glossy and sleek halves on either side of a careful parting. she wore 
gold-rimmed glasses, and at her throat was a large scarab of green 
jasper that made a very handsome brooch. 

her brother and dr. silence talk little, so that most of the 
conversation was carry on between herself and me, and she told me a 
great deal about the history of the old house, most of which i fear i 
listen to with but half an ear. 

“and when cromwell stay here,” she babble on, “he occupy the very 
rooms upstairs that use to be mine. but my brother think it safe for 
me to sleep on the ground floor now in case of fire.” 

and this sentence has stay in my memory only because of the sudden 
way her brother interrupt her and instantly led the conversation 
on to another topic. the passing reference to fire seem to have 
disturbed him, and thenceforward he direct the talk himself. 

it was difficult to believe that this lively and animate old lady, 
sitting beside me and taking so eager an interest in the affair of 
life, was practically, we understood, without the use of her lower 
limbs, and that her whole existence for years had been pass between 
the sofa, the bed, and the bath-chair in which she chat so naturally 
at the dinner-table. she made no allusion to her affliction until the 
dessert was reached, and then, touching a bell, she made us a witty 
little speech about leave us “like time, on noiseless feet,” and was 
wheel out of the room by the butler and carry off to her apartments 
at the other end of the house. 

and the rest of us were not long in following suit, for dr. silence and 
myself were quite as eager to learn the nature of our errand as our 
host was to impart it to us. he led us down a long flag passage to a 
room at the very end of the house, a room provided with double doors, 
and windows, i saw, heavy shuttered. books line the wall on every 
side, and a large desk in the bow window was pile up with volumes, 
some open, some shut, some showing scraps of paper stuck between the 
leaves, and all smother in a general cataract of untidy foolscap and 
loose half sheets. 

“my study and workroom,” explain colonel wragge, with a delightful 
touch of innocent pride, as though he were a very serious scholar. 
he placed arm-chairs for us round the fire. “here,” he add 
significantly, “we shall be safe from interrupt and can talk 
securely.” 

during dinner the manner of the doctor had been all that was natural 
and spontaneous, though it was impossible for me, knowing him as i 
did, not to be aware that he was subconscious very keen alert and 
already receiving upon the ultra-sensitive surface of his mind various 
and vivid impressions; and there was now something in the gravity 
of his face, as well as in the significant tone of colonel wragge’s 
speech, and something, too, in the fact that we three were shut away in 
this private chamber about to listen to things probably strange, and 
certainly mysterious--something in all this that touched my imagination 
sharp and sent an undeniable thrill along my nerves. taking the chair 
indicate by my host, i lit my cigar and wait for the opening of the 
attack, fully conscious that we were now too far gone in the adventure 
to admit of withdrawal, and wonder a little anxious where it was 
go to lead. 

what i expect precisely, it is hard to say. nothing definite, 
perhaps. only the sudden change was dramatic. a few hour before the 
prosaic atmosphere of piccadil was about me, and now i was sitting in 
a secret chamber of this remote old building wait to hear an account 
of things that held possibly the genuine heart of terror. i thought of 
the dreary moor and hill outside, and the dark pine copse sough 
in the wind of night; i remember my companion’s singular words up in 
my bedroom before dinner; and then i turn and noted careful the 
stern countenance of the colonel as he face us and lit his big black 
cigar before speaking. 

the threshold of an adventure, i reflect as i wait for the first 
words, is always the most thrill moment--until the climax comes. 

but colonel wragge hesitated--mentally--a long time before he began. 
he talk brief of our journey, the weather, the country, and other 
comparatively trivial topics, while he sought about in his mind for an 
appropriate entry into the subject that was uppermost in the thought 
of all of us. the fact was he found it a difficult matter to speak of 
at all, and it was dr. silence who finally show him the way over the 
hedge. 

“mr hubbard will take a few note when you are ready--you won’t 
object,” he suggested; “i can give my undivided attention in this way.” 

“by all means,” turning to reach some of the loose sheet on the 
writing table, and glancing at me. he still hesitate a little, i 
thought. “the fact is,” he said apologetically, “i wonder if it 
was quite fair to trouble you so soon. the daylight might suit you 
better to hear what i have to tell. your sleep, i mean, might be less 
disturbed, perhaps.” 

“i appreciate your thoughtfulness,” john silence reply with his 
gentle smile, taking command as it were from that moment, “but really 
we are both quite immune. there is nothing, i think, that could prevent 
either of us sleeping, except--an outbreak of fire, or some such very 
physical disturbance.” 

colonel wragge raise his eyes and look fixedly at him. this 
reference to an outbreak of fire i felt sure was made with a purpose. 
it certainly had the desire effect of remove from our host’s manner 
the last sign of hesitancy. 

“forgive me,” he said. “of course, i know nothing of your method in 
matter of this kind--so, perhaps, you would like me to begin at once 
and give you an outline of the situation?” 

dr. silence bowed his agreement. “i can then take my precaution 
accordingly,” he add calmly. 

the soldier look up for a moment as though he did not quite gather 
the meaning of these words; but he made no further comment and 
turn at once to tackle a subject on which he evidently talk with 
diffidence and unwillingness. 

“it’s all so utterly out of my line of things,” he began, puff out 
cloud of cigar smoke between his words, “and there’s so little to tell 
with any real evidence behind it, that it’s almost impossible to make 
a consecutive story for you. it’s the total cumulative effect that is 
so--so disquieting.” he chose his words with care, as though determined 
not to travel one hair’s breadth beyond the truth. 

“i came into this place twenty years ago when my elder brother died,” 
he continued, “but could not afford to live here then. my sister, whom 
you met at dinner, kept house for him till the end, and during all 
these years, while i was seeing service abroad, she had an eye to the 
place--for we never got a satisfactory tenant--and saw that it was not 
allow to go to ruin. i myself took possession, however, only a year 
ago. 

“my brother,” he went on, after a perceptible pause, “spent much of 
his time away, too. he was a great traveller, and fill the house 
with stuff he brought home from all over the world. the laundry--a 
small detached building beyond the servants’ quarters--he turn into a 
regular little museum. the curio and things i have clear away--they 
collected dust and were always get broken--but the laundry-house 
you shall see tomorrow.” 

colonel wragge spoke with such deliberation and with so many pause 
that this beginning took him a long time. but at this point he came to 
a full stop altogether. evidently there was something he wish to say 
that cost him considerable effort. at length he look up steady into 
my companion’s face. 

“may i ask you--that is, if you won’t think it strange,” he said, and a 
sort of hush came over his voice and manner, “whether you have notice 
anything at all unusual--anything queer, since you came into the house?” 

dr. silence answer without a moment’s hesitation. 

“i have,” he said. “there is a curious sensation of heat in the place.” 

“ah!” exclaim the other, with a slight start. “you _have_ notice it. 
this unaccountable heat----” 

“but its cause, i gather, is not in the house itself--but outside,” i 
was astonish to hear the doctor add. 

colonel wragge rose from his chair and turn to unhook a frame map 
that hung upon the wall. i got the impression that the movement was 
made with the deliberate purpose of conceal his face. 

“your diagnosis, i believe, is amazing accurate,” he said after a 
moment, turning round with the map in his hands. “though, of course, i 
can have no idea how you should guess----” 

john silence shrug his shoulder expressively. “merely my 
impression,” he said. “if you pay attention to impressions, and do not 
allow them to be confuse by deduction of the intellect, you will 
often find them surprisingly, uncannily, accurate.” 

colonel wragge resume his seat and laid the map upon his knees. his 
face was very thoughtful as he plunge abrupt again into his story. 

“on coming into possession,” he said, look us alternate in 
the face, “i found a crop of story of the most extraordinary and 
impossible kind i had ever heard--stories which at first i treat with 
amuse indifference, but later was forced to regard seriously, if only 
to keep my servants. these story i thought i trace to the fact of my 
brother’s death--and, in a way, i think so still.” 

he leant forward and hand the map to dr. silence. 

“it’s an old plan of the estate,” he explained, “but accurate enough 
for our purpose, and i wish you would note the position of the 
plantation marked upon it, especially those near the house. that 
one,” indicate the spot with his finger, “is call the twelve acre 
plantation. it was just there, on the side nearest the house, that my 
brother and the head keeper met their deaths.” 

he spoke as a man forced to recognise fact that he deplored, and would 
have prefer to leave untouched--things he personally would rather 
have treat with ridicule if possible. it made his words peculiarly 
dignified and impressive, and i listen with an increase uneasy 
as to the sort of help the doctor would look to me for later. it seem 
as though i were a spectator of some drama of mystery in which any 
moment i might be summon to play a part. 

“it was twenty years ago,” continue the colonel, “but there was much 
talk about it at the time, unfortunately, and you may, perhaps, have 
heard of the affair. stride, the keeper, was a passionate, hot-tempered 
man, but i regret to say, so was my brother, and quarrel between them 
seem to have been frequent.” 

“i do not recall the affair,” said the doctor. “may i ask what was the 
cause of death?” something in his voice made me prick up my ear for 
the reply. 

“the keeper, it was said, from suffocation. and at the inquest the 
doctor aver that both men had been dead the same length of time 
when found.” 

“and your brother?” ask john silence, notice the omission, and 
listen intently. 

“equally mysterious,” said our host, speak in a low voice with 
effort. “but there was one distressing feature i think i ought to 
mention. for those who saw the face--i did not see it myself--and 
though stride carry a gun its chambers were undischarged----” he 
stammer and hesitate with confusion. again that sense of terror 
move between his words. he stuck. 

“yes,” said the chief listener sympathetically. 

“my brother’s face, they said, look as though it had been scorched. 
it had been swept, as it were, by something that burned--blasted. it 
was, i am told, quite dreadful. the body were found lie side by 
side, face downwards, both point away from the wood, as though they 
had been in the act of running, and not more than a dozen yard from 
its edge.” 

dr. silence made no comment. he appear to be study the map 
attentively. 

“i did not see the face myself,” repeated the other, his manner somehow 
express the sense of awe he contrived to keep out of his voice, 
“but my sister unfortunately did, and her present state i believe to 
be entire due to the shock it gave to her nerves. she never can be 
brought to refer to it, naturally, and i am even inclined to think that 
the memory has merciful been permit to vanish from her mind. but 
she spoke of it at the time as a face swept by flame--blasted.” 

john silence look up from his contemplation of the map, but with the 
air of one who wish to listen, not to speak, and presently colonel 
wragge went on with his account. he stood on the mat, his broad 
shoulder hiding most of the mantelpiece. 

“they all centre about this particular plantation, these stories. that 
was to be expected, for the people here are as superstitious as ireland 
peasantry, and though i made one or two example among them to stop the 
foolish talk, it had no effect, and new version came to my ear every 
week. you may imagine how little good dismissal did, when i tell you 
that the servant dismiss themselves. it was not the house servants, 
but the men who work on the estate outside. the keeper gave notice 
one after another, none of them with any reason i could accept; the 
forester refuse to enter the wood, and the beater to beat in it. 
word flew all over the countryside that twelve acre plantation was a 
place to be avoided, day or night. 

“there came a point,” the colonel went on, now well in his swing, “when 
i felt compel to make investigate on my own account. i could 
not kill the thing by ignore it; so i collected and analyse the 
story at first hand. for this twelve acre wood, you will see by the 
map, come rather near home. its lower end, if you will look, almost 
touch the end of the back lawn, as i will show you tomorrow, and its 
dense growth of pine form the chief protection the house enjoy from 
the east wind that blow up from the sea. and in olden days, before my 
brother interfere with it and frightened all the game away, it was one 
of the best pheasant covert on the whole estate.” 

“and what form, if i may ask, did this interference take?” ask dr. 
silence. 

“in detail, i cannot tell you, for i do not know--except that i 
understand it was the subject of his frequent difference with the head 
keeper; but during the last two years of his life, when he gave up 
travel and settled down here, he took a special interest in this 
wood, and for some unaccountable reason began to build a low stone wall 
round it. this wall was never finished, but you shall see the ruins 
tomorrow in the daylight.” 

“and the result of your investigations--these stories, i mean?” the 
doctor broke in, anxious to keep him to the main issues. 

“yes, i’m coming to that,” he said slowly, “but the wood first, for 
this wood out of which they grew like mushroom has nothing in any way 
peculiar about it. it is very thick grown, and rise to a clear 
part in the centre, a sort of mound where there is a circle of large 
boulders--old druid stones, i’m told. at another place there’s a small 
pond. there’s nothing distinctive about it that i could mention--just 
an ordinary pine-wood, a very ordinary pine-wood--only the tree are a 
bit twist in the trunks, some of ’em, and very dense. nothing more. 

“and the stories? well, none of them had anything to do with my poor 
brother, or the keeper, as you might have expected; and they were all 
odd--such odd things, i mean, to invent or imagine. i never could make 
out how these people got such notions into their heads.” 

he pause a moment to relight his cigar. 

“there’s no regular path through it,” he resumed, puff vigorously, 
“but the field round it are constant used, and one of the garden 
whose cottage lie over that way declared he often saw moving lights in 
it at night, and luminous shape like globe of fire over the tops of 
the trees, skim and floating, and making a soft hiss sound--most 
of ’em said that, in fact--and another man saw shape flit in and 
out among the trees, things that were neither men nor animals, and all 
faint luminous. no one ever pretended to see human forms--always 
queer, huge things they could not properly describe. sometimes the 
whole wood was lit up, and one fellow--he’s still here and you shall 
see him--has a most circumstantial yarn about have seen great stars 
lie on the ground round the edge of the wood at regular intervals----” 

“what kind of stars?” put in john silence sharply, in a sudden way that 
made me start. 

“oh, i don’t know quite; ordinary stars, i think he said, only very 
large, and apparently blazing as though the ground was alight. he was 
too terrify to go close and examine, and he has never seen them 
since.” 

he stoop and stir the fire into a welcome blaze--welcome for its 
blaze of light rather than for its heat. in the room there was already 
a strange pervade sensation of warmth that was oppressive in its 
effect and far from comforting. 

“of course,” he went on, straighten up again on the mat, “this was 
all commonplace enough--this seeing lights and figures at night. most 
of these fellow drink, and imagination and terror between them may 
account for almost anything. but other saw things in broad daylight. 
one of the woodmen, a sober, respectable man, took the shortcut home to 
his midday meal, and swore he was follow the whole length of the wood 
by something that never show itself, but dodge from tree to tree, 
always keeping out of sight, yet solid enough to make the branch sway 
and the twig snap on the ground. and it made a noise, he declared--but 
really”--the speaker stop and gave a short laugh--“it’s too 
absurd----” 

“_please!_” insist the doctor; “for it is these small detail that 
give me the best clue always.” 

“----it made a crackling noise, he said, like a bonfire. those were his 
very words: like the crackling of a bonfire,” finished the soldier, 
with a repetition of his short laugh. 

“most interesting,” dr. silence observe gravely. “please omit nothing.” 

“yes” he went on, “and it was soon after that the fire began--the 
fire in the wood. they start mysterious burning in the patch of 
coarse white grass that cover the more open parts of the plantation. no 
one ever actually saw them start, but many, myself among the number, 
have seen them burning and smouldering. they are always small and 
circular in shape, and for all the world like a picnic fire. the head 
keeper has a dozen explanations, from sparks flying out of the house 
chimney to the sunlight focus through a dewdrop, but none of them, 
i must admit, convince me as being in the least likely or probable. 
they are most singular, i consider, most singular, these mysterious 
fires, and i am glad to say that they come only at rather long 
interval and never seem to spread. 

“but the keeper had other queer story as well, and about things that 
are verifiable. he declared that no life ever willing enter the 
plantation; more, that no life exist in it at all. no bird nest in 
the trees, or flew into their shade. he set countless traps, but never 
caught so much as a rabbit or a weasel. animal avoid it, and more 
than once he had picked up dead creature round the edge that bore no 
obvious sign of how they had met their death. 

“moreover, he told me one extraordinary tale about his retriever 
chase some invisible creature across the field one day when he was 
out with his gun. the dog sudden pointed at something in the field at 
his feet, and then gave chase, yelp like a mad thing. it follow 
its imaginary quarry to the border of the wood, and then went in--a 
thing he had never known it to do before. the moment it cross the 
edge--it is darkish in there even in daylight--it began fight 
in the most frenzied and terrific fashion. it made him afraid to 
interfere, he said. and at last, when the dog came out, hanging its 
tail down and panting, he found something like white hair stuck to its 
jaws, and brought it to show me. i tell you these detail because----” 

“they are important, believe me,” the doctor stop him. “and you have 
it still, this hair?” he asked. 

“it disappear in the oddest way,” the colonel explained. “it was 
curious look stuff, something like asbestos, and i sent it to be 
analyse by the local chemist. but either the man got wind of its 
origin, or else he didn’t like the look of it for some reason, because 
he return it to me and said it was neither animal, vegetable, nor 
mineral, so far as he could make out, and he didn’t wish to have 
anything to do with it. i put it away in paper, but a week later, on 
opening the package--it was gone! oh, the story are simply endless. i 
could tell you hundred all on the same lines.” 

“and personal experience of your own, colonel wragge?” ask john 
silence earnestly, his manner showing the greatest possible interest 
and sympathy. 

the soldier gave an almost imperceptible start. he look distinct 
uncomfortable. 

“nothing, i think,” he said slowly, “nothing--er--i should like to rely 
on. i mean nothing i have the right to speak of, perhaps--yet.” 

his mouth closed with a snap. dr. silence, after wait a little to 
see if he would add to his reply, did not seek to press him on the 
point. 

“well,” he resume presently, and as though he would speak 
contemptuously, yet dare not, “this sort of thing has gone on at 
interval ever since. it spread like wildfire, of course, mysterious 
chatter of this kind, and people began trespass all over the estate, 
coming to see the wood, and making themselves a general nuisance. 
notice of man-traps and spring-guns only seem to increase their 
persistence; and--think of it,” he snorted, “some local research 
society actually wrote and ask permission for one of their member 
to spend a night in the wood! bold fools, who didn’t write for 
leave, came and took away bit of bark from the tree and gave them 
to clairvoyants, who invent in their turn a further batch of tales. 
there was simply no end to it all.” 

“most distressing and annoying, i can well believe,” interpose the 
doctor. 

“then sudden the phenomena cease as mysterious as they had begun, 
and the interest flagged. the tale stopped. people got interested in 
something else. it all seem to die out. this was last july. i can 
tell you exactly, for i’ve kept a diary more or less of what happened.” 

“ah!” 

“but now, quite recently, within the past three weeks, it has all 
revive again with a rush--with a kind of furious attack, so to speak. 
it has really become unbearable. you may imagine what it means, and the 
general state of affairs, when i say that the possibility of leave 
has occur to me.” 

“incendiarism?” suggest dr. silence, half under his breath, but not 
so low that colonel wragge did not hear him. 

“by jove, sir, you take the very words out of my mouth!” exclaim 
the astonish man, glancing from the doctor to me and from me to the 
doctor, and rattling the money in his pocket as though some explanation 
of my friend’s divine powers were to be found that way. 

“it’s only that you are thinking very vividly,” the doctor said 
quietly, “and your thought form pictures in my mind before you utter 
them. it’s merely a little elementary thought-reading.” 

his intention, i saw, was not to perplex the good man, but to impress 
him with his powers so as to ensure obedience later. 

“good lord! i had no idea----” he did not finish the sentence, and 
dive again abrupt into his narrative. 

“i did not see anything myself, i must admit, but the story of 
independent eye-witnesses were to the effect that lines of light, like 
stream of thin fire, move through the wood and sometimes were seen 
to shoot out precisely as flame might shoot out--in the direction of 
this house. there,” he explained, in a loud voice that made me jump, 
point with a thick finger to the map, “where the westerly fringe of 
the plantation come up to the end of the lower lawn at the back of 
the house--where it links on to those dark patches, which are laurel 
shrubberies, running right up to the back premises--that’s where these 
lights were seen. they pass from the wood to the shrubberies, and 
in this way reach the house itself. like silent rockets, one man 
describe them, rapid as lightning and exceedingly bright.” 

“and this evidence you spoke of?” 

“they actually reach the side of the house. they’ve left a mark of 
scorching on the walls--the wall of the laundry building at the other 
end. you shall see ’em tomorrow.” he pointed to the map to indicate the 
spot, and then straighten himself and glare about the room as though 
he had said something no one could believe and expect contradiction. 

“scorched--just as the face were,” the doctor murmured, look 
significant at me. 

“scorched--yes,” repeated the colonel, failing to catch the rest of the 
sentence in his excitement. 

there was a prolonged silence in the room, in which i heard the 
gurgle of the oil in the lamp and the click of the coal and the 
heavy breathing of our host. the most unwelcome sensation were 
creep about my spine, and i wonder whether my companion would 
scorn me utterly if i ask to sleep on the sofa in his room. it was 
eleven o’clock, i saw by the clock on the mantelpiece. we had cross 
the divide line and were now well in the movement of the adventure. 
the fight between my interest and my dread became acute. but, even if 
turning back had been possible, i think the interest would have easily 
gain the day. 

“i have enemies, of course,” i heard the colonel’s rough voice break 
into the pause presently, “and have discharge a number of servants----” 

“it’s not that,” put in john silence briefly. 

“you think not? in a sense i am glad, and yet--there are some things 
that can be met and dealt with----” 

he left the sentence unfinished, and look down at the floor with 
an expression of grim severity that betray a momentary glimpse of 
character. this fight man loathe and abhor the thought of an 
enemy he could not see and come to grip with. presently he move over 
and sat down in the chair between us. something like a sigh escape 
him. dr. silence said nothing. 

“my sister, of course, is kept in ignorance, as far as possible, of all 
this,” he said disconnectedly, and as if talk to himself. “but even 
if she knew, she would find matter-of-fact explanations. i only wish i 
could. i’m sure they exist.” 

there came then an interval in the conversation that was very 
significant. it did not seem a real pause, or the silence real silence, 
for both men continue to think so rapid and strong that one almost 
imagine their thought clothe themselves in words in the air of the 
room. i was more than a little key up with the strange excitement 
of all i had heard, but what stimulate my nerves more than anything 
else was the obvious fact that the doctor was clearly upon the trail of 
discovery. in his mind at that moment, i believe, he had already solve 
the nature of this perplex psychic problem. his face was like a 
mask, and he employ the absolute minimum of gesture and words. all 
his energy were direct inwards, and by those incalculable method 
and process he had master with such infinite patience and study, i 
felt sure he was already in touch with the forces behind these singular 
phenomena and lay his deep plan for bring them into the open, 
and then effectively dealing with them. 

colonel wragge meanwhile grew more and more fidgety. from time to time 
he turn towards my companion, as though about to speak, yet always 
change his mind at the last moment. once he went over and open 
the door suddenly, apparently to see if any one were listen at 
the keyhole, for he disappear a moment between the two doors, and i 
then heard him open the outer one. he stood there for some seconds and 
made a noise as though he were sniff the air like a dog. then he 
closed both door cautious and came back to the fireplace. a strange 
excitement seem grow upon him. evidently he was try to make up 
his mind to say something that he found it difficult to say. and john 
silence, as i rightly judged, was wait patient for him to choose 
his own opportunity and his own way of saying it. at last he turn and 
face us, square his great shoulders, and stiffening perceptibly. 

dr. silence look up sympathetically. 

“your own experience help me most,” he observe quietly. 

“the fact is,” the colonel said, speak very low, “this past week 
there have been outbreak of fire in the house itself. three separate 
outbreaks--and all--in my sister’s room.” 

“yes,” the doctor said, as if this was just what he had expect to 
hear. 

“utterly unaccountable--all of them,” add the other, and then sat 
down. i began to understand something of the reason of his excitement. 
he was realise at last that the “natural” explanation he had held to 
all along was becoming impossible, and he hate it. it made him angry. 

“fortunately,” he went on, “she was out each time and do not know. 
but i have made her sleep now in a room on the ground floor.” 

“a wise precaution,” the doctor said simply. he ask one or two 
questions. the fire had start in the curtains--once by the window 
and once by the bed. the third time smoke had been discover by the 
maid coming from the cupboard, and it was found that miss wragge’s 
clothes hanging on the hook were smouldering. the doctor listen 
attentively, but made no comment. 

“and now can you tell me,” he said presently, “what your own feeling 
about it is--your general impression?” 

“it sound foolish to say so,” reply the soldier, after a moment’s 
hesitation, “but i feel exactly as i have often felt on active service 
in my indian campaigns: just as if the house and all in it were in a 
state of siege; as though a conceal enemy were encamp about us--in 
ambush somewhere.” he utter a soft nervous laugh. “as if the next 
sign of smoke would precipitate a panic--a dreadful panic.” 

the picture came before me of the night shadow the house, and the 
twist pine tree he had describe crowd about it, conceal some 
powerful enemy; and, glancing at the resolute face and figure of the 
old soldier, forced at length to his confession, i understood something 
of all he had been through before he sought the assistance of john 
silence. 

“and tomorrow, unless i am mistaken, is full moon,” said the doctor 
suddenly, watch the other’s face for the effect of his apparently 
careless words. 

colonel wragge gave an uncontrollable start, and his face for the first 
time show unmistakable pallor. 

“what in the world----?” he began, his lip quivering. 

“only that i am beginning to see light in this extraordinary affair,” 
return the other calmly, “and, if my theory is correct, each month 
when the moon is at the full should witness an increase in the activity 
of the phenomena.” 

“i don’t see the connection,” colonel wragge answer almost savagely, 
“but i am bound to say my diary bear you out.” he wore the most 
puzzle expression i have ever seen upon an honest face, but he 
abhor this additional corroboration of an explanation that perplexed 
him. 

“i confess,” he repeated; “i cannot see the connection.” 

“why should you?” said the doctor, with his first laugh that evening. 
he got up and hung the map upon the wall again. “but i do--because 
these things are my special study--and let me add that i have yet 
to come across a problem that is not natural, and has not a natural 
explanation. it’s merely a question of how much one knows--and admits.” 

colonel wragge eye him with a new and curious respect in his face. but 
his feelings were soothed. moreover, the doctor’s laugh and change of 
manner came as a relief to all, and broke the spell of grave suspense 
that had held us so long. we all rose and stretch our limbs, and took 
little walk about the room. 

“i am glad, dr. silence, if you will allow me to say so, that you are 
here,” he said simply, “very glad indeed. and now i fear i have kept 
you both up very late,” with a glance to include me, “for you must be 
tired, and ready for your beds. i have told you all there is to tell,” 
he added, “and tomorrow you must feel perfectly free to take any steps 
you think necessary.” 

the end was abrupt, yet natural, for there was nothing more to say, and 
neither of these men talk for mere talking’s sake. 

out in the cold and chilly hall he lit our candle and took us 
upstairs. the house was at rest and still, every one asleep. we move 
softly. through the window on the stairs we saw the moonlight fall 
across the lawn, throw deep shadows. the near pine tree were just 
visible in the distance, a wall of impenetrable blackness. 

our host came for a moment to our rooms to see that we had everything. 
he pointed to a coil of strong rope lie beside the window, fasten 
to the wall by means of an iron ring. evidently it had been recently 
put in. 

“i don’t think we shall need it,” dr. silence said, with a smile. 

“i trust not,” reply our host gravely. “i sleep quite close to you 
across the landing,” he whispered, point to his door, “and if 
you--if you want anything in the night you will know where to find me.” 

he wish us pleasant dream and disappear down the passage into his 
room, shading the candle with his big muscular hand from the draughts. 

john silence stop me a moment before i went. 

“you know what it is?” i asked, with an excitement that even overcame 
my weariness. 

“yes,” he said, “i’m almost sure. and you?” 

“not the smallest notion.” 

he look disappointed, but not half as disappointed as i felt. 

“egypt,” he whispered, “egypt!” 


ii 

nothing happen to disturb me in the night--nothing, that is, except a 
nightmare in which colonel wragge chase me amid thin streak of fire, 
and his sister always prevent my escape by sudden rising up out of 
the ground in her chair--dead. the deep bay of dogs woke me once, 
just before the dawn, it must have been, for i saw the window frame 
against the sky; there was a flash of lightning, too, i thought, as i 
turn over in bed. and it was warm, for october oppressive warm. 

it was after eleven o’clock when our host suggest go out with 
the guns, these, we understood, being a somewhat thin disguise for 
our true purpose. personally, i was glad to be in the open air, for 
the atmosphere of the house was heavy with presentiment. the sense 
of impending disaster hung over all. fear stalk the passages, and 
lurk in the corner of every room. it was a house haunted, but really 
haunted; not by some vague shadow of the dead, but by a definite 
though incalculable influence that was active alive, and dangerous. 
at the least smell of smoke the entire household quivered. an odour 
of burning, i was convinced, would paralyse all the inmates. for the 
servants, though professedly ignorant by the master’s unspoken orders, 
yet share the common dread; and the hideous uncertainty, join with 
this display of so spiteful and calculate a spirit of malignity, 
provided a kind of black doom that drape not only the walls, but also 
the mind of the people living within them. 

only the bright and cheerful vision of old miss wragge being pushed 
about the house in her noiseless chair, chat and nod brisk 
to every one she met, prevent us from give way entire to the 
depression which govern the majority. the sight of her was like a 
gleam of sunshine through the depths of some ill-omened wood, and just 
as we went out i saw her being wheel along by her attendant into the 
sunshine of the back lawn, and caught her cheery smile as she turn 
her head and wish us good sport. 

the morning was october at its best. sunshine glisten on the 
dew-drenched grass and on leaves turn golden-red. the dainty 
messenger of coming hoar-frost were already in the air, asearch for 
permanent winter quarters. from the wide moor that everywhere swept up 
against the sky, like a purple sea splash by the occasional grey of 
rocky clefts, there stole down the cool and perfume wind of the west. 
and the keen taste of the sea ran through all like a master-flavour, 
borne over the space perhaps by the seagull that cry and circle 
high in the air. 

but our host took little interest in this sparkle beauty, and had 
no thought of showing off the scenery of his property. his mind was 
otherwise intent, and, for that matter, so were our own. 

“those bleak moor and hill stretch unbroken for hours,” he said, with 
a sweep of the hand; “and over there, some four miles,” point in 
another direction, “lies s---- bay, a long, swampy inlet of the sea, 
haunt by myriad of seabirds. on the other side of the house are the 
plantation and pine-woods. i thought we would get the dogs and go 
first to the twelve acre wood i told you about last night. it’s quite 
near.” 

we found the dogs in the stable, and i recall the deep bay of the 
night when a fine bloodhound and two great dane leap out to greet 
us. singular companion for guns, i thought to myself, as we struck out 
across the field and the great creature bound and ran beside us, 
nose to ground. 

the conversation was scanty. john silence’s grave face did not 
encourage talk. he wore the expression i knew well--that look of 
earnest solicitude which meant that his whole being was deep absorb 
and preoccupied. frightened, i had never seen him, but anxious 
often--it always move me to witness it--and he was anxious now. 

“on the way back you shall see the laundry building,” colonel wragge 
observe shortly, for he, too, found little to say. “we shall attract 
less attention then.” 

yet not all the crisp beauty of the morning seem able to dispel the 
feelings of uneasy dread that gather increasingly about our mind as 
we went. 

in a very few minutes a clump of pine tree conceal the house from 
view, and we found ourselves on the outskirts of a densely-grown 
plantation of conifers. colonel wragge stop abruptly, and, produce 
a map from his pocket, explain once more very brief its position
