with regard to the house. he show how it ran up almost to the wall 
of the laundry building--though at the moment beyond our actual 
view--and pointed to the window of his sister’s bedroom where the 
fire had been. the room, now empty, look straight on to the wood. 
then, glancing nervous about him, and calling the dogs to heel, 
he propose that we should enter the plantation and make as thorough 
examination of it as we thought worth while. the dogs, he added, might 
perhaps be persuade to accompany us a little way--and he pointed to 
where they cower at his feet--but he doubt it. “neither voice 
nor whip will get them very far, i’m afraid,” he said. “i know by 
experience.” 

“if you have no objection,” reply dr. silence, with decision, and 
speak almost for the first time, “we will make our examination 
alone--mr. hubbard and myself. it will be best so.” 

his tone was absolutely final, and the colonel acquiesce so polite 
that even a less intuitive man than myself must have seen that he was 
genuine relieved. 

“you doubtless have good reasons,” he said. 

“merely that i wish to obtain my impression uncoloured. this delicate 
clue i am working on might be so easily blur by the thought-currents 
of another mind with strong preconceived ideas.” 

“perfectly. i understand,” rejoin the soldier, though with an 
expression of countenance that plainly contradict his words. “then i 
will wait here with the dogs; and we’ll have a look at the laundry on 
our way home.” 

i turn once to look back as we clamber over the low stone wall 
built by the late owner, and saw his straight, soldierly figure 
standing in the sunlit field watch us with a curious intent look 
on his face. there was something to me incongruous, yet distinct 
pathetic, in the man’s effort to meet all far-fetched explanation 
of the mystery with contempt, and at the same time in his stolid, 
unswerving investigate of it all. he nod at me and made a gesture 
of farewell with his hand. that picture of him, standing in the 
sunshine with his big dogs, steady watch us, remains with me to 
this day. 

dr. silence led the way in among the twist trunks, plant close 
together in serried ranks, and i follow sharp at his heels. the 
moment we were out of sight he turn and put down his gun against the 
roots of a big tree, and i did likewise. 

“we shall hardly want these cumbersome weapon of murder,” he observed, 
with a passing smile. 

“you are sure of your clue, then?” i ask at once, burst with 
curiosity, yet fear to betray it lest he should think me unworthy. 
his own method were so absolutely simple and untheatrical. 

“i am sure of my clue,” he answer gravely. “and i think we have come 
just in time. you shall know in due course. for the present--be content 
to follow and observe. and think steadily. the support of your mind 
will help me.” 

his voice had that quiet mastery in it which leads men to face death 
with a sort of happiness and pride. i would have follow him anywhere 
at that moment. at the same time his words convey a sense of dread 
seriousness. i caught the thrill of his confidence; but also, in this 
broad light of day, i felt the measure of alarm that lay behind. 

“you still have no strong impressions?” he asked. “nothing happen in 
the night, for instance? no vivid dreamings?” 

he look close for my answer, i was aware. 

“i slept almost an unbroken sleep. i was tremendous tired, you know, 
and, but for the oppressive heat----” 

“good! you still notice the heat, then,” he said to himself, rather 
than expect an answer. “and the lightning?” he added, “that 
lightning out of a clear sky--that flashing--did you notice _that_?” 

i answer truly that i thought i had seen a flash during a moment of 
wakefulness, and he then drew my attention to certain fact before 
moving on. 

“you remember the sensation of warmth when you put the letter to your 
forehead in the train; the heat generally in the house last evening, 
and, as you now mention, in the night. you heard, too, the colonel’s 
story about the appearances of fire in this wood and in the house 
itself, and the way his brother and the gamekeeper came to their death 
twenty years ago.” 

i nodded, wonder what in the world it all meant. 

“and you get no clue from these facts?” he asked, a trifle surprised. 

i search every corner of my mind and imagination for some inkling of 
his meaning, but was oblige to admit that i understood nothing so far. 

“never mind; you will later. and now,” he added, “we will go over the 
wood and see what we can find.” 

his words explain to me something of his method. we were to keep our 
mind alert and report to each other the least fancy that cross the 
picture-gallery of our thoughts. then, just as we started, he turn 
again to me with a final warning. 

“and, for your safety,” he said earnestly, “imagine _now_--and for that 
matter, imagine always until we leave this place--imagine with the 
utmost keenness, that you are surround by a shell that protect you. 
picture yourself inside a protective envelope, and build it up with the 
most intense imagination you can evoke. pour the whole force of your 
thought and will into it. believe vivid all through this adventure 
that such a shell, construct of your thought, will and imagination, 
surround you completely, and that nothing can pierce it to attack.” 

he spoke with dramatic conviction, gaze hard at me as though to 
enforce his meaning, and then move forward and began to pick his way 
over the rough, tussocky ground into the wood. and meanwhile, knowing 
the efficacy of his prescription, i adopt it to the best of my 
ability. 

the tree at once closed about us like the night. their branch met 
overhead in a continuous tangle, their stem crept close and closer, 
the bramble undergrowth thicken and multiplied. we tore our trousers, 
scratch our hands, and our eyes fill with fine dust that made it 
most difficult to avoid the clinging, prickly network of branch and 
creepers. coarse white grass that caught our feet like string grew here 
and there in patches. it crown the lump of peaty growth that stuck 
up like human heads, fantastic dressed, thrust up at us out of 
the ground with crest of dead hair. we stumble and flounder among 
them. it was hard going, and i could well conceive it impossible to 
find a way at all in the night-time. we jumped, when possible, from 
tussock to tussock, and it seem as though we were spring among 
head on a battlefield, and that this dead white grass conceal eyes 
that turn to stare as we passed. 

here and there the sunlight shot in with vivid spot of white light, 
dazzle the sight, but only making the surrounding gloom deep by 
contrast and on two occasion we pass dark circular place in the 
grass where fire had eaten their mark and left a ring of ashes. dr. 
silence pointed to them, but without comment and without pausing, and 
the sight of them woke in me a singular realisation of the dread that 
lay so far only just out of sight in this adventure. 

it was exhaust work, and heavy going. we kept close together. the 
warmth, too, was extraordinary. yet it did not seem the warmth of the 
body due to violent exertion, but rather an inner heat of the mind that 
laid glowing hand of fire upon the heart and set the brain in a kind 
of steady blaze. when my companion found himself too far in advance, 
he wait for me to come up. the place had evidently been untouched by 
hand of man, keeper, forester or sportsman, for many a year; and my 
thoughts, as we advanced painfully, were not unlike the state of the 
wood itself--dark, confused, full of a haunting wonder and the shadow 
of fear. 

by this time all sign of the open field behind us were hid. no 
single gleam penetrated. we might have been grope in the heart of 
some primeval forest. then, suddenly, the bramble and tussock and 
string-like grass came to an end; the tree open out; and the ground 
began to slope upwards towards a large central mound. we had reach 
the middle of the plantation, and before us stood the broken druid 
stone our host had mentioned. we walk easily up the little hill, 
between the sparse stems, and, rest upon one of the ivy-covered 
boulders, look round upon a comparatively open space, as large, 
perhaps, as a small london square. 

thinking of the ceremony and sacrifice this rough circle of 
prehistoric monolith might have witnessed, i look up into my 
companion’s face with an unspoken question. but he read my thought and 
shook his head. 

“our mystery has nothing to do with these dead symbols,” he said, 
“but with something perhaps even more ancient, and of another country 
altogether.” 

“egypt?” i said half under my breath, hopeless puzzled, but recall 
his words in my bedroom. 

he nodded. mental i still floundered, but he seem intense 
preoccupied and it was no time for ask questions; so while his words 
circle unintelligib in my mind i look round at the scene before 
me, glad of the opportunity to recover breath and some measure of 
composure. but hardly had i time to notice the twist and contort 
shape of many of the pine tree close at hand when dr. silence lean 
over and touched me on the shoulder. he pointed down the slope. and the 
look i saw in his eyes key up every nerve in my body to its utmost 
pitch. 

a thin, almost imperceptible column of blue smoke was rising among the 
tree some twenty yard away at the foot of the mound. it curl up and 
up, and disappear from sight among the tangle branch overhead. it 
was scarcely thick than the smoke from a small brand of burning wood. 

“protect yourself! imagine your shell strongly,” whisper the doctor 
sharply, “and follow me closely.” 

he rose at once and move swift down the slope towards the smoke, 
and i followed, afraid to remain alone. i heard the soft crunch of 
our steps on the pine needles. over his shoulder i watch the thin 
blue spiral, without once taking my eyes off it. i hardly know how 
to describe the peculiar sense of vague horror inspired in me by the 
sight of that streak of smoke pencil its way upwards among the 
dark trees. and the sensation of increase heat as we approach was 
phenomenal. it was like walking towards a glowing yet invisible fire. 

as we drew near his pace slackened. then he stop and pointed, and 
i saw a small circle of burnt grass upon the ground. the tussock were 
blacken and smouldering, and from the centre rose this line of smoke, 
pale, blue, steady. then i notice a movement of the atmosphere beside 
us, as if the warm air were rising and the cooler air rush in to 
take its place: a little centre of wind in the stillness. overhead the 
bough stir and tremble where the smoke disappeared. otherwise, not 
a tree sighed, not a sound made itself heard. the wood was still as a 
graveyard. a horrible idea came to me that the course of nature was 
about to change without warning, _had_ change a little already, that 
the sky would drop, or the surface of the earth crash inwards like a 
broken bubble. something, certainly, reach up to the citadel of my 
reason, cause its throne to shake. 

john silence move forward again. i could not see his face, but his 
attitude was plainly one of resolution, of muscle and mind ready for 
vigorous action. we were within ten feet of the blacken circle when 
the smoke of a sudden cease to rise, and vanished. the tail of the 
column disappear in the air above, and at the same instant it seem 
to me that the sensation of heat pass from my face, and the motion 
of the wind was gone. the calm spirit of the fresh october day resume 
command. 

side by side we advanced and examine the place. the grass was 
smouldering, the ground still hot. the circle of burn earth was a 
foot to a foot and a half in diameter. it look like an ordinary 
picnic fire-place. i bent down cautious to look, but in a second 
i sprang back with an involuntary cry of alarm, for, as the doctor 
stamp on the ashes to prevent them spreading, a sound of hiss rose 
from the spot as though he had kick a living creature. this hiss 
was faint audible in the air. it move past us, away towards the 
thick portion of the wood in the direction of our field, and in a 
second dr. silence had left the fire and start in pursuit. 

and then began the most extraordinary hunt of invisible i can ever 
conceive. 

he went fast even at the beginning, and, of course, it was perfectly 
obvious that he was following something. to judge by the poise of his 
head he kept his eyes steady at a certain level--just above the 
height of a man--and the consequence was he stumble a good deal over 
the roughness of the ground. the hiss sound had stopped. there was 
no sound of any kind, and what he saw to follow was utterly beyond me. 
i only know, that in mortal dread of being left behind, and with a 
biting curiosity to see whatever there was to be seen, i follow as 
quick as i could, and even then barely succeed in keeping up with 
him. 

and, as we went, the whole mad jumble of the colonel’s story ran 
through my brain, touching a sense of frightened laughter that was only 
held in check by the sight of this earnest, hurry figure before me. 
for john silence at work inspired me with a kind of awe. he look 
so diminutive among these giant twist trees, while yet i knew that 
his purpose and his knowledge were so great, and even in hurry he was 
dignified. the fancy that we were play some queer, exaggerate game 
together met the fact that we were two men dance upon the brink of 
some possible tragedy, and the mingle of the two emotion in my mind 
was both grotesque and terrifying. 

he never turn in his mad chase, but pushed rapid on, while i pant 
after him like a figure in some unreasoning nightmare. and, as i ran, 
it came upon me that he had been aware all the time, in his quiet, 
internal way, of many things that he had kept for his own secret 
consideration; he had been watching, waiting, plan from the very 
moment we enter the shade of the wood. by some inner, concentrated 
process of mind, dynamic if not actually magical, he had been in direct 
contact with the source of the whole adventure, the very essence of the 
real mystery. and now the forces were moving to a climax. something 
was about to happen, something important, something possibly dreadful. 
every nerve, every sense, every significant gesture of the plunge 
figure before me proclaim the fact just as surely as the skies, the 
winds, and the face of the earth tell the bird the time to migrate and 
warn the animal that danger lurk and they must move. 

in a few moments we reach the foot of the mound and enter the 
tangle undergrowth that lay between us and the sunlight of the field. 
here the difficulty of fast travel increase a hundredfold. there 
were bramble to dodge, low bough to dive under, and countless tree 
trunks close up to make a direct path impossible. yet dr. silence 
never seem to falter or hesitate. he went, diving, jumping, dodging, 
ducking, but ever in the same main direction, following a clean trail. 
twice i trip and fell, and both times, when i picked myself up 
again, i saw him ahead of me, still force a way like a dog after 
its quarry. and sometimes, like a dog, he stop and pointed--human 
point it was, psychic pointing,--and each time he stop to point i 
heard that faint high hiss in the air beyond us. the instinct of an 
infallible dowse possessed him, and he made no mistakes. 

at length, abruptly, i caught up with him, and found that we stood 
at the edge of the shallow pond colonel wragge had mention in his 
account the night before. it was long and narrow, fill with dark 
brown water, in which the tree were dim reflected. not a ripple 
stir its surface. 

“watch!” he cry out, as i came up. “it’s go to cross. it’s bound 
to betray itself. the water is its natural enemy, and we shall see the 
direction.” 

and, even as he spoke, a thin line like the track of a water-spider, 
shot swift across the shiny surface; there was a ghost of steam in 
the air above; and immediately i became aware of an odour of burning. 

dr. silence turn and shot a glance at me that made me think of 
lightning. i began to shake all over. 

“quick!” he cry with excitement, “to the trail again! we must run 
round. it’s go to the house!” 

the alarm in his voice quite terrify me. without a false step i 
dashed round the slippery bank and dive again at his heel into the 
sea of bush and tree trunks. we were now in the thick of the very 
dense belt that ran round the outer edge of the plantation, and the 
field was near; yet so dark was the tangle that it was some time before 
the first shaft of white sunlight became visible. the doctor now ran 
in zigzags. he was following something that dodge and double quite 
wonderfully, yet had begun, i fancied, to move more slow than before. 

“quick!” he cried. “in the light we shall lose it!” 

i still saw nothing, heard nothing, caught no suggestion of a trail; 
yet this man, guide by some interior divine that seem infallible, 
made no false turns, though how we fail to crash headlong into the 
tree has remain a mystery to me ever since. and then, with a sudden 
rush, we found ourselves on the skirt of the wood with the open field 
lie in bright sunshine before our eyes. 

“too late!” i heard him cry, a note of anguish in his voice. “it’s 
out--and, by god, it’s making for the house!” 

i saw the colonel standing in the field with his dogs where we had left 
him. he was bend double, peer into the wood where he heard us 
running, and he straighten up like a bent whip released. john silence 
dashed passed, calling him to follow. 

“we shall lose the trail in the light,” i heard him cry as he ran. “but 
quick! we may yet get there in time!” 

that wild rush across the open field, with the dogs at our heels, 
leap and barking, and the elderly colonel behind us running as 
though for his life, shall i ever forget it? though i had only vague 
idea of the meaning of it all, i put my best foot forward, and, being 
the youngest of the three, i reach the house an easy first. i drew 
up, panting, and turn to wait for the others. but, as i turned, 
something moving a little distance away caught my eye, and in that 
moment i swear i experienced the most overwhelming and singular shock 
of surprise and terror i have ever known, or can conceive as possible. 

for the front door was open, and the waist of the house being narrow, i 
could see through the hall into the dining-room beyond, and so out on 
to the back lawn, and there i saw no less a sight than the figure of 
miss wragge--running. even at that distance it was plain that she had 
seen me, and was coming fast towards me, running with the frantic gait 
of a terror-stricken woman. she had recover the use of her legs. 

her face was a livid grey, as of death itself, but the general 
expression was one of laughter, for her mouth was gaping, and her eyes, 
always bright shone with the light of a wild merriment that seem the 
merriment of a child, yet was singularly ghastly. and that very second, 
as she flee past me into her brother’s arms behind, i smelt again most 
unmistakable the odour of burning, and to this day the smell of smoke 
and fire can come very near to turning me sick with the memory of what 
i had seen. 

fast on her heels, too, came the terrify attendant, more mistress of 
herself, and able to speak--which the old lady could not do--but with a 
face almost, if not quite, as fearful. 

“we were down by the bush in the sun,”--she gasp and scream in 
reply to colonel wragge’s distracted questionings,--“i was wheeling the 
chair as usual when she shriek and leaped--i don’t know exactly--i 
was too frightened to see--oh, my god! she jump clean out of the 
chair--_and ran!_ there was a blast of hot air from the wood, and she 
hid her face and jumped. she didn’t make a sound--she didn’t cry out, 
or make a sound. she just ran.” 

but the nightmare horror of it all reach the break point a few 
minutes later, and while i was still standing in the hall temporary 
bereft of speech and movement; for while the doctor, the colonel and 
the attendant were half-way up the staircase, helping the faint 
woman to the privacy of her room, and all in a confuse group of dark 
figures, there sound a voice behind me, and i turn to see the 
butler, his face dripping with perspiration, his eyes start out of 
his head. 

“the laundry’s on fire!” he cried; “the laundry building’s a-caught!” 

i remember his odd expression “a-caught,” and wanting to laugh, but 
finding my face rigid and inflexible. 

“the devil’s about again, s’help me gawd!” he cried, in a voice thin 
with terror, running about in circles. 

and then the group on the stairs scattered as at the sound of a shot, 
and the colonel and dr. silence came down three steps at a time, 
leave the afflict miss wragge to the care of her single attendant. 

we were out across the front lawn in a moment and round the corner 
of the house, the colonel leading, silence and i at his heels, and 
the portly butler puff some distance in the rear, get more and 
more mixed in his address to god and the devil; and the moment we 
pass the stable and came into view of the laundry building, we saw a 
wicked-looking volume of smoke pour out of the narrow windows, and 
the frightened women-servants and groom running hither and thither, 
calling aloud as they ran. 

the arrival of the master restore order instantly, and this retired 
soldier, poor think perhaps, but capable man of action, had the 
matter in hand from the start. he issue orders like a martinet, and, 
almost before i could realise it, there were stream bucket on the 
scene and a line of men and women form between the building and the 
stable pump. 

“inside,” i heard john silence cry, and the colonel follow him 
through the door, while i was just quick enough at their heel to hear 
him add, “the smoke’s the worst part of it. there’s no fire yet, i 
think.” 

and, true enough, there was no fire. the interior was thick with smoke, 
but it speedy clear and not a single bucket was use upon the floor 
or walls. the air was stifling, the heat fearful. 

“there’s precious little to burn in here; it’s all stone,” the colonel 
exclaimed, coughing. but the doctor was point to the wooden cover 
of the great cauldron in which the clothes were washed, and we saw that 
these were smoulder and charred. and when we sprinkle half a bucket 
of water on them the surrounding brick hiss and fizz and sent up 
cloud of steam. through the open door and window this pass out with 
the rest of the smoke, and we three stood there on the brick floor 
staring at the spot and wondering, each in our own fashion, how in the 
name of natural law the place could have caught fire or smoke at all. 
and each was silent--myself from sheer incapacity and befuddlement, the 
colonel from the quiet pluck that face all things yet speak little, 
and john silence from the intense mental grapple with this latest 
manifestation of a profound problem that call for concentration of 
thought rather than for any words. 

there was really nothing to say. the fact were indisputable. 

colonel wragge was the first to utter. 

“my sister,” he said briefly, and move off. in the yard i heard him 
send the frightened servant about their business in an excellent 
matter-of-fact voice, scold some one roundly for making such a big 
fire and letting the flue get over-heated, and pay no heed to the 
stammer reply that no fire had been lit there for several days. then 
he dispatch a groom on horseback for the local doctor. 

then dr. silence turn and look at me. the absolute control he 
possessed, not only over the outward expression of emotion by gesture, 
change of colour, light in the eyes, and so forth, but also, as i well 
knew, over its very birth in his heart, the mask-like face of the dead 
he could assume at will, made it extremely difficult to know at any 
given moment what was at work in his inner consciousness. but now, when 
he turn and look at me, there was no sphinx-expression there, but 
rather the keen, triumphant face of a man who had solve a dangerous 
and complicated problem, and saw his way to a clean victory. 

“_now_ do you guess?” he ask quietly, as though it were the simplest 
matter in the world, and ignorance were impossible. 

i could only stare stupid and remain silent. he glance down at the 
char cauldron-lids, and trace a figure in the air with his finger. 
but i was too excited, or too mortified, or still too dazed, perhaps, 
to see what it was he outlined, or what it was he meant to convey. i 
could only go on staring and shake my puzzle head. 

“a fire-elemental,” he cried, “a fire-elemental of the most powerful 
and malignant kind----” 

“a what?” thunder the voice of colonel wragge behind us, have 
return sudden and overheard. 

“it’s a fire-elemental,” repeated dr. silence more calmly, but 
with a note of triumph in his voice he could not keep out, “and a 
fire-elemental enraged.” 

the light began to dawn in my mind at last. but the colonel--who had 
never heard the term before, and was besides feeling considerably 
worked-up for a plain man with all this mystery he knew not how to 
grapple with--the colonel stood, with the most dumfoundere look ever 
seen on a human countenance, and continue to roar, and stammer, and 
stare. 

“and why,” he began, savage with the desire to find something visible 
he could fight--“why, in the name of all the blazes----?” and then 
stop as john silence move up and took his arm. 

“there, my dear colonel wragge,” he said gently, “you touch the heart 
of the whole thing. you ask ‘why.’ that is precisely our problem.” he 
held the soldier’s eyes firm with his own. “and that, too, i think, 
we shall soon know. come and let us talk over a plan of action--that 
room with the double doors, perhaps.” 

the word “action” calm him a little, and he led the way, without 
further speech, back into the house, and down the long stone passage to 
the room where we had heard his story on the night of our arrival. 
i understood from the doctor’s glance that my presence would not make 
the interview easy for our host, and i went upstairs to my own 
room--shaking. 

but in the solitude of my room the vivid memory of the last hour 
revive so merciless that i began to feel i should never in my whole 
life lose the dreadful picture of miss wragge running--that dreadful 
human climax after all the non-human mystery in the wood--and i was not 
sorry when a servant knock at my door and said that colonel wragge 
would be glad if i would join them in the little smoking-room. 

“i think it is better you should be present,” was all colonel wragge 
said as i enter the room. i took the chair with my back to the 
window. there was still an hour before lunch, though i imagine that 
the usual division of the day hardly found a place in the thought of 
any one of us. 

the atmosphere of the room was what i might call electric. the colonel 
was positively bristling; he stood with his back to the fire, fingering 
an unlit black cigar, his face flushed, his being obviously rouse 
and ready for action. he hate this mystery. it was poisonous to his 
nature, and he long to meet something face to face--something he 
could gauge and fight. dr. silence, i notice at once, was sitting 
before the map of the estate which was spread upon a table. i knew 
by his expression the state of his mind. he was in the thick of it 
all, knew it, delight in it, and was working at high pressure. he 
recognise my presence with a lift eyelid, and the flash of the eye, 
contrast with his still and composure, told me volumes. 

“i was about to explain to our host brief what seem to me afoot in 
all this business,” he said without look up, “when he ask that you 
should join us so that we can all work together.” and, while signify 
my assent, i caught myself wonder what quality it was in the calm 
speech of this undemonstrative man that was so full of power, so 
charged with the strange, virile personality behind it, and that seem 
to inspire us with his own confidence as by a process of radiation. 

“mr. hubbard,” he went on gravely, turning to the soldier, “knows 
something of my methods, and in more than one--er--interesting 
situation has prove of assistance. what we want now”--and here he 
sudden got up and took his place on the mat beside the colonel, and 
look hard at him--“is men who have self-control, who are sure of 
themselves, whose mind at the critical moment will emit positive 
forces, instead of the waver and uncertain current due to negative 
feelings--due, for instance, to fear.” 

he look at us each in turn. colonel wragge move his feet farther 
apart, and square his shoulders; and i felt guilty but said nothing, 
conscious that my latent store of courage was being deliberate haul 
to the front. he was winding me up like a clock. 

“so that, in what is yet to come,” continue our leader, “each of us 
will contribute his share of power, and ensure success for my plan.” 

“i’m not afraid of anything i can _see_,” said the colonel bluntly. 

“i’m ready,” i heard myself say, as it were automatically, “for 
anything,” and then added, feeling the declaration was lame 
insufficient, “and everything.” 

dr. silence left the mat and began walking to and fro about the room, 
both hand plunge deep into the pocket of his shooting-jacket. 
tremendous vitality stream from him. i never took my eyes off the 
small, moving figure; small, yes,--and yet somehow making me think of 
a giant plot the destruction of worlds. and his manner was gentle, 
as always, soothe almost, and his words utter quiet without 
emphasis or emotion. most of what he said was addressed, though not too 
obviously, to the colonel. 

“the violence of this sudden attack,” he said softly, pace to and 
fro beneath the bookcase at the end of the room, “is due, of course, 
partly to the fact that to-night the moon is at the full”--here he 
glance at me for a moment--“and partly to the fact that we have all 
been so deliberate concentrate upon the matter. our thinking, our 
investigation, has stir it into unusual activity. i mean that the 
intelligent force behind these manifestation has realise that some 
one is busy about its destruction. and it is now on the defensive: 
more, it is aggressive.” 

“but ‘it’--what is ‘it’?” began the soldier, fuming. “what, in the name 
of all that’s dreadful, _is_ a fire-elemental?” 

“i cannot give you at this moment,” reply dr. silence, turning to 
him, but undisturbed by the interruption, “a lecture on the nature and 
history of magic, but can only say that an elemental is the active 
force behind the elements,--whether earth, air, water, _or fire_,--it 
is impersonal in its essential nature, but can be focused, personified, 
ensouled, so to say, by those who know how--by magicians, if you 
will--for certain purpose of their own, much in the same way that 
steam and electricity can be harness by the practical man of this 
century. 

“alone, these blind elemental energy can accomplish little, but 
govern and direct by the train will of a powerful manipulate 
they may become potent activity for good or evil. they are the basis 
of all magic, and it is the motive behind them that constitute the 
magic ‘black’ or ‘white’; they can be the vehicle of curse or of 
blessings, for a curse is nothing more than the thought of a violent 
will perpetuated. and in such cases--cases like this--the conscious, 
direct will of the mind that is use the elemental stand always 
behind the phenomena----” 

“you think that my brother----!” broke in the colonel, aghast. 

“has nothing whatever to do with it--directly. the fire-elemental that 
has here been torment you and your household was sent upon its 
mission long before you, or your family, or your ancestors, or even the 
nation you belong to--unless i am much mistaken--was even in existence. 
we will come to that a little later; after the experiment i propose to 
make we shall be more positive. at present i can only say we have to 
deal now, not only with the phenomenon of attack fire merely, but 
with the vindictive and enrage intelligence that is direct it from 
behind the scenes--vindictive and enraged,”--he repeated the words. 

“that explains----” began colonel wragge, seek furiously for words 
he could not find quick enough. 

“much,” said john silence, with a gesture to restrain him. 

he stop a moment in the middle of his walk, and a deep silence 
came down over the little room. through the window the sunlight 
seem less bright, the long line of dark hill less friendly, making 
me think of a vast wave towering to heaven and about to break and 
overwhelm us. something formidable had crept into the world about us. 
for, undoubtedly, there was a disquiet thought, holding terror as 
well as awe, in the picture his words conjure up: the conception of a 
human will reach its deathless hand, spiteful and destructive, down 
through the ages, to strike the living and afflict the innocent. 

“but what _is_ its object?” burst out the soldier, unable to restrain 
himself long in the silence. “why do it come from that plantation? 
and why should it attack us, or any one in particular?” question 
began to pour from him in a stream. 

“all in good time,” the doctor answer quietly, have let him run 
on for several minutes. “but i must first discover positively what, 
or who, it is that direct this particular fire-elemental. and, to 
do that, we must first”--he spoke with slow deliberation--“seek to 
capture--to confine by visibility--to limit its sphere in a concrete 
form.” 

“good heaven almighty!” exclaim the soldier, mix his words in his 
unfeigned surprise. 

“quite so,” pursue the other calmly; “for in so do i think we 
can release it from the purpose that bind it, restore it to its 
normal condition of latent fire, and also”--he lower his voice 
perceptibly--“also discover the face and form of the being that ensoul 
it.” 

“the man behind the gun!” cry the colonel, beginning to understand 
something, and leaning forward so as not to miss a single syllable. 

“i mean that in the last resort, before it returns to the womb of 
potential fire, it will probably assume the face and figure of its 
director, of the man of magical knowledge who originally bound it with 
his incantation and sent it forth upon its mission of centuries.” 

the soldier sat down and gasp openly in his face, breathing hard; but 
it was a very subdued voice that frame the question. 

“and how do you propose to make it visible? how capture and confine it? 
what d’ye mean, dr. john silence?” 

“by furnish it with the material for a form. by the process of 
materialise simply. once limited by dimensions, it will become 
slow, heavy, visible. we can then dissipate it. invisible fire, you 
see, is dangerous and incalculable; lock up in a form we can perhaps 
manage it. we must betray it--to its death.” 

“and this material?” we ask in the same breath, although i think i 
had already guessed. 

“not pleasant, but effective,” came the quiet reply; “the exhalation 
of freshly-spilled blood.” 

“not human blood!” cry colonel wragge, start up from his chair 
with a voice like an explosion. i thought his eyes would start from 
their sockets. 

the face of dr. silence relax in spite of himself, and his 
spontaneous little laugh brought a welcome though momentary relief. 

“the days of human sacrifice, i hope, will never come again,” he 
explained. “animal blood will answer the purpose, and we can make the 
experiment as pleasant as possible. only, the blood must be freshly 
spill and strong with the vital emanate that attract this peculiar 
class of elemental creature. perhaps--perhaps if some pig on the estate 
is ready for the market----” 

he turn to hide a smile; but the passing touch of comedy found 
no echo in the mind of our host, who did not understand how to 
change quick from one emotion to another. clearly he was debate 
many things laborious in his honest brain. but, in the end, the 
earnest and scientific disinterested of the doctor, whose 
influence over him was already very great, won the day, and he 
presently look up more calmly, and observe shortly that he thought 
perhaps the matter could be arranged. 

“there are other and pleasant methods,” dr. silence went on to 
explain, “but they require time and preparation, and things have gone 
much too far, in my opinion, to admit of delay. and the process need 
cause you no distress: we sit round the bowl and await results. nothing 
more. the emanate of blood--which, as levi says, is the first 
incarnation of the universal fluid--furnish the material out of which 
the creature of discarnate life, spirits if you prefer, can fashion 
themselves a temporary appearance. the process is old, and lie at 
the root of all blood sacrifice. it was known to the priest of baal, 
and it is known to the modern ecstasy dance who cut themselves to 
produce objective phantom who dance with them. and the least gifted 
clairvoyant could tell you that the form to be seen in the vicinity of 
slaughterhouses, or hover above the desert battlefield, are--well, 
simply beyond all description. i do not mean,” he added, notice the 
uneasy fidget of his host, “that anything in our laundry-experiment 
need appear to terrify us, for this case seem a comparatively simple 
one, and it is only the vindictive character of the intelligence 
direct this fire-elemental that cause anxiety and make for 
personal danger.” 

“it is curious,” said the colonel, with a sudden rush of words, 
drawing a deep breath, and as though speak of things distasteful 
to him, “that during my years among the hill tribe of northern india 
i came across--personally came across--instances of the sacrifice of 
blood to certain deity being stop suddenly, and all manner of 
disaster happening until they were resumed. fire broke out in the 
huts, and even on the clothes, of the natives--and--and i admit i 
have read, in the course of my studies,”--he made a gesture towards 
his books and heavy laden table,--“of the yezidi of syria evoke 
phantom by means of cutting their body with knives during their 
whirl dances--enormous globe of fire which turn into monstrous 
and terrible forms--and i remember an account somewhere, too, how the 
emaciate form and pallid countenance of the spectres, that appear 
to the emperor julian, claim to be the true immortals, and told him 
to renew the sacrifice of blood ‘for the fumes of which, since the 
establishment of christianity, they had been pining’--that these were 
in reality the phantom evoke by the rite of blood.” 

both dr. silence and myself listen in amazement, for this sudden 
speech was so unexpected, and betray so much more knowledge than we 
had either of us suspect in the old soldier. 

“then perhaps you have read, too,” said the doctor, “how the cosmic 
deity of savage races, elemental in their nature, have been kept 
alive through many age by these blood rites?” 

“no,” he answered; “that is new to me.” 

“in any case,” dr. silence added, “i am glad you are not wholly 
unfamiliar with the subject, for you will now bring more sympathy, and 
therefore more help, to our experiment. for, of course, in this case, 
we only want the blood to tempt the creature from its lair and enclose 
it in a form----” 

“i quite understand. and i only hesitate just now,” he went on, 
his words coming much more slowly, as though he felt he had already 
said too much, “because i wish to be quite sure it was no mere 
curiosity, but an actual sense of necessity that dictate this horrible 
experiment.” 

“it is your safety, and that of your household, and of your sister, 
that is at stake,” reply the doctor. “once i have _seen_, i hope to 
discover whence this elemental comes, and what its real purpose is.” 

colonel wragge signify his assent with a bow. 

“and the moon will help us,” the other said, “for it will be full in 
the early hour of the morning, and this kind of elemental-being is 
always most active at the period of full moon. hence, you see, the clue 
furnish by your diary.” 

so it was finally settled. colonel wragge would provide the material 
for the experiment, and we were to meet at midnight. how he would 
contrive at that hour--but that was his business. i only know we 
both realise that he would keep his word, and whether a pig die at 
midnight, or at noon, was after all perhaps only a question of the 
sleep and personal comfort of the executioner. 

“to-night, then, in the laundry,” said dr. silence finally, to clinch 
the plan; “we three alone--and at midnight, when the household is 
asleep and we shall be free from disturbance.” 

he exchange significant glance with our host, who, at that moment, 
was call away by the announcement that the family doctor had arrived, 
and was ready to see him in his sister’s room. 

for the remainder of the afternoon john silence disappeared. i had my 
suspicion that he made a secret visit to the plantation and also to 
the laundry building; but, in any case, we saw nothing of him, and 
he kept strict to himself. he was prepare for the night, i felt 
sure, but the nature of his preparation i could only guess. there was 
movement in his room, i heard, and an odour like incense hung about the 
door, and knowing that he regard rite as the vehicle of energies, 
my guess were probably not far wrong. 

colonel wragge, too, remain absent the greater part of the afternoon, 
and, deep afflicted, had scarcely left his sister’s bedside, but in 
response to my inquiry when we met for a moment at tea-time, he told me 
that although she had moments of attempt speech, her talk was quite 
incoherent and hysterical, and she was still quite unable to explain 
the nature of what she had seen. the doctor, he said, fear she had 
recover the use of her limbs, only to lose that of her memory, and 
perhaps even of her mind. 

“then the recovery of her legs, i trust, may be permanent, at any 
rate,” i ventured, finding it difficult to know what sympathy to offer. 
and he reply with a curious short laugh, “oh yes; about that there 
can be no doubt whatever.” 

and it was due merely to the chance of my overhear a fragment of 
conversation--unwillingly, of course--that a little further light was 
thrown upon the state in which the old lady actually lay. for, as i 
came out of my room, it happen that colonel wragge and the doctor 
were go downstairs together, and their words float up to my ear 
before i could make my presence known by so much as a cough. 

“then you must find a way,” the doctor was saying with decision; “for 
i cannot insist too strong upon that--and at all costs she must be 
kept quiet. these attempt to go out must be prevented--if necessary, 
by force. this desire to visit some wood or other she keeps talk 
about is, of course, hysterical in nature. it cannot be permit for a 
moment.” 

“it shall not be permitted,” i heard the soldier reply, as they reach 
the hall below. 

“it has impress her mind for some reason----” the doctor went on, by 
way evidently of soothe explanation, and then the distance made it 
impossible for me to hear more. 

at dinner dr. silence was still absent, on the public plea of a 
headache, and though food was sent to his room, i am inclined to 
believe he did not touch it, but spent the entire time fasting. 

we retired early, desire that the household should do likewise, and 
i must confess that at ten o’clock when i bid my host a temporary 
good-night, and sought my room to make what mental preparation i could, 
i realise in no very pleasant fashion that it was a singular and 
formidable assignation, this midnight meeting in the laundry building, 
and that there were moments in every adventure of life when a wise 
man, and one who knew his own limitations, owe it to his dignity 
to withdraw discreetly. and, but for the character of our leader, i 
probably should have then and there offer the best excuse i could 
think of, and have allow myself quiet to fall asleep and wait for 
an exciting story in the morning of what had happened. but with a man 
like john silence, such a lapse was out of the question, and i sat 
before my fire count the minutes and do everything i could think 
of to fortify my resolution and fasten my will at the point where i 
could be reasonably sure that my self-control would hold against all 
attack of men, devils, or elementals. 


iii 

at a quarter before midnight, clad in a heavy ulster, and with 
slipper feet, i crept cautious from my room and stole down the 
passage to the top of the stairs. outside the doctor’s door i wait 
a moment to listen. all was still; the house in utter darkness; no 
gleam of light beneath any door; only, down the length of the corridor, 
from the direction of the sick-room, came faint sound of laughter 
and incoherent talk that were not things to reassure a mind already 
half a-tremble, and i made haste to reach the hall and let myself out 
through the front door into the night. 

the air was keen and frosty, perfume with night smells, and 
exquisite fresh; all the million candle of the sky were alight, and 
a faint breeze rose and fell with far-away sigh in the tops of the 
pine trees. my blood leap for a moment in the spacious of the 
night, for the splendid stars brought courage; but the next instant, 
as i turn the corner of the house, moving stealthy down the gravel 
drive, my spirits sank again ominously. for, yonder, over the funereal 
plume of the twelve acre plantation, i saw the huge and yellow face 
of the full moon just rising in the east, staring down like some vast 
being come to watch upon the progress of our doom. seen through the 
distort vapours of the earth’s atmosphere, her face look weird 
unfamiliar, her usual expression of benignant vacancy somehow a-twist. 
i slip along by the shadow of the wall, keeping my eyes upon the 
ground. 

the laundry-house, as already described, stood detached from the other 
offices, with laurel shrubbery crowd thick behind it, and the 
kitchen-garden so close on the other side that the strong smell of 
soil and grow things came across almost heavily. the shadow of the 
haunt plantation, hugely lengthen by the rising moon behind them, 
reach to the very wall and cover the stone tile of the roof with 
a dark pall. so keen were my senses alert at this moment that i 
believe i could fill a chapter with the endless small detail of the 
impression i received--shadows, odour, shapes, sounds--in the space of 
the few seconds i stood and wait before the closed wooden door. 

then i became aware of some one moving towards me through the 
moonlight, and the figure of john silence, without overcoat and 
bareheaded, came quick and without noise to join me. his eyes, i saw 
at once, were wonderful bright, and so marked was the shining pallor 
of his face that i could hardly tell when he pass from the moonlight 
into the shade. 

he pass without a word, beckon me to follow, and then pushed the 
door open, and went in. 

the chill air of the place met us like that of an underground vault; 
and the brick floor and whitewash walls, streak with damp and 
smoke, threw back the cold in our faces. directly opposite gape the 
black throat of the huge open fireplace, the ashes of wood fire 
still pile and scattered about the hearth, and on either side of 
the project chimney-column were the deep recess holding the big 
twin cauldron for boil clothes. upon the lid of these cauldron 
stood the two little oil lamps, shade red, which gave all the light 
there was, and immediately in front of the fireplace there was a small 
circular table with three chair set about it. overhead, the narrow 
slit windows, high up the walls, pointed to a dim network of wooden 
rafter half lost among the shadows, and then came the dark vault of 
the roof. cheerless and unalluring, for all the red light, it certainly 
was, remind me of some unused conventicle, bare of pew or pulpit, 
ugly and severe, and i was forcibly struck by the contrast between the 
normal use to which the place was ordinarily put, and the strange and 
mediæval purpose which had brought us under its roof to-night. 

possibly an involuntary shudder ran over me, for my companion turn 
with a confident look to reassure me, and he was so completely master 
of himself that i at once absorb from his abundance, and felt the 
chink of my failing courage beginning to close up. to meet his eye in 
the presence of danger was like finding a mental railing that guide 
and support thought along the giddy edge of alarm. 

“i am quite ready,” i whispered, turning to listen for approach 
footsteps. 

he nodded, still keeping his eyes on mine. our whisper sound hollow 
as they echo overhead among the rafters. 

“i’m glad you are here,” he said. “not all would have the courage. 
keep your thought controlled, and imagine the protective shell round 
you--round your _inner_ being.” 

“i’m all right,” i repeated, curse my chatter teeth. 

he took my hand and shook it, and the contact seem to shake into me 
something of his supreme confidence. the eyes and hand of a strong man 
can touch the soul. i think he guess my thought, for a passing smile 
flash about the corner of his mouth. 

“you will feel more comfortable,” he said, in a low tone, “when 
the chain is complete. the colonel we can count on, of course. 
remember, though,” he add warningly, “he may perhaps become 
controlled--possessed--when the thing comes, because he won’t know how 
to resist. and to explain the business to such a man----!” he shrug 
his shoulder expressively. “but it will only be temporary, and i will 
see that no harm come to him.” 

he glance round at the arrangement with approval. 

“red light,” he said, indicate the shade lamps, “has the lowest rate 
of vibration. materialise are dissipated by strong light--won’t 
form, or hold together--in rapid vibrations.” 

i was not sure that i approve altogether of this dim light, for in 
complete dark there is something protective--the knowledge that one 
cannot be seen, probably--which a half-light destroys, but i remember 
the warning to keep my thought steady, and forbore to give them 
expression. 

there was a step outside, and the figure of colonel wragge stood in the 
doorway. though enter on tiptoe, he made considerable noise and 
clatter, for his free movements were impede by the burden he carried, 
and we saw a large yellowish bowl held out at arms’ length from his 
body, the mouth cover with a white cloth. his face, i noted, was 
rigid composed. he, too, was master of himself. and, as i thought of 
this old soldier moving through the long series of alarms, worn with 
watch and weary with assault, unenlightened yet undismayed, even 
down to the dreadful shock of his sister’s terror, and still showing 
the dogged pluck that persist in the face of defeat, i understood what 
dr. silence meant when he describe him as a man “to be count on.” 

i think there was nothing beyond this rigidity of his stern features, 
and a certain grey of the complexion, to betray the turmoil of the 
emotion that was doubtless go on within; and the quality of these 
two men, each in his own way, so key me up that, by the time the door 
was shut and we had exchange silent greetings, all the latent courage 
i possessed was well to the fore, and i felt as sure of myself as i 
knew i ever could feel. 

colonel wragge set the bowl careful in the centre of the table. 

“midnight,” he said shortly, glancing at his watch, and we all three 
move to our chairs. 

there, in the middle of that cold and silent place, we sat, with 
the vile bowl before us, and a thin, hardly perceptible steam 
rising through the damp air from the surface of the white cloth and 
disappear upwards the moment it pass beyond the zone of red light 
and enter the deep shadow thrown forward by the project wall of 
chimney. 

the doctor had indicate our respective places, and i found myself 
seat with my back to the door and opposite the black hearth. the 
colonel was on my left, and dr. silence on my right, both half facing 
me, the latter more in shadow than the former. we thus divide the 
little table into even sections, and sitting back in our chair we 
await event in silence. 

for something like an hour i do not think there was even the faintest 
sound within those four wall and under the canopy of that vaulted 
roof. our slipper made no scratch on the gritty floor, and our 
breathing was suppress almost to nothing; even the rustle of our 
clothes as we shift from time to time upon our seat was inaudible. 
silence smother us absolutely--the silence of night, of listening, 
the silence of a haunt expectancy. the very gurgle of the lamp was 
too soft to be heard, and if light itself had sound, i do not think we 
should have notice the silvery tread of the moonlight as it enter 
the high narrow window and threw upon the floor the slender trace of 
its pallid footsteps. 

colonel wragge and the doctor, and myself too for that matter, sat thus 
like figures of stone, without speech and without gesture. my eyes 
pass in ceaseless journey from the bowl to their faces, and from 
their face to the bowl. they might have been masks, however, for all 
the sign of life they gave; and the light steam from the horrid 
contents beneath the white cloth had long cease to be visible. 

then presently, as the moon rose higher, the wind rose with it. it 
sighed, like the lightest of passing wings, over the roof; it crept 
most soft round the walls; it made the brick floor like ice beneath 
our feet. with it i saw mental the desolate moorland flowing like 
a sea about the old house, the treeless expanse of lonely hills, the 
near copses, sombre, and mysterious in the night. _the_ plantation, 
too, in particular i saw, and imagine i heard the mournful whisper 
that must now be a-stirring among its tree-tops as the breeze play 
down between the twist stems. in the depth of the room behind us the 
shaft of moonlight met and cross in a grow network. 

it was after an hour of this wearing and unbroken attention, and i 
should judge about one o’clock in the morning, when the bay of 
the dogs in the stable-yard first began, and i saw john silence move 
sudden in his chair and sit up in an attitude of attention. every 
force in my being instantly leap into the keenest vigilance. colonel 
wragge move too, though slowly, and without raise his eyes from the 
table before him. 

the doctor stretch his arm out and took the white cloth from the bowl. 

it was perhaps imagination that persuade me the red glare of the lamp 
grew faint and the air over the table before us thickened. i had been 
expect something for so long that the movement of my companions, 
and the lift of the cloth, may easily have cause the momentary 
delusion that something hover in the air before my face, touching 
the skin of my cheek with a silken run. but it was certainly not a 
delusion that the colonel look up at the same moment and glance over 
his shoulder, as though his eyes follow the movements of something 
to and fro about the room, and that he then button his overcoat more 
tight about him and his eyes sought my own face first, and then the 
doctor’s. and it was no delusion that his face seem somehow to have 
turn dark, become spread as it were with a shadowy blackness. i saw 
his lip tighten and his expression grow hard and stern, and it came to 
me then with a rush that, of course, this man had told us but a part of 
the experience he had been through in the house, and that there was 
much more he had never been able to bring himself to reveal at all. 
i felt sure of it. the way he turn and stare about him betray a 
familiarity with other things than those he had describe to us. it was 
not merely a sight of fire he look for; it was a sight of something 
alive, intelligent, something able to evade his searching; it was _a 
person_. it was the watch for the ancient being who sought to obsess 
him. 

and the way in which dr. silence answer his look--though it was only 
by a glance of subtlest sympathy--confirmed my impression. 

“we may be ready now,” i heard him say in a whisper, and i understood 
that his words were intended as a steady warning, and brace myself 
mental to the utmost of my power. 

yet long before colonel wragge had turn to stare about the room, and 
long before the doctor had confirmed my impression that things were at 
last beginning to stir, i had become aware in most singular fashion 
that the place held more than our three selves. with the rising of the 
wind this increase to our numbers had first taken place. the bay of 
the hound almost seem to have signal it. i cannot say how it may 
be possible to realise that an empty place has sudden become--not 
empty, when the new arrival is nothing that appeal to any one of the 
senses; for this recognition of an “invisible,” as of the change in the 
balance of personal forces in a human group, is indefinable and beyond 
proof. yet it is unmistakable. and i knew perfectly well at what given 
moment the atmosphere within these four wall became charged with the 
presence of other living being besides ourselves. and, on reflection, 
i am convinced that both my companion knew it too. 

“watch the light,” said the doctor under his breath, and, then i knew 
too that it was no fancy of my own that had turn the air darker, and 
the way he turn to examine the face of our host sent an electric 
thrill of wonder and expectancy shiver along every nerve in my body. 

yet it was no kind of terror that i experienced, but rather a sort of 
mental dizziness, and a sensation as of being suspend in some remote 
and dreadful altitude where things might happen, indeed were about to 
happen, that had never before happen within the ken of man. horror 
may have form an ingredient, but it was not chiefly horror, and in no 
sense ghostly horror. 

uncommon thought kept beating on my brain like tiny hammers, soft yet 
persistent, seek admission; their unbidden tide began to wash along 
the far fringe of my mind, the current of unwonted sensation to rise 
over the remote frontier of my consciousness. i was aware of thoughts, 
and the fantasy of thoughts, that i never knew before existed. 
portion of my being stir that had never stir before, and things 
ancient and inexplicable rose to the surface and beckon me to follow. 
i felt as though i were about to fly off, at some immense tangent, 
into an outer space hitherto unknown even in dreams. and so singular 
was the result produce upon me that i was uncommonly glad to anchor my 
mind, as well as my eyes, upon the masterful personality of the doctor 
at my side, for there, i realised, i could draw always upon the forces 
of sanity and safety. 

with a vigorous effort of will i return to the scene before me, and 
try to focus my attention, with steady thoughts, upon the table, 
and upon the silent figures seat round it. and then i saw that 
certain change had come about in the place where we sat. 

the patch of moonlight on the floor, i noted, had become curious 
shaded; the face of my companion opposite were not so clearly 
visible as before; and the forehead and cheek of colonel wragge were 
glisten with perspiration. i realise further, that an extraordinary 
change had come about in the temperature of the atmosphere. the 
increase warmth had a painful effect, not alone on colonel wragge, but 
upon all of us. it was oppressive and unnatural. we gasp figurative 
as well as actually. 

“you are the first to feel it,” said dr. silence in low tones, look 
across at him. “you are in more intimate touch, of course----” 

the colonel was trembling, and appear to be in considerable distress. 
his knee shook, so that the shuffle of his slipper feet became 
audible. he inclined his head to show that he had heard, but made no 
other reply. i think, even then, he was sore put to it to keep himself 
in hand. i knew what he was struggle against. as dr. silence had 
warn me, he was about to be obsessed, and was savagely, though 
vainly, resisting. 

but, meanwhile, a curious and whirl sense of exhilarate began 
to come over me. the increase heat was delightful, bring a 
sensation of intense activity, of thought pour through the mind 
at high speed, of vivid pictures in the brain, of fierce desire and 
lightning energy alive in every part of the body. i was conscious of 
no physical distress, such as the colonel felt, but only of a vague 
feeling that it might all grow sudden too intense--that i might be 
consumed--that my personality as well as my body, might become resolve 
into the flame of pure spirit. i began to live at a speed too intense 
to last. it was as if a thousand ecstasy besiege me---- 

“steady!” whisper the voice of john silence in my ear, and i look 
up with a start to see that the colonel had risen from his chair. the 
doctor rose too. i follow suit, and for the first time saw down into 
the bowl. to my amaze and horror i saw that the contents were 
troubled. the blood was astir with movement. 

the rest of the experiment was witness by us standing. it came, too, 
with a curious suddenness. there was no more dreaming, for me at any 
rate. 

i shall never forget the figure of colonel wragge standing there beside 
me, upright and unshaken, square plant on his feet, look about 
him, puzzle beyond belief, yet full of a fight anger. frame by 
the white walls, the red glow of the lamp upon his stream cheeks, 
his eyes glowing against the deathly pallor of his skin, breathing 
hard and making convulsive effort of hand and body to keep himself 
under control, his whole being rouse to the point of savage fighting, 
yet with nothing visible to get at anywhere--he stood there, immovable 
against odds. and the strange contrast of the pale skin and the burning 
face i had never seen before, or wish to seen again. 

but what has left an even sharper impression on my memory was the 
black that then began crawl over his face, obliterate the 
features, conceal their human outline, and hiding him inch by 
inch from view. this was my first realisation that the process of 
materialise was at work. his visage became shrouded. i move from 
one side to the other to keep him in view, and it was only then i 
understood that, properly speaking, the black was not upon the 
countenance of colonel wragge, but that something had insert itself 
between me and him, thus screening his face with the effect of a dark 
veil. something that apparently rose through the floor was passing 
slow into the air above the table and above the bowl. the blood in 
the bowl, moreover, was considerably less than before. 

and, with this change in the air before us, there came at the same 
time a further change, i thought, in the face of the soldier. one-half 
was turn towards the red lamps, while the other caught the pale 
illumination of the moonlight fall aslant from the high windows, 
so that it was difficult to estimate this change with accuracy of 
detail. but it seem to me that, while the features--eyes, nose, 
mouth--remained the same, the life inform them had undergone some 
profound transformation. the signature of a new power had crept into 
the face and left its trace there--an expression dark, and in some 
unexplained way, terrible. 

then sudden he open his mouth and spoke, and the sound of this 
change voice, deep and musical though it was, made me cold and set 
my heart beating with uncomfortable rapidity. the being, as he had 
dreaded, was already in control of his brain, use his mouth. 

“i see a black like the black of egypt before my face,” said 
the tone of this unknown voice that seem half his own and half 
another’s. “and out of this dark they come, they come.” 

i gave a dreadful start. the doctor turn to look at me for an 
instant, and then turn to centre his attention upon the figure of our 
host, and i understood in some intuitive fashion that he was there to 
watch over the strangest contest man ever saw--to watch over and, if 
necessary, to protect. 

“he is being controlled--possessed,” he whisper to me through the 
shadows. his face wore a wonderful expression, half triumph, half 
admiration. 

even as colonel wragge spoke, it seem to me that this visible 
dark began to increase, pour up thick out of the ground by 
the hearth, rising up in sheet and veils, shroud our eyes and 
faces. it stole up from below--an awful black that seem to drink 
in all the radiation of light in the building, leave nothing but 
the ghost of a radiance in their place. then, out of this rising sea 
of shadows, issue a pale and spectral light that gradual spread 
itself about us, and from the heart of this light i saw the shape of 
fire crowd and gather. and these were not human shapes, or the shape 
of anything i recognise as alive in the world, but outline of fire 
that trace globes, triangles, crosses, and the luminous body of 
various geometric figures. they grew bright, faded, and then grew 
bright again with an effect almost of pulsation. they pass swift 
to and fro through the air, rising and falling, and particularly in 
the immediate neighbourhood of the colonel, often gathering about his 
head and shoulders, and even appear to settle upon him like giant 
insect of flame. they were accompanied, moreover, by a faint sound of 
hissing--the same sound we had heard that afternoon in the plantation. 

“the fire-elementals that precede their master,” the doctor said in an 
undertone. “be ready.” 

and while this weird display of the shape of fire alternate flash 
and faded, and the hiss echo faint among the dim rafter 
overhead, we heard the awful voice issue at interval from the lip of 
the afflict soldier. it was a voice of power, splendid in some way i 
cannot describe, and with a certain sense of majesty in its cadences, 
and, as i listen to it with quickly-beating heart, i could fancy it 
was some ancient voice of time itself, echo down immense corridor 
of stone, from the depths of vast temples, from the very heart of 
mountain tombs. 

“i have seen my divine father, osiris,” thunder the great tones. “i 
have scattered the gloom of the night. i have burst through the earth, 
and am one with the starry deities!” 

something grand came into the soldier’s face. he was staring fixedly 
before him, as though seeing nothing. 

“watch,” whisper dr. silence in my ear, and his whisper seem to 
come from very far away. 

again the mouth open and the awesome voice issue forth. 

“thoth,” it boomed, “has loosen the bandage of set which fetter my 
mouth. i have taken my place in the great wind of heaven.” 

i heard the little wind of night, with its mournful voice of ages, 
sigh round the wall and over the roof. 

“listen!” came from the doctor at my side, and the thunder of the voice 
continued-- 

“i have hidden myself with you, o ye stars that never diminish. i 
remember my name--in--the--house--of--fire!” 

the voice cease and the sound die away. something about the face and 
figure of colonel wragge relaxed, i thought. the terrible look pass 
from his face. the being that obsess him was gone. 

“the great ritual,” said dr. silence aside to me, very low, “the book 
of the dead. now it’s leave him. soon the blood will fashion it a 
body.” 

colonel wragge, who had stood absolutely motionless all this time, 
sudden swayed, so that i thought he was go to fall,--and, but for 
the quick support of the doctor’s arm, he probably would have fallen, 
for he stagger as in the beginning of collapse. 

“i am drunk with the wine of osiris,” he cried,--and it was half with 
his own voice this time--“but horus, the eternal watcher, is about my 
path--for--safety.” the voice dwindle and failed, die away into 
something almost like a cry of distress. 

“now, watch closely,” said dr. silence, speak loud, “for after the 
cry will come the fire!” 

i began to tremble involuntarily; an awful change had come without 
warning into the air; my leg grew weak as paper beneath my weight and 
i had to support myself by leaning on the table. colonel wragge, i 
saw, was also leaning forward with a kind of droop. the shape of fire 
had vanish all, but his face was lit by the red lamp and the pale 
shift moonlight rose behind him like mist. 

we were both gaze at the bowl, now almost empty; the colonel stoop 
so low i fear every minute he would lose his balance and drop into 
it; and the shadow, that had so long been in process of forming, now at 
length began to assume material outline in the air before us. 

then john silence move forward quickly. he took his place between us 
and the shadow. erect, formidable, absolute master of the situation, i 
saw him stand there, his face calm and almost smiling, and fire in his 
eyes. his protective influence was astound and incalculable. even 
the abhorrent dread i felt at the sight of the creature grow into 
life and substance before us, lessen in some way so that i was able 
to keep my eyes fixed on the air above the bowl without too vivid a 
terror. 

but as it took shape, rising out of nothing as it were, and grow 
momentarily more define in outline, a period of utter and wonderful 
silence settled down upon the building and all it contained. a hush of 
ages, like the sudden centre of peace at the heart of the travel 
cyclone, descended through the night, and out of this hush, as out of 
the emanate of the steam blood, issue the form of the ancient 
being who had first sent the elemental of fire upon its mission. it 
grew and darken and solidify before our eyes. it rose from just 
beyond the table so that the lower portion remain invisible, but i 
saw the outline limn itself upon the air, as though slow reveal by 
the rising of a curtain. it apparently had not then quite concentrated 
to the normal proportions, but was spread out on all side into space, 
huge, though rapid condensing, for i saw the colossal shoulders, the 
neck, the lower portion of the dark jaws, the terrible mouth, and then 
the teeth and lips--and, as the veil seem to lift further upon the 
tremendous face--i saw the nose and cheek bones. in another moment i 
should have look straight into the eyes---- 

but what dr. silence did at that moment was so unexpected, and took me 
so by surprise, that i have never yet properly understood its nature, 
and he has never yet seen fit to explain in detail to me. he utter 
some sound that had a note of command in it--and, in so doing, step 
forward and intervene between me and the face. the figure, just 
near completeness, he therefore hid from my sight--and i have always 
thought purposely hid from my sight. 

“the fire!” he cry out. “the fire! beware!” 

there was a sudden roar as of flame from the very mouth of the pit, 
and for the space of a single second all grew light as day. a blind 
flash pass across my face, and there was heat for an instant that 
seem to shrivel skin, and flesh, and bone. then came steps, and i 
heard colonel wragge utter a great cry, wild than any human cry i 
have ever known. the heat suck all the breath out of my lung with a 
rush, and the blaze of light, as it vanished, swept my vision with it 
into envelop darkness. 

when i recover the use of my senses a few moments later i saw that 
colonel wragge with a face of death, its white strange stained, 
had move close to me. dr. silence stood beside him, an expression of 
triumph and success in his eyes. the next minute the soldier try to 
clutch me with his hand. then he reeled, staggered, and, unable to save 
himself, fell with a great crash upon the brick floor. 

after the sheet of flame, a wind rage round the building as though it 
would lift the roof off, but then pass as sudden as it came. and in 
the intense calm that follow i saw that the form had vanished, and 
the doctor was stoop over colonel wragge upon the floor, try to 
lift him to a sitting position. 

“light,” he said quietly, “more light. take the shades off.” 

colonel wragge sat up and the glare of the unshaded lamp fell upon 
his face. it was grey and drawn, still running heat, and there was a 
look in the eyes and about the corner of the mouth that seem in this 
short space of time to have add years to its age. at the same time, 
the expression of effort and anxiety had left it. it show relief. 

“gone!” he said, look up at the doctor in a daze fashion, and 
struggle to his feet. “thank god! it’s gone at last.” he stare 
round the laundry as though to find out where he was. “did it control 
me--take possession of me? did i talk nonsense?” he ask bluntly. 
“after the heat came, i remember nothing----” 

“you’ll feel yourself again in a few minutes,” the doctor said. to my 
infinite horror i saw that he was surreptitious wipe sundry dark 
stain from the face. “our experiment has been a success and----” 

he gave me a swift glance to hide the bowl, standing between me and our 
host while i hurried stuff it down under the lid of the nearest 
cauldron. 

“----and none of us the worse for it,” he finished. 

“and fires?” he asked, still dazed, “there’ll be no more fires?” 

“it is dissipated--partly, at any rate,” reply dr. silence cautiously. 

“and the man behind the gun,” he went on, only half realise what he 
was saying, i think; “have you discover _that_?” 

“a form materialised,” said the doctor briefly. “i know for certain now 
what the direct intelligence was behind it all.” 

colonel wragge pull himself together and got upon his feet. the words 
convey no clear meaning to him yet. but his memory was return 
gradually, and he was try to piece together the fragment into a 
connected whole. he shiver a little, for the place had grown sudden 
chilly. the air was empty again, lifeless. 

“you feel all right again now,” dr. silence said, in the tone of a man 
state a fact rather than ask a question. 

“thanks to you--both, yes.” he drew a deep breath, and mop his face, 
and even attempt a smile. he made me think of a man coming from the 
battlefield with the stain of fight still upon him, but scornful of 
his wounds. then he turn grave towards the doctor with a question 
in his eyes. memory had return and he was himself again. 

“precisely what i expected,” the doctor said calmly; “a fire-elemental 
sent upon its mission in the days of thebes, century before christ, 
and to-night, for the first time all these thousand of years, release 
from the spell that originally bound it.” 

we stare at him in amazement, colonel wragge opening his lip for 
words that refuse to shape themselves. 

“and, if we dig,” he continue significantly, point to the floor 
where the black had pour up, “we shall find some underground 
connection--a tunnel most likely--leading to the twelve acre wood. it 
was made by--your predecessor.” 

“a tunnel made by my brother!” gasp the soldier. “then my sister 
should know--she live here with him----” he stop suddenly. 

john silence inclined his head slowly. “i think so,” he said quietly. 
“your brother, no doubt, was as much torment as you have been,” 
he continue after a pause in which colonel wragge seem deep 
preoccupied with his thoughts, “and try to find peace by bury it 
in the wood, and surrounding the wood then, like a large magic circle, 
with the enchantment of the old formulæ. so the stars the man saw 
blazing----” 

“but bury _what_?” ask the soldier faintly, step backwards 
towards the support of the wall. 

dr. silence regard us both intent for a moment before he replied. 
i think he weigh in his mind whether to tell us now, or when the 
investigate was absolutely complete. 

“the mummy,” he said softly, after a moment; “the mummy that your 
brother took from its resting-place of centuries, and brought 
home--here.” 

colonel wragge drop down upon the nearest chair, hanging 
breathless on every word. he was far too amaze for speech. 

“the mummy of some important person--a priest most likely--protected 
from disturbance and desecrate by the ceremonial magic of the time. 
for they understood how to attach to the mummy, to lock up with it in 
the tomb, an elemental force that would direct itself even after age 
upon any one who dare to molest it. in this case it was an elemental 
of fire.” 

dr. silence cross the floor and turn out the lamp one by one. he 
had nothing more to say for the moment. following his example, i fold 
the table together and took up the chairs, and our host, still daze 
and silent, mechanical obey him and move to the door. 

we remove all trace of the experiment, taking the empty bowl back to 
the house conceal beneath an ulster. 

the air was cool and fragrant as we walk to the house, the stars 
beginning to fade overhead and a fresh wind of early morning blow up 
out of the east where the sky was already hint of the coming day. it 
was after five o’clock. 

stealthy we enter the front hall and lock the door, and as we 
went on tiptoe upstairs to our rooms, the colonel, peer at us over 
his candle as he nod good-night, whisper that if we were ready 
the digging should be begun that very day. 

then i saw him steal along to his sister’s room and disappear. 


iv 

but not even the mysterious reference to the mummy, or the prospect of 
a revelation by digging, were able to hinder the reaction that follow 
the intense excitement of the past twelve hours, and i slept the sleep 
of the dead, dreamless and undisturbed. a touch on the shoulder woke 
me, and i saw dr. silence standing beside the bed, dress to go out. 

“come,” he said, “it’s tea-time. you’ve slept the best part of a dozen 
hours.” 

i sprang up and made a hurried toilet, while my companion sat and 
talked. he look fresh and rested, and his manner was even quiet 
than usual. 

“colonel wragge has provided spade and pickaxes. we’re go out to 
unearth this mummy at once,” he said; “and there’s no reason we should 
not get away by the morning train.” 

“i’m ready to go to-night, if you are,” i said honestly. 

but dr. silence shook his head. 

“i must see this through to the end,” he said gravely, and in a tone 
that made me think he still anticipate serious things, perhaps. he 
went on talk while i dressed. 

“this case is really typical of all story of mummy-haunting, and 
none of them are case to trifle with,” he explained, “for the mummy 
of important people--kings, priests, magicians--were laid away with 
profound significant ceremonial, and were very effectively protected, 
as you have seen, against desecration, and especially against 
destruction. 

“the general belief,” he went on, anticipate my questions, “held, 
of course, that the perpetuity of the mummy guarantee that of its 
ka,--the owner’s spirit,--but it is not improbable that the magical 
embalm was also use to retard reincarnation, the preservation of 
the body prevent the return of the spirit to the toil and discipline 
of earth-life; and, in any case, they knew how to attach powerful 
guardian-forces to keep off trespassers. and any one who dare to 
remove the mummy, or especially to unwind it--well,” he added, with 
meaning, “you have seen--and you _will_ see.” 

i caught his face in the mirror while i struggle with my collar. it 
was deep serious. there could be no question that he spoke of what he 
believe and knew. 

“the traveller-brother who brought it here must have been haunt too,” 
he continued, “for he try to banish it by burial in the wood, making 
a magic circle to enclose it. something of genuine ceremonial he must 
have known, for the stars the man saw were of course the remains of the 
still flaming pentagram he trace at interval in the circle. only he 
did not know enough, or possibly was ignorant that the mummy’s guardian 
was a fire-force. fire cannot be enclose by fire, though, as you saw, 
it can be release by it.”
