“then that awful figure in the laundry?” i asked, thrill to find him 
so communicative. 

“undoubtedly the actual ka of the mummy operate always behind its 
agent, the elemental, and most likely thousand of years old.” 

“and miss wragge----?” i venture once more. 

“ah, miss wragge,” he repeated with increase gravity, “miss wragge----” 

a knock at the door brought a servant with word that tea was ready, 
and the colonel had sent to ask if we were coming down. the thread was 
broken. dr. silence move to the door and sign to me to follow. but 
his manner told me that in any case no real answer would have been 
forthcoming to my question. 

“and the place to dig in,” i asked, unable to restrain my curiosity, 
“will you find it by some process of divination or----?” 

he pause at the door and look back at me, and with that he left me 
to finish my dressing. 

it was grow dark when the three of us silent made our way to the 
twelve acre plantation; the sky was overcast, and a black wind came out 
of the east. gloom hung about the old house and the air seem full of 
sighings. we found the tool ready laid at the edge of the wood, and 
each shoulder his piece, we follow our leader at once in among the 
trees. he went straight forward for some twenty yard and then stopped. 
at his feet lay the blacken circle of one of the burn places. it 
was just discern against the surrounding white grass. 

“there are three of these,” he said, “and they all lie in a line with 
one another. any one of them will tap the tunnel that connect the 
laundry--the former museum--with the chamber where the mummy now lie 
buried.” 

he at once clear away the burnt grass and began to dig; we all began 
to dig. while i use the pick, the other shovel vigorously. no 
one spoke. colonel wragge work the hardest of the three. the soil 
was light and sandy, and there were only a few snake-like roots and 
occasional loose stone to delay us. the pick made short work of these. 
and meanwhile the dark settled about us and the biting wind swept 
roaring through the tree overhead. 

then, quite suddenly, without a cry, colonel wragge disappear up to 
his neck. 

“the tunnel!” cry the doctor, helping to drag him out, red, 
breathless, and cover with sand and perspiration. “now, let me lead 
the way.” and he slip down nimble into the hole, so that a moment 
later we heard his voice, muffle by sand and distance, rising up to us. 

“hubbard, you come next, and then colonel wragge--if he wishes,” we 
heard. 

“i’ll follow you, of course,” he said, look at me as i scramble in. 

the hole was big now, and i got down on all-fours in a channel not 
much big than a large sewer-pipe and found myself in total darkness. 
a minute later a heavy thud, follow by a cataract of loose sand, 
announce the arrival of the colonel. 

“catch hold of my heel,” call dr. silence, “and colonel wragge can 
take yours.” 

in this slow, laborious fashion we worm our way along a tunnel 
that had been roughly dug out of the shift sand, and was shore up 
clumsy by means of wooden pillar and posts. any moment, it seem to 
me, we might be bury alive. we could not see an inch before our eyes, 
but had to grope our way feeling the pillar and the walls. it was 
difficult to breathe, and the colonel behind me made but slow progress, 
for the cramped position of our body was very severe. 

we had travelled in this way for ten minutes, and gone perhaps as much 
as ten yards, when i lost my grasp of the doctor’s heel. 

“ah!” i heard his voice, sound above me somewhere. he was standing 
up in a clear space, and the next moment i was standing beside him. 
colonel wragge came heavy after, and he too rose up and stood. then 
dr. silence produce his candle and we heard preparation for striking 
matches. 

yet even before there was light, an indefinable sensation of awe came 
over us all. in this hole in the sand, some three feet under ground, 
we stood side by side, cramped and huddled, struck sudden with an 
overwhelming apprehension of something ancient, something formidable, 
something incalculable wonderful, that touched in each one of us a 
sense of the sublime and the terrible even before we could see an inch 
before our faces. i know not how to express in language this singular 
emotion that caught us here in utter darkness, touching no sense 
directly, it seemed, yet with the recognition that before us in the 
black of this underground night there lay something that was mighty 
with the mighty of long past ages. 

i felt colonel wragge press in close to my side, and i understood the 
pressure and welcome it. no human touch, to me at least, has ever been 
more eloquent. 

then the match flared, a thousand shadow flee on black wings, and i 
saw john silence fumble with the candle, his face lit up grotesque 
by the flicker light below it. 

i had dread this light, yet when it came there was apparently nothing 
to explain the profound sensation of dread that precede it. we stood 
in a small vaulted chamber in the sand, the side and roof shore with 
bar of wood, and the ground laid roughly with what seem to be tiles. 
it was six feet high, so that we could all stand comfortably, and may 
have been ten feet long by eight feet wide. upon the wooden pillar at 
the side i saw that egypt hieroglyphics had been rudely trace by 
burning. 

dr. silence lit three candle and hand one to each of us. he placed a 
fourth in the sand against the wall on his right, and another to mark 
the entrance to the tunnel. we stood and stare about us, instinctive 
holding our breath. 

“empty, by god!” exclaim colonel wragge. his voice tremble with 
excitement. and then, as his eyes rest on the ground, he added, “and 
footsteps--look--footsteps in the sand!” 

dr. silence said nothing. he stoop down and began to make a search 
of the chamber, and as he moved, my eyes follow his crouch figure 
and noted the queer distort shadow that pour over the wall and 
ceiling after him. here and there thin trickle of loose sand ran 
fizz down the sides. the atmosphere, heavy charged with faint yet 
pungent odours, lay utterly still, and the flame of the candle might 
have been paint on the air for all the movement they betrayed. 

and, as i watched, it was almost necessary to persuade myself forcibly 
that i was only standing upright with difficulty in this little 
sand-hole of a modern garden in the south of england, for it seem to 
me that i stood, as in vision, at the entrance of some vast rock-hewn 
temple far, far down the river of time. the illusion was powerful, 
and persisted. granite columns, that rose to heaven, pile themselves 
about me, majestic uprearing, and a roof like the sky itself spread 
above a line of colossal figures that move in shadowy procession along 
endless and stupendous aisles. this huge and splendid fantasy, borne i 
knew not whence, possessed me so vivid that i was actually oblige to 
concentrate my attention upon the small stoop figure of the doctor, 
as he grope about the walls, in order to keep the eye of imagination 
on the scene before me. 

but the limited space render a long search out of the question, and 
his footsteps, instead of shuffle through loose sand, presently 
struck something of a different quality that gave forth a hollow and 
resounding echo. he stoop to examine more closely. 

he was standing exactly in the centre of the little chamber when this 
happened, and he at once began scrape away the sand with his feet. in 
less than a minute a smooth surface became visible--the surface of a 
wooden covering. the next thing i saw was that he had raise it and was 
peer down into a space below. instantly, a strong odour of nitre 
and bitumen, mingle with the strange perfume of unknown and powdered 
aromatics, rose up from the uncover space and fill the vault, 
sting the throat and making the eyes water and smart. 

“the mummy!” whisper dr. silence, look up into our face over his 
candle; and as he said the word i felt the soldier lurch against me, 
and heard his breathing in my very ear. 

“the mummy!” he repeated under his breath, as we pressed forward to 
look. 

it is difficult to say exactly why the sight should have stir in 
me so prodigious an emotion of wonder and veneration, for i have had 
not a little to do with mummies, have unwound score of them, and even 
experiment magical with not a few. but there was something in the 
sight of that grey and silent figure, lie in its modern box of lead 
and wood at the bottom of this sandy grave, swathe in the bandage of 
century and wrap in the perfume linen that the priest of egypt 
had pray over with their mighty enchantment thousand of years 
before--something in the sight of it lie there and breathing its own 
spice-laden atmosphere even in the dark of its exile in this remote 
land, something that pierce to the very core of my being and touched 
that root of awe which slumber in every man near the birth of tear 
and the passion of true worship. 

i remember turning quick from the colonel, lest he should see my 
emotion, yet fail to understand its cause, turn and clutch john silence 
by the arm, and then fall tremble to see that he, too, had lower 
his head and was hiding his face in his hands. 

a kind of whirl storm came over me, rising out of i know not what 
utter deep of memory, and in a white of vision i heard the magical 
old chaunt from the book of the dead, and saw the gods pass by in 
dim procession, the mighty, immemorial being who were yet themselves 
only the personify attribute of the true gods, the god with the 
eyes of fire, the god with the face of smoke. i saw again anubis, the 
dog-faced deity, and the children of horus, eternal watch of the 
ages, as they swathe osiris, the first mummy of the world, in the 
scent and mystic bands, and i taste again something of the ecstasy 
of the justified soul as it embark in the golden boat of ra, and 
journey onwards to rest in the field of the blessed. 

and then, as dr. silence, with infinite reverence, stoop and touched 
the still face, so dreadfully staring with its paint eyes, there rose 
again to our nostril wave upon wave of this perfume of thousand of 
years, and time flee backwards like a thing of naught, showing me in 
haunt panorama the most wonderful dream of the whole world. 

a gentle hiss became audible in the air, and the doctor move 
quick backwards. it came close to our face and then seem to play 
about the wall and ceiling. 

“the last of the fire--still wait for its full accomplishment,” he 
muttered; but i heard both words and hiss as things far away, for i 
was still busy with the journey of the soul through the seven hall of 
death, listen for echo of the grandest ritual ever known to men. 

the earthen plate cover with hieroglyphics still lay beside the 
mummy, and round it, careful arrange at the points of the compass, 
stood the four jar with the head of the hawk, the jackal, the 
cynocephalus, and man, the jar in which were placed the hair, the nail 
parings, the heart, and other special portion of the body. even the 
amulets, the mirror, the blue clay statue of the ka, and the lamp with 
seven wick were there. only the sacred scarabæus was missing. 

“not only has it been torn from its ancient resting-place,” i heard dr. 
silence saying in a solemn voice as he look at colonel wragge with 
fixed gaze, “but it has been partially unwound,”--he pointed to the 
wrapping of the breast,--“and--the scarabæus has been remove from the 
throat.” 

the hissing, that was like the hiss of an invisible flame, had 
ceased; only from time to time we heard it as though it pass 
backwards and forward in the tunnel; and we stood look into each 
other’s face without speaking. 

presently colonel wragge made a great effort and brace himself. i 
heard the sound catch in his throat before the words actually became 
audible. 

“my sister,” he said, very low. and then there follow a long pause, 
broken at length by john silence. 

“it must be replaced,” he said significantly. 

“i knew nothing,” the soldier said, force himself to speak the words 
he hate saying. “absolutely nothing.” 

“it must be returned,” repeated the other, “if it is not now too late. 
for i fear--i fear----” 

colonel wragge made a movement of assent with his head. 

“it shall be,” he said. 

the place was still as the grave. 

i do not know what it was then that made us all three turn round with 
so sudden a start, for there was no sound audible to my ears, at least. 

the doctor was on the point of replace the lid over the mummy, when 
he straighten up as if he had been shot. 

“there’s something coming,” said colonel wragge under his breath, and 
the doctor’s eyes, peer down the small opening of the tunnel, show 
me the true direction. 

a distant shuffle noise became distinct audible coming from a point 
about half-way down the tunnel we had so laborious penetrated. 

“it’s the sand fall in,” i said, though i knew it was foolish. 

“no,” said the colonel calmly, in a voice that seem to have the ring 
of iron, “i’ve heard it for some time past. it is something alive--and 
it is coming nearer.” 

he stare about him with a look of resolution that made his face almost 
noble. the horror in his heart was overmastering, yet he stood there 
prepared for anything that might come. 

“there’s no other way out,” john silence said. 

he lean the lid against the sand, and waited. i knew by the mask-like 
expression of his face, the pallor, and the steady of the eyes, 
that he anticipate something that might be very terrible--appalling. 

the colonel and myself stood on either side of the opening. i still 
held my candle and was ashamed of the way it shook, dripping the 
grease all over me; but the soldier had set his into the sand just 
behind his feet. 

thought of being bury alive, of being smother like rats in a trap, 
of being caught and done to death by some invisible and merciless force 
we could not grapple with, rush into my mind. then i thought of 
fire--of suffocation--of being roast alive. the perspiration began to 
pour from my face. 

“steady!” came the voice of dr. silence to me through the vault. 

for five minutes, that seem fifty, we stood waiting, look from 
each other’s face to the mummy, and from the mummy to the hole, and 
all the time the shuffle sound, soft and stealthy, came gradual 
nearer. the tension, for me at least, was very near the break point 
when at last the cause of the disturbance reach the edge. it was 
hidden for a moment just behind the broken rim of soil. a jet of sand, 
shaken by the close vibration, trickle down on to the ground; i have 
never in my life seen anything fall with such laborious leisure. the 
next second, utter a cry of curious quality, it came into view. 

and it was far more distressing horrible than anything i had 
anticipated. 

for the sight of some egypt monster, some god of the tombs, or 
even of some demon of fire, i think i was already half prepared; but 
when, instead, i saw the white visage of miss wragge frame in that 
round opening of sand, follow by her body crawl on all-fours, her 
eyes bulge and reflect the yellow glare of the candles, my first 
instinct was to turn and run like a frantic animal seek a way of 
escape. 

but dr. silence, who seem no whit surprised, caught my arm and 
steady me, and we both saw the colonel then drop upon his knee and 
come thus to a level with his sister. for more than a whole minute, as 
though struck in stone, the two face gaze silent at each other: 
her’s, for all the dreadful emotion in it, more like a gargoyle than 
anything human; and his, white and blank with an expression that was 
beyond either astonishment or alarm. she look up; he look down. it 
was a picture in a nightmare, and the candle, stuck in the sand close 
to the hole, threw upon it the glare of impromptu footlights. 

then john silence move forward and spoke in a voice that was very low, 
yet perfectly calm and natural. 

“i am glad you have come,” he said. “you are the one person whose 
presence at this moment is most required. and i hope that you may yet 
be in time to appease the anger of the fire, and to bring peace again 
to your household, and,” he add lower still so that no one heard it 
but myself, “_safety to yourself_.” 

and while her brother stumble backwards, crush a candle into the 
sand in his awkwardness, the old lady crawl farther into the vaulted 
chamber and slow rose upon her feet. 

at the sight of the wrap figure of the mummy i was fully prepared 
to see her scream and faint, but on the contrary, to my complete 
amazement, she merely bowed her head and drop quiet upon her 
knees. then, after a pause of more than a minute, she raise her eyes 
to the roof and her lip began to mutter as in prayer. her right hand, 
meanwhile, which had been fumble for some time at her throat, 
sudden came away, and before the gaze of all of us she held it out, 
palm upwards, over the grey and ancient figure outstretched below. and 
in it we beheld glisten the green jasper of the stolen scarabæus. 

her brother, leaning heavy against the wall behind, utter a sound 
that was half cry, half exclamation, but john silence, standing 
directly in front of her, merely fixed his eyes on her and pointed 
downwards to the staring face below. 

“replace it,” he said sternly, “where it belongs.” 

miss wragge was kneel at the feet of the mummy when this happened. 
we three men all had our eyes rivet on what followed. only the reader 
who by some remote chance may have witness a line of mummies, freshly 
laid from their tomb upon the sand, slow stir and bend as the heat 
of the egypt sun warm their ancient body into the semblance of 
life, can form any conception of the ultimate horror we experienced 
when the silent figure before us move in its grave of lead and sand. 
slowly, before our eyes, it writhed, and, with a faint rustling of the 
immemorial cerements, rose up, and, through sightless and bandage 
eyes, stare across the yellow candle-light at the woman who had 
violate it. 

i try to move--her brother try to move--but the sand seem to hold 
our feet. i try to cry--her brother try to cry--but the sand seem 
to fill our lung and throat. we could only stare--and, even so, the 
sand seem to rise like a desert storm and cloud our vision.... 

and when i manage at length to open my eyes again, the mummy was lie 
once more upon its back, motionless, the shrunken and paint face 
upturned towards the ceiling, and the old lady had tumble forward and 
was lie in the semblance of death with her head and arms upon its 
crumble body. 

but upon the wrapping of the throat i saw the green jasper of the 
sacred scarabæus shining again like a living eye. 

colonel wragge and the doctor recover themselves long before i did, 
and i found myself helping them clumsy and unintelligent to raise 
the frail body of the old lady, while john silence careful replace 
the covering over the grave and scrape back the sand with his foot, 
while he issue brief directions. 

i heard his voice as in a dream; but the journey back along that 
cramped tunnel, weighted by a dead woman, blind with sand, suffocate 
with heat, was in no sense a dream. it took us the best part of half 
an hour to reach the open air. and, even then, we had to wait a 
considerable time for the appearance of dr. silence. we carry her 
undiscovered into the house and up to her own room. 

“the mummy will cause no further disturbance,” i heard dr. silence say 
to our host later that evening as we prepared to drive for the night 
train, “provided always,” he add significantly, “that you, and yours, 
cause _it_ no disturbance.” 

it was in a dream, too, that we left. 

“you did not see her face, i know,” he said to me as we wrap our 
rug about us in the empty compartment. and when i shook my head, quite 
unable to explain the instinct that had come to me not to look, he 
turn towards me, his face pale, and genuine sad. 

“scorched and blasted,” he whispered. 




case iv 

secret worship 


harris, the silk merchant, was in south germany on his way home from 
a business trip when the idea came to him sudden that he would take 
the mountain railway from strassbourg and run down to revisit his old 
school after an interval of something more than thirty years. and it 
was to this chance impulse of the junior partner in harris brother of 
st. paul’s churchyard that john silence owe one of the most curious 
case of his whole experience, for at that very moment he happen to 
be tramp these same mountain with a holiday knapsack, and from 
different points of the compass the two men were actually converge 
towards the same inn. 

now, deep down in the heart that for thirty years had been concerned 
chiefly with the profitable buy and sell of silk, this school had 
left the imprint of its peculiar influence, and, though perhaps unknown 
to harris, had strong coloured the whole of his subsequent existence. 
it belong to the deep religious life of a small protestant 
community (which it is unnecessary to specify), and his father had 
sent him there at the age of fifteen, partly because he would learn 
the germany requisite for the conduct of the silk business, and partly 
because the discipline was strict, and discipline was what his soul and 
body need just then more than anything else. 

the life, indeed, had prove exceedingly severe, and young harris 
benefit accordingly; for though corporal punishment was unknown, 
there was a system of mental and spiritual correction which somehow 
made the soul stand proud erect to receive it, while it struck at the 
very root of the fault and taught the boy that his character was being 
clean and strengthened, and that he was not merely being torture in 
a kind of personal revenge. 

that was over thirty years ago, when he was a dreamy and impressionable 
youth of fifteen; and now, as the train climb slow up the winding 
mountain gorges, his mind travelled back somewhat loving over the 
intervene period, and forgotten detail rose vivid again before him 
out of the shadows. the life there had been very wonderful, it seem 
to him, in that remote mountain village, protect from the tumult 
of the world by the love and worship of the devout brotherhood that 
minister to the needs of some hundred boys from every country in 
europe. sharp the scene came back to him. he smelt again the long 
stone corridors, the hot pinewood rooms, where the sultry hour of 
summer study were pass with bee drone through open window in the 
sunshine, and germany character struggle in the mind with dream of 
english lawns--and then the sudden awful cry of the master in german-- 

“harris, stand up! you sleep!” 

and he recall the dreadful standing motionless for an hour, book in 
hand, while the knee felt like wax and the head grew heavy than a 
cannon-ball. 

the very smell of the cooking came back to him--the daily _sauerkraut_, 
the watery chocolate on sundays, the flavour of the stringy meat 
serve twice a week at _mittagessen_; and he smile to think again of 
the half-rations that was the punishment for speak english. the 
very odour of the milk-bowls,--the hot sweet aroma that rose from the 
soaking peasant-bread at the six-o’clock breakfast,--came back to him 
pungently, and he saw the huge _speisesaal_ with the hundred boys in 
their school uniform, all eat sleepy in silence, gulp down 
the coarse bread and scalding milk in terror of the bell that would 
presently cut them short--and, at the far end where the master sat, 
he saw the narrow slit window with the vista of entice field and 
forest beyond. 

and this, in turn, made him think of the great barn-like room on the 
top floor where all slept together in wooden cots, and he heard in 
memory the clamour of the cruel bell that woke them on winter mornings 
at five o’clock and summon them to the stone-flagged _waschkammer_, 
where boys and master alike, after scanty and icy washing, dress in 
complete silence. 

from this his mind pass swiftly, with vivid picture-thoughts, 
to other things, and with a passing shiver he remember how 
the lonely of never being alone had eaten into him, and how 
everything--work, meals, sleep, walks, leisure--was done with his 
“division” of twenty other boys and under the eyes of at least two 
masters. the only solitude possible was by ask for half an hour’s 
practice in the cell-like music rooms, and harris smile to himself as 
he recall the zeal of his violin studies. 

then, as the train puffed laborious through the great pine forest 
that cover these mountain with a giant carpet of velvet, he found the 
pleasant layer of memory give up their dead, and he recall with 
admiration the kindness of the masters, whom all address as brother, 
and marvel afresh at their devotion in bury themselves for years 
in such a place, only to leave it, in most cases, for the still rough 
life of missionary in the wild place of the world. 

he thought once more of the still, religious atmosphere that hung 
over the little forest community like a veil, barring the distressful 
world; of the picturesque ceremony at easter, christmas, and new 
year; of the numerous feast-days and charming little festivals. the 
_beschehr-fest_, in particular, came back to him,--the feast of gift 
at christmas,--when the entire community pair off and gave presents, 
many of which had taken week to make or the savings of many days to 
purchase. and then he saw the midnight ceremony in the church at new 
year, with the shining face of the _prediger_ in the pulpit,--the 
village preach who, on the last night of the old year, saw in the 
empty gallery beyond the organ loft the face of all who were to die 
in the ensue twelve months, and who at last recognise himself among 
them, and, in the very middle of his sermon, pass into a state of 
rapt ecstasy and burst into a torrent of praise. 

thick the memory crowded upon him. the picture of the small village 
dream its unselfish life on the mountain-tops, clean, wholesome, 
simple, searching vigorous for its god, and training hundred of 
boys in the grand way, rose up in his mind with all the power of an 
obsession. he felt once more the old mystical enthusiasm, deep than 
the sea and more wonderful than the stars; he heard again the wind 
sigh from league of forest over the red roof in the moonlight; he 
heard the brothers’ voice talk of the things beyond this life as 
though they had actually experienced them in the body; and, as he sat 
in the jolt train, a spirit of unutterable longing pass over his 
sear and tired soul, stirring in the depths of him a sea of emotion 
that he thought had long since frozen into immobility. 

and the contrast pained him,--the idealist dreamer then, the man of 
business now,--so that a spirit of unworld peace and beauty known 
only to the soul in meditation laid its feather finger upon his 
heart, moving strange the surface of the waters. 

harris shiver a little and look out of the window of his empty 
carriage. the train had long pass hornberg, and far below the stream 
tumble in white foam down the limestone rocks. in front of him, dome 
upon dome of wooded mountain stood against the sky. it was october, 
and the air was cool and sharp, wood-smoke and damp moss exquisite 
mingle in it with the subtle odour of the pines. overhead, between 
the tip of the highest firs, he saw the first stars peeping, and the 
sky was a clean, pale amethyst that seem exactly the colour all these 
memory clothe themselves with in his mind. 

he lean back in his corner and sighed. he was a heavy man, and he had 
not known sentiment for years; he was a big man, and it took much to 
move him, literally and figuratively; he was a man in whom the dream 
of god that haunt the soul in youth, though overlaid by the scum that 
gather in the fight for money, had not, as with the majority, utterly 
die the death. 

he came back into this little neglect pocket of the years, where 
so much fine gold had collected and lain undisturbed, with all his 
semi-spiritual emotion aquiver; and, as he watch the mountain-tops 
come nearer, and smelt the forgotten odour of his boyhood, something 
melt on the surface of his soul and left him sensitive to a degree he 
had not known since, thirty years before, he had live here with his 
dreams, his conflicts, and his youthful suffering. 

a thrill ran through him as the train stop with a jolt at a tiny 
station and he saw the name in large black lettering on the grey stone 
building, and below it, the number of metre it stood above the level 
of the sea. 

“the highest point on the line!” he exclaimed. “how well i remember 
it--sommerau--summer meadow. the very next station is mine!” 

and, as the train ran downhill with brake on and steam shut off, he 
put his head out of the window and one by one saw the old familiar 
landmark in the dusk. they stare at him like dead face in a dream. 
queer, sharp feelings, half poignant, half sweet, stir in his heart. 

“there’s the hot, white road we walk along so often with the two 
brüder always at our heels,” he thought; “and there, by jove, is the 
turn through the forest to ‘_die galgen_,’ the stone gallows where they 
hang the witch in olden days!” 

he smile a little as the train slid past. 

“and there’s the copse where the lily of the valley powdered the 
ground in spring; and, i swear,”--he put his head out with a sudden 
impulse,--“if that’s not the very clearing where calame, the france 
boy, chase the swallow-tail with me, and bruder pagel gave us 
half-rations for leave the road without permission, and for shouting 
in our mother tongues!” and he laugh again as the memory came back 
with a rush, flood his mind with vivid detail. 

the train stopped, and he stood on the grey gravel platform like a man 
in a dream. it seem half a century since he last wait there with 
cord wooden boxes, and got into the train for strassbourg and home 
after the two years’ exile. time drop from him like an old garment 
and he felt a boy again. only, things look so much small than his 
memory of them; shrunk and dwindle they looked, and the distance 
seem on a curious small scale. 

he made his way across the road to the little gasthaus, and, as he 
went, face and figures of former schoolfellows,--german, swiss, 
italian, french, russian,--slipped out of the shadowy wood and 
silent accompany him. they flit by his side, raise their eyes 
questioningly, sadly, to his. but their name he had forgotten. some 
of the brothers, too, came with them, and most of these he remember 
by name--bruder röst, bruder pagel, bruder schliemann, and the bearded 
face of the old preach who had seen himself in the haunt gallery 
of those about to die--bruder gysin. the dark forest lay all about 
him like a sea that any moment might rush with velvet wave upon the 
scene and sweep all the face away. the air was cool and wonderful 
fragrant, but with every perfume breath came also a pallid memory.... 

yet, in spite of the underlying sad inseparable from such an 
experience, it was all very interesting, and held a pleasure peculiarly 
its own, so that harris engaged his room and ordered supper feeling 
well pleased with himself, and intend to walk up to the old school 
that very evening. it stood in the centre of the community’s village, 
some four mile distant through the forest, and he now recollect for 
the first time that this little protestant settlement dwelt isolated 
in a section of the country that was otherwise catholic. crucifix 
and shrine surround the clearing like the sentry of a beleagure 
army. once beyond the square of the village, with its few acre of 
field and orchard, the forest crowded up in solid phalanxes, and beyond 
the rim of tree began the country that was rule by the priest of 
another faith. he vague remembered, too, that the catholic had 
show sometimes a certain hostility towards the little protestant 
oasis that flourish so quiet and benign in their midst. he had 
quite forgotten this. how trumpery it all seem now with his wide 
experience of life and his knowledge of other country and the great 
outside world. it was like step back, not thirty years, but three 
hundred. 

there were only two other besides himself at supper. one of them, a 
bearded, middle-aged man in tweeds, sat by himself at the far end, 
and harris kept out of his way because he was english. he fear he 
might be in business, possibly even in the silk business, and that 
he would perhaps talk on the subject. the other traveller, however, 
was a catholic priest. he was a little man who ate his salad with a 
knife, yet so gently that it was almost inoffensive, and it was the 
sight of “the cloth” that recall his memory of the old antagonism. 
harris mention by way of conversation the object of his sentimental 
journey, and the priest look up sharp at him with raise eyebrow 
and an expression of surprise and suspicion that somehow pique him. he 
ascribe it to his difference of belief. 

“yes,” went on the silk merchant, pleased to talk of what his mind was 
so full, “and it was a curious experience for an english boy to be 
drop down into a school of a hundred foreigners. i well remember the 
lonely and intolerable heimweh of it at first.” his germany was very 
fluent. 

the priest opposite look up from his cold veal and potato salad and 
smiled. it was a nice face. he explain quiet that he did not belong 
here, but was making a tour of the parish of württemberg and baden. 

“it was a strict life,” add harris. “we english, i remember, use to 
call it _gefängnisleben_--prison life!” 

the face of the other, for some unaccountable reason, darkened. after a 
slight pause, and more by way of polite than because he wish to 
continue the subject, he said quietly-- 

“it was a flourish school in those days, of course. afterwards, 
i have heard----” he shrug his shoulder slightly, and the odd 
look--it almost seem a look of alarm--came back into his eyes. the 
sentence remain unfinished. 

something in the tone of the man seem to his listener uncall 
for--in a sense reproachful, singular. harris bridle in spite of 
himself. 

“it has changed?” he asked. “i can hardly believe----” 

“you have not heard, then?” observe the priest gently, making a 
gesture as though to cross himself, yet not actually complete it. 
“you have not heard what happen there before it was abandoned----?” 

it was very childish, of course, and perhaps he was overtire and 
overwrought in some way, but the words and manner of the little priest 
seem to him so offensive--so disproportionate offensive--that he 
hardly notice the conclude sentence. he recall the old bitter 
and the old antagonism, and for a moment he almost lost his temper. 

“nonsense,” he interrupt with a forced laugh, “_unsinn!_ you must 
forgive me, sir, for contradict you. but i was a pupil there 
myself. i was at school there. there was no place like it. i cannot 
believe that anything serious could have happen to--to take away its 
character. the devotion of the brother would be difficult to equal 
anywhere----” 

he broke off suddenly, realise that his voice had been raise unduly 
and that the man at the far end of the table might understand german; 
and at the same moment he look up and saw that this individual’s 
eyes were fixed upon his face intently. they were peculiarly bright. 
also they were rather wonderful eyes, and the way they met his own 
serve in some way he could not understand to convey both a reproach 
and a warning. the whole face of the stranger, indeed, made a vivid 
impression upon him, for it was a face, he now notice for the first 
time, in whose presence one would not willing have said or done 
anything unworthy. harris could not explain to himself how it was he 
had not become conscious soon of its presence. 

but he could have bitten off his tongue for have so far forgotten 
himself. the little priest lapsed into silence. only once he said, 
look up and speak in a low voice that was not intended to be 
overheard, but that evidently _was_ overheard, “you will find it 
different.” presently he rose and left the table with a polite bow that 
included both the others. 

and, after him, from the far end rose also the figure in the tweed 
suit, leave harris by himself. 

he sat on for a bit in the darken room, sip his coffee and 
smoking his fifteen-pfennig cigar, till the girl came in to light the 
oil lamps. he felt vex with himself for his lapse from good manners, 
yet hardly able to account for it. most likely, he reflected, he 
had been annoy because the priest had unintentional change the 
pleasant character of his dream by introduce a jar note. later he 
must seek an opportunity to make amends. at present, however, he was 
too impatient for his walk to the school, and he took his stick and hat 
and pass out into the open air. 

and, as he cross before the gasthaus, he notice that the priest 
and the man in the tweed suit were engaged already in such deep 
conversation that they hardly notice him as he pass and raise his 
hat. 

he start off briskly, well remember the way, and hope to reach 
the village in time to have a word with one of the brüder. they might 
even ask him in for a cup of coffee. he felt sure of his welcome, and 
the old memory were in full possession once more. the hour of return 
was a matter of no consequence whatever. 

it was then just after seven o’clock, and the october evening was 
drawing in with chill airs from the recess of the forest. the road 
plunge straight from the railway clearing into its depths, and in a 
very few minutes the tree engulf him and the clack of his boots 
fell dead and echoless against the serried stem of a million firs. it 
was very black; one trunk was hardly distinguishable from another. he 
walk smartly, swinging his holly stick. once or twice he pass a 
peasant on his way to bed, and the guttural “gruss got,” unheard for 
so long, emphasise the passage of time, while yet making it seem as 
nothing. a fresh group of pictures crowded his mind. again the figures 
of former schoolfellow flit out of the forest and kept pace by his 
side, whisper of the doings of long ago. one reverie step hard 
upon the heel of another. every turn in the road, every clearing of 
the forest, he knew, and each in turn brought forgotten association to 
life. he enjoy himself thoroughly. 

he march on and on. there was powdered gold in the sky till the moon 
rose, and then a wind of faint silver spread silent between the 
earth and stars. he saw the tip of the fir tree shimmer, and heard 
them whisper as the breeze turn their needle towards the light. the 
mountain air was indescribable sweet. the road shone like the foam of a 
river through the gloom. white moth flit here and there like silent 
thought across his path, and a hundred smell greet him from the 
forest cavern across the years. 

then, when he least expect it, the tree fell away abrupt on both 
sides, and he stood on the edge of the village clearing. 

he walk faster. there lay the familiar outline of the houses, 
sheet with silver; there stood the tree in the little central square 
with the fountain and small green lawns; there loom the shape of 
the church next to the gasthof der brüdergemeinde; and just beyond, 
dim rising into the sky, he saw with a sudden thrill the mass of 
the huge school building, block castle-like with deep shadow in 
the moonlight, standing square and formidable to face him after the 
silence of more than a quarter of a century. 

he pass quick down the desert village street and stop close 
beneath its shadow, staring up at the wall that had once held 
him prisoner for two years--two unbroken years of discipline and 
homesickness. memory and emotion surge through his mind; for the 
most vivid sensation of his youth had focus about this spot, and 
it was here he had first begun to live and learn values. not a single 
footstep broke the silence, though lights glimmer here and there 
through cottage windows; but when he look up at the high wall of 
the school, drape now in shadow, he easily imagine that well-known 
face crowded to the window to greet him--closed window that really 
reflect only moonlight and the gleam of stars. 

this, then, was the old school building, standing foursquare to the 
world, with its shutter windows, its lofty, tile roof, and the 
spike lightning-conductors point like black and talon finger 
from the corners. for a long time he stood and stared. then, 
presently, he came to himself again, and realise to his joy that a 
light still shone in the window of the _bruderstube_. 

he turn from the road and pass through the iron railings; then 
climb the twelve stone steps and stood facing the black wooden door 
with the heavy bar of iron, a door he had once loathe and dread 
with the hatred and passion of an imprison soul, but now look upon 
tender with a sort of boyish delight. 

almost timorous he pull the rope and listen with a tremor of 
excitement to the clang of the bell deep within the building. and 
the long-forgotten sound brought the past before him with such a 
vivid sense of reality that he positively shivered. it was like the 
magic bell in the fairy-tale that rolls back the curtain of time and 
summons the figures from the shadow of the dead. he had never felt so 
sentimental in his life. it was like being young again. and, at the 
same time, he began to bulk rather large in his own eyes with a certain 
spurious importance. he was a big man from the world of strife and 
action. in this little place of peaceful dream would he, perhaps, not 
cut something of a figure? 

“i’ll try once more,” he thought after a long pause, seize the iron 
bell-rope, and was just about to pull it when a step sound on the 
stone passage within, and the huge door slow swung open. 

a tall man with a rather severe cast of countenance stood facing him in 
silence. 

“i must apologise--it is somewhat late,” he began a trifle pompously, 
“but the fact is i am an old pupil. i have only just arrive and really 
could not restrain myself.” his germany seem not quite so fluent as 
usual. “my interest is so great. i was here in ’70.” 

the other open the door wide and at once bowed him in with a smile 
of genuine welcome. 

“i am bruder kalkmann,” he said quiet in a deep voice. “i myself was 
a master here about that time. it is a great pleasure always to welcome 
a former pupil.” he look at him very keen for a few seconds, 
and then added, “i think, too, it is splendid of you to come--very 
splendid.” 

“it is a very great pleasure,” harris replied, delight with his 
reception. 

the dimly-lighted corridor with its flooring of grey stone, and the 
familiar sound of a germany voice echo through it,--with the peculiar 
intonation the brother always use in speaking,--all combine to lift 
him bodily, as it were, into the dream-atmosphere of long-forgotten 
days. he step gladly into the building and the door shut with the 
familiar thunder that complete the reconstruction of the past. he 
almost felt the old sense of imprisonment, of ache nostalgia, of 
have lost his liberty. 

harris sigh involuntary and turn towards his host, who return 
his smile faint and then led the way down the corridor. 

“the boys have retired,” he explained, “and, as you remember, we keep 
early hour here. but, at least, you will join us for a little while in 
the _bruderstube_ and enjoy a cup of coffee.” this was precisely what 
the silk merchant had hoped, and he accept with an alacrity that he 
intended to be temper by graciousness. “and tomorrow,” continue the 
bruder, “you must come and spend a whole day with us. you may even find 
acquaintances, for several pupil of your day have come back here as 
masters.” 

for one brief second there pass into the man’s eyes a look that 
made the visitor start. but it vanish as quick as it came. it was 
impossible to define. harris convinced himself it was the effect of a 
shadow cast by the lamp they had just pass on the wall. he dismiss 
it from his mind. 

“you are very kind, i’m sure,” he said politely. “it is perhaps a 
greater pleasure to me than you can imagine to see the place again. 
ah,”--he stop short opposite a door with the upper half of glass 
and peer in--“surely there is one of the music rooms where i use to 
practise the violin. how it come back to me after all these years!” 

bruder kalkmann stop indulgently, smiling, to allow his guest a 
moment’s inspection. 

“you still have the boys’ orchestra? i remember i use to play ‘_zweite 
geige_’ in it. bruder schliemann conduct at the piano. dear me, i can 
see him now with his long black hair and--and----” he stop abruptly. 
again the odd, dark look pass over the stern face of his companion. 
for an instant it seem curious familiar. 

“we still keep up the pupils’ orchestra,” he said, “but bruder 
schliemann, i am sorry to say----” he hesitate an instant, and then 
added, “bruder schliemann is dead.” 

“indeed, indeed,” said harris quickly. “i am sorry to hear it.” he was 
conscious of a faint feeling of distress, but whether it arose from 
the news of his old music teacher’s death, or--from something else--he 
could not quite determine. he gaze down the corridor that lost itself 
among shadows. in the street and village everything had seem so 
much small than he remembered, but here, inside the school building, 
everything seem so much bigger. the corridor was lofty and longer, 
more spacious and vast, than the mental picture he had preserved. his 
thought wander dreamy for an instant. 

he glance up and saw the face of the bruder watch him with a smile 
of patient indulgence. 

“your memory possess you,” he observe gently, and the stern look 
pass into something almost pitying. 

“you are right,” return the man of silk, “they do. this was the most 
wonderful period of my whole life in a sense. at the time i hate 
it----” he hesitated, not wish to hurt the brother’s feelings. 

“according to english idea it seem strict, of course,” the other 
said persuasively, so that he went on. 

“----yes, partly that; and partly the ceaseless nostalgia, and the 
solitude which came from never being really alone. in english school 
the boys enjoy peculiar freedom, you know.” 

bruder kalkmann, he saw, was listen intently. 

“but it produce one result that i have never wholly lost,” he 
continue self-consciously, “and am grateful for.” 

“_ach! wie so, denn?_” 

“the constant inner pain threw me headlong into your religious life, so 
that the whole force of my being seem to project itself towards the 
search for a deep satisfaction--a real resting-place for the soul. 
during my two years here i yearn for god in my boyish way as perhaps 
i have never yearn for anything since. moreover, i have never quite 
lost that sense of peace and inward joy which accompany the search. i 
can never quite forget this school and the deep things it taught me.” 

he pause at the end of his long speech, and a brief silence fell 
between them. he fear he had said too much, or express himself 
clumsy in the foreign language, and when bruder kalkmann laid a hand 
upon his shoulder, he gave a little involuntary start. 

“so that my memory perhaps do possess me rather strongly,” he add 
apologetically; “and this long corridor, these rooms, that barred and 
gloomy front door, all touch chord that--that----” his germany fail 
him and he glance at his companion with an explanatory smile and 
gesture. but the brother had remove the hand from his shoulder and was 
standing with his back to him, look down the passage. 

“naturally, naturally so,” he said hasty without turning round. “_es 
ist doch selbstverständlich._ we shall all understand.” 

then he turn suddenly, and harris saw that his face had turn most 
oddly and disagreeable sinister. it may only have been the shadow 
again play their trick with the wretched oil lamp on the wall, 
for the dark expression pass instantly as they retrace their steps 
down the corridor, but the englishman somehow got the impression that 
he had said something to give offence, something that was not quite to 
the other’s taste. opposite the door of the _bruderstube_ they stopped. 
harris realise that it was late and he had possibly stay talk too 
long. he made a tentative effort to leave, but his companion would not 
hear of it. 

“you must have a cup of coffee with us,” he said firm as though he 
meant it, “and my colleague will be delight to see you. some of them 
will remember you, perhaps.” 

the sound of voice came pleasant through the door, men’s voice 
talk together. bruder kalkmann turn the handle and they enter a 
room ablaze with light and full of people. 

“ah,--but your name?” he whispered, bend down to catch the reply; 
“you have not told me your name yet.” 

“harris,” said the englishman quick as they went in. he felt nervous 
as he cross the threshold, but ascribe the momentary trepidation 
to the fact that he was break the strictest rule of the whole 
establishment, which forbade a boy under severest penalty to come 
near this holy of holy where the master took their brief leisure. 

“ah, yes, of course--harris,” repeated the other as though he 
remember it “come in, herr harris, come in, please. your visit will 
be immensely appreciated. it is really very fine, very wonderful of you 
to have come in this way.” 

the door closed behind them and, in the sudden light which made his 
sight swim for a moment, the exaggeration of the language escape his 
attention. he heard the voice of bruder kalkmann introduce him. he 
spoke very loud, indeed, unnecessarily,--absurdly loud, harris thought. 

“brothers,” he announced, “it is my pleasure and privilege to introduce 
to you herr harris from england. he has just arrive to make us a 
little visit, and i have already express to him on behalf of us all 
the satisfaction we feel that he is here. he was, as you remember, a 
pupil in the year ’70.” 

it was a very formal, a very germany introduction, but harris rather 
like it. it made him feel important and he appreciate the tact that 
made it almost seem as though he had been expected. 

the black form rose and bowed; harris bowed; kalkmann bowed. every one 
was very polite and very courtly. the room swam with moving figures; 
the light dazzle him after the gloom of the corridor; there was thick 
cigar smoke in the atmosphere. he took the chair that was offer to 
him between two of the brothers, and sat down, feeling vague that his 
perception were not quite as keen and accurate as usual. he felt a 
trifle daze perhaps, and the spell of the past came strong over him, 
confuse the immediate present and making everything dwindle oddly to 
the dimensions of long ago. he seem to pass under the mastery of a 
great mood that was a composite reproduction of all the mood of his 
forgotten boyhood. 

then he pull himself together with a sharp effort and enter into 
the conversation that had begun again to buzz round him. moreover, 
he enter into it with keen pleasure, for the brothers--there were 
perhaps a dozen of them in the little room--treated him with a charm 
of manner that speedy made him feel one of themselves. this, again, 
was a very subtle delight to him. he felt that he had step out 
of the greedy, vulgar, self-seeking world, the world of silk and 
market and profit-making--stepped into the cleaner atmosphere where 
spiritual ideal were paramount and life was simple and devoted. 
it all charm him inexpressibly, so that he realised--yes, in a 
sense--the degrade of his twenty years’ absorption in business. 
this keen atmosphere under the stars where men thought only of their 
souls, and of the soul of others, was too rarefied for the world he 
was now associate with. he found himself making comparison to his 
own disadvantage,--comparisons with the mystical little dreamer that 
had step thirty years before from the stern peace of this devout 
community, and the man of the world that he had since become,--and 
the contrast made him shiver with a keen regret and something like 
self-contempt. 

he glance round at the other face floating towards him through 
tobacco smoke--this acrid cigar smoke he remember so well: how keen 
they were, how strong, placid, touched with the nobility of great aim 
and unselfish purposes. at one or two he look particularly. he hardly 
knew why. they rather fascinate him. there was something so very stern 
and uncompromising about them, and something, too, oddly, subtly, 
familiar, that yet just elude him. but whenever their eyes met his own 
they held undeniable welcome in them; and some held more--a kind of 
perplexed admiration, he thought, something that was between esteem and 
deference. this note of respect in all the face was very flatter to 
his vanity. 

coffee was serve presently, made by a black-haired brother who sat 
in the corner by the piano and bore a marked resemblance to bruder 
schliemann, the musical director of thirty years ago. harris exchange 
bow with him when he took the cup from his white hands, which he 
notice were like the hand of a woman. he lit a cigar, offer to him 
by his neighbour, with whom he was chat delightfully, and who, in 
the glare of the light match, remind him sharp for a moment of 
bruder pagel, his former room-master. 

“_es ist wirklich merkwürdig_,” he said, “how many resemblance i see, 
or imagine. it is really _very_ curious!” 

“yes,” reply the other, peer at him over his coffee cup, “the 
spell of the place is wonderful strong. i can well understand that 
the old face rise before your mind’s eye--almost to the exclusion of 
ourselves perhaps.” 

they both laugh pleasantly. it was soothe to find his mood 
understood and appreciated. and they pass on to talk of the mountain 
village, its isolation, its remote from worldly life, its peculiar 
fitness for meditation and worship, and for spiritual development--of a 
certain kind. 

“and your coming back in this way, herr harris, has pleased us all so 
much,” join in the bruder on his left. “we esteem you for it most 
highly. we honour you for it.” 

harris made a deprecate gesture. “i fear, for my part, it is only a 
very selfish pleasure,” he said a trifle unctuously. 

“not all would have had the courage,” add the one who resemble 
bruder pagel. 

“you mean,” said harris, a little puzzled, “the disturb 
memories----?” 

bruder pagel look at him steadily, with unmistakable admiration and 
respect. “i mean that most men hold so strong to life, and can give 
up so little for their beliefs,” he said gravely. 

the englishman felt slightly uncomfortable. these worthy men really 
made too much of his sentimental journey. besides, the talk was get 
a little out of his depth. he hardly follow it. 

“the worldly life still has _some_ charm for me,” he reply 
smilingly, as though to indicate that sainthood was not yet quite 
within his grasp. 

“all the more, then, must we honour you for so freely coming,” said the 
brother on his left; “so unconditionally!” 

a pause followed, and the silk merchant felt relieved when the 
conversation took a more general turn, although he noted that it never 
travelled very far from the subject of his visit and the wonderful 
situation of the lonely village for men who wish to develop their 
spiritual powers and practise the rite of a high worship. other 
join in, compliment him on his knowledge of the language, 
making him feel utterly at his ease, yet at the same time a little 
uncomfortable by the excess of their admiration. after all, it was such 
a very small thing to do, this sentimental journey. 

the time pass along quickly; the coffee was excellent, the cigar 
soft and of the nutty flavour he loved. at length, fear to outstay 
his welcome, he rose reluctantly to take his leave. but the other 
would not hear of it. it was not often a former pupil return to visit 
them in this simple, unaffected way. the night was young. if necessary 
they could even find him a corner in the great _schlafzimmer_ upstairs. 
he was easily persuade to stay a little longer. somehow he had become 
the centre of the little party. he felt pleased, flattered, honoured. 

“and perhaps bruder schliemann will play something for us--now.” 

it was kalkmann speaking, and harris start visibly as he heard the 
name, and saw the black-haired man by the piano turn with a smile. for 
schliemann was the name of his old music director, who was dead. could 
this be his son? they were so exactly alike. 

“if bruder meyer has not put his amati to bed, i will accompany him,” 
said the musician suggestively, look across at a man whom harris had 
not yet noticed, and who, he now saw, was the very image of a former 
master of that name. 

meyer rose and excuse himself with a little bow, and the englishman 
quick observe that he had a peculiar gesture as though his neck had 
a false join on to the body just below the collar and fear it might 
break. meyer of old had this trick of movement. he remember how the 
boys use to copy it. 

he glance sharp from face to face, feeling as though some silent, 
unseen process were change everything about him. all the face seem 
oddly familiar. pagel, the brother he had been talk with, was of 
course the image of pagel, his former room-master; and kalkmann, he now 
realise for the first time, was the very twin of another master whose 
name he had quite forgotten, but whom he use to dislike intense in 
the old days. and, through the smoke, peer at him from the corner 
of the room, he saw that all the brother about him had the face he 
had known and live with long ago--röst, fluheim, meinert, rigel, gysin. 

he stare hard, sudden grown more alert, and everywhere saw, or 
fancy he saw, strange likenesses, ghostly resemblances,--more, the 
identical face of years ago. there was something queer about it all, 
something not quite right, something that made him feel uneasy. he 
shook himself, mental and actually, blow the smoke from before his 
eyes with a long breath, and as he did so he notice to his dismay that 
every one was fixedly staring. they were watch him. 

this brought him to his senses. as an englishman, and a foreigner, he 
did not wish to be rude, or to do anything to make himself foolish 
conspicuous and spoil the harmony of the evening. he was a guest, and a 
privileged guest at that. besides, the music had already begun. bruder 
schliemann’s long white finger were caress the key to some purpose. 

he subside into his chair and smoke with half-closed eyes that yet 
saw everything. 

but the shudder had establish itself in his being, and, whether he 
would or not, it kept repeating itself. as a town, far up some inland 
river, feel the pressure of the distant sea, so he became aware that 
mighty forces from somewhere beyond his ken were urge themselves 
up against his soul in this smoky little room. he began to feel 
exceedingly ill at ease. 

and as the music fill the air his mind began to clear. like a lift 
veil there rose up something that had hitherto obscure his vision. 
the words of the priest at the railway inn flash across his brain 
unbidden: “you will find it different.” and also, though why he 
could not tell, he saw mental the strong, rather wonderful eyes of 
that other guest at the supper-table, the man who had overhead his 
conversation, and had later got into earnest talk with the priest. he 
took out his watch and stole a glance at it. two hour had slip by. 
it was already eleven o’clock. 

schliemann, meanwhile, utterly absorb in his music, was play a 
solemn measure. the piano sang marvellously. the power of a great 
conviction, the simplicity of great art, the vital spiritual message 
of a soul that had found itself--all this, and more, were in the 
chords, and yet somehow the music was what can only be describe as 
impure--atrociously and diabolical impure. and the piece itself, 
although harris did not recognise it as anything familiar, was surely 
the music of a mass--huge, majestic, sombre? it stalk through the 
smoky room with slow power, like the passage of something that was 
mighty, yet profound intimate, and as it went there stir into each 
and every face about him the signature of the enormous forces of which 
it was the audible symbol. the countenance round him turn sinister, 
but not idly, negative sinister: they grew dark with purpose. he 
sudden recall the face of bruder kalkmann in the corridor early 
in the evening. the motive of their secret soul rose to the eyes, and 
mouths, and foreheads, and hung there for all to see like the black 
banner of an assembly of ill-starred and fallen creatures. demons--was 
the horrible word that flash through his brain like a sheet of fire. 

when this sudden discovery leap out upon him, for a moment he lost 
his self-control. without wait to think and weigh his extraordinary 
impression, he did a very foolish but a very natural thing. feeling 
himself irresistible driven by the sudden stress to some kind of 
action, he sprang to his feet--and screamed! to his own utter 
amaze he stood up and shriek aloud! 

but no one stirred. no one, apparently, took the slightest notice of 
his absurd wild behaviour. it was almost as if no one but himself 
had heard the scream at all--as though the music had drown it and 
swallow it up--as though after all perhaps he had not really scream 
as loud as he imagined, or had not scream at all. 

then, as he glance at the motionless, dark face before him, something 
of utter cold pass into his being, touching his very soul.... all 
emotion cool suddenly, leave him like a recede tide. he sat down 
again, ashamed, mortified, angry with himself for behave like a fool 
and a boy. and the music, meanwhile, continue to issue from the white 
and snake-like finger of bruder schliemann, as poison wine might 
issue from the weirdly-fashioned neck of antique phials. 

and, with the rest of them, harris drank it in. 

force himself to believe that he had been the victim of some kind of 
illusory perception, he vigorous restrained his feelings. then the 
music presently ceased, and every one applaud and began to talk at 
once, laughing, change seats, compliment the player, and behave 
naturally and easily as though nothing out of the way had happened. 
the face appear normal once more. the brother crowded round their 
visitor, and he join in their talk and even heard himself thank 
the gifted musician. 

but, at the same time, he found himself edging towards the door, near 
and nearer, change his chair when possible, and join the group 
that stood closest to the way of escape. 

“i must thank you all _tausendmal_ for my little reception and the 
great pleasure--the very great honour you have done me,” he began in 
decided tone at length, “but i fear i have trespass far too long 
already on your hospitality. moreover, i have some distance to walk to 
my inn.” 

a chorus of voice greet his words. they would not hear of his 
going,--at least not without first partake of refreshment. they 
produce pumpernickel from one cupboard, and rye-bread and sausage from 
another, and all began to talk again and eat. more coffee was made, 
fresh cigar lighted, and bruder meyer took out his violin and began to 
tune it softly. 

“there is always a bed upstairs if herr harris will accept it,” said 
one. 

“and it is difficult to find the way out now, for all the door are 
locked,” laugh another loudly. 

“let us take our simple pleasure as they come,” cry a third. “bruder 
harris will understand how we appreciate the honour of this last visit 
of his.” 

they made a dozen excuses. they all laughed, as though the polite 
of their words was but formal, and veiled thinly--more and more 
thinly--a very different meaning. 

“and the hour of midnight draw near,” add bruder kalkmann with a 
charming smile, but in a voice that sound to the englishman like the 
grating of iron hinges. 

their germany seem to him more and more difficult to understand. 
he noted that they call him “bruder” too, class him as one of 
themselves. 

and then sudden he had a flash of keen perception, and 
realise with a creep of his flesh that he had all along 
misinterpreted--grossly misinterpret all they had been saying. they 
had talk about the beauty of the place, its isolation and remote 
from the world, its peculiar fitness for certain kind of spiritual 
development and worship--yet hardly, he now grasped, in the sense in 
which he had taken the words. they had meant something different. 
their spiritual powers, their desire for loneliness, their passion 
for worship, were not the powers, the solitude, or the worship that 
_he_ meant and understood. he was play a part in some horrible 
masquerade; he was among men who cloak their lives with religion in 
order to follow their real purpose unseen of men. 

what did it all mean? how had he blunder into so equivocal a 
situation? had he blunder into it at all? had he not rather been led 
into it, deliberate led? his thought grew dreadfully confused, and 
his confidence in himself began to fade. and why, he sudden thought 
again, were they so impress by the mere fact of his coming to revisit 
his old school? what was it they so admire and wonder at in his 
simple act? why did they set such store upon his have the courage to 
come, to “give himself so freely,” “unconditionally” as one of them had 
express it with such a mockery of exaggeration? 

fear stir in his heart most horribly, and he found no answer to any 
of his questionings. only one thing he now understood quite clearly: 
it was their purpose to keep him here. they did not intend that he 
should go. and from this moment he realise that they were sinister, 
formidable and, in some way he had yet to discover, inimical to 
himself, inimical to his life. and the phrase one of them had use a 
moment ago--“this _last_ visit of his”--rose before his eyes in letters 
of flame. 

harris was not a man of action, and had never known in all the course 
of his career what it meant to be in a situation of real danger. he 
was not necessarily a coward, though, perhaps, a man of untry nerve. 
he realise at last plainly that he was in a very awkward predicament 
indeed, and that he had to deal with men who were utterly in earnest. 
what their intentions were he only vague guessed. his mind, indeed, 
was too confuse for definite ratiocination, and he was only able to 
follow blind the strongest instinct that move in him. it never 
occur to him that the brother might all be mad, or that he himself 
might have temporary lost his senses and be suffering under some 
terrible delusion. in fact, nothing occur to him--he realise 
nothing--except that he meant to escape--and the quick the better. a 
tremendous revulsion of feeling set in and overpower him. 

accordingly, without further protest for the moment, he ate his 
pumpernickel and drank his coffee, talk meanwhile as naturally and 
pleasant as he could, and when a suitable interval had passed, he 
rose to his feet and announce once more that he must now take his 
leave. he spoke very quietly, but very decidedly. no one hearing him 
could doubt that he meant what he said. he had got very close to the 
door by this time. 

“i regret,” he said, use his best german, and speak to a hush 
room, “that our pleasant evening must come to an end, but it is now 
time for me to wish you all good-night.” and then, as no one said 
anything, he added, though with a trifle less assurance, “and i thank 
you all most sincerely for your hospitality.” 

“on the contrary,” reply kalkmann instantly, rising from his chair 
and ignore the hand the englishman had stretch out to him, “it is 
we who have to thank you; and we do so most grateful and sincerely.” 

and at the same moment at least half a dozen of the brother took up 
their position between himself and the door. 

“you are very good to say so,” harris reply as firm as he could 
manage, notice this movement out of the corner of his eye, “but 
really i had no conception that--my little chance visit could have 
afford you so much pleasure.” he move another step near the door, 
but bruder schliemann came across the room quick and stood in front 
of him. his attitude was uncompromising. a dark and terrible expression 
had come into his face. 

“but it was _not_ by chance that you came, bruder harris,” he said so 
that all the room could hear; “surely we have not misunderstood your 
presence here?” he raise his black eyebrows. 

“no, no,” the englishman hasten to reply. “i was--i am delight 
to be here. i told you what pleasure it gave me to find myself among 
you. do not misunderstand me, i beg.” his voice falter a little, and 
he had difficulty in finding the words. more and more, too, he had 
difficulty in understanding _their_ words. 

“of course,” interpose bruder kalkmann in his iron bass, “_we_ have 
not misunderstood. you have come back in the spirit of true and 
unselfish devotion. you offer yourself freely, and we all appreciate 
it. it is your willing and nobility that have so completely won our 
venerate and respect.” a faint murmur of applause ran round the room. 
“what we all delight in--what our great master will especially delight 
in--is the value of your spontaneous and voluntary----” 

he use a word harris did not understand. he said “_opfer_.” the 
bewilder englishman search his brain for the translation, and 
search in vain. for the life of him he could not remember what it 
meant. but the word, for all his inability to translate it, touched his 
soul with ice. it was worse, far worse, than anything he had imagined. 
he felt like a lost, helpless creature, and all power to fight sank out 
of him from that moment. 

“it is magnificent to be such a willing----” add schliemann, sidle 
up to him with a dreadful leer on his face. he made use of the same 
word--“_opfer_.” 

god! what could it all mean? “offer himself!” “true spirit of 
devotion!” “willing,” “unselfish,” “magnificent!” _opfer, opfer, 
opfer!_ what in the name of heaven did it mean, that strange, 
mysterious word that struck such terror into his heart? 

he made a valiant effort to keep his presence of mind and hold his 
nerves steady. turning, he saw that kalkmann’s face was a dead white. 
kalkmann! he understood that well enough. _kalkmann_ meant “man of 
chalk”; he knew that. but what did “_opfer_” mean? that was the real 
key to the situation. words pour through his disorder mind in an 
endless stream--unusual, rare words he had perhaps heard but once in 
his life--while “_opfer_,” a word in common use, entire escape him. 
what an extraordinary mockery it all was! 

then kalkmann, pale as death, but his face hard as iron, spoke a few 
low words that he did not catch, and the brother standing by the wall 
at once turn the lamp down so that the room became dim. in the half 
light he could only just discern their face and movements. 

“it is time,” he heard kalkmann’s remorseless voice continue just 
behind him. “the hour of midnight is at hand. let us prepare. he comes! 
he comes; bruder asmodeliu comes!” his voice rose to a chant. 

and the sound of that name, for some extraordinary reason, was 
terrible--utterly terrible; so that harris shook from head to foot as 
he heard it. its utterance fill the air like soft thunder, and a hush 
came over the whole room. forces rose all about him, transform the 
normal into the horrible, and the spirit of craven fear ran through all 
his being, bring him to the verge of collapse. 

_asmodelius! asmodelius!_ the name was appalling. for he understood at 
last to whom it refer and the meaning that lay between its great 
syllables. at the same instant, too, he sudden understood the meaning 
of that unremembered word. the import of the word “_opfer_” flash 
upon his soul like a message of death. 

he thought of making a wild effort to reach the door, but the weakness 
of his tremble knees, and the row of black figures that stood 
between, dissuade him at once. he would have scream for help, but 
remember the empty of the vast building, and the lonely of 
the situation, he understood that no help could come that way, and he 
kept his lip closed. he stood still and did nothing. but he knew now 
what was coming. 

two of the brother approach and took him gently by the arm. 

“bruder asmodeliu accept you,” they whispered; “are you ready?” 

then he found his tongue and try to speak. “but what have i to do 
with this bruder asm--asmo----?” he stammered, a desperate rush of 
words crowd vain behind the halting tongue. 

the name refuse to pass his lips. he could not pronounce it as they 
did. he could not pronounce it at all. his sense of helpless then 
enter the acute stage, for this inability to speak the name produce 
a fresh sense of quite horrible confusion in his mind, and he became 
extraordinarily agitated. 

“i came here for a friendly visit,” he try to say with a great 
effort, but, to his intense dismay, he heard his voice saying something 
quite different, and actually making use of that very word they had all 
used: “i came here as a willing _opfer_,” he heard his own voice say, 
“and _i am quite ready_.” 

he was lost beyond all recall now! not alone his mind, but the very 
muscle of his body had pass out of control. he felt that he was 
hover on the confines of a phantom or demon-world,--a world in which 
the name they had spoken constitute the master-name, the word of 
ultimate power. 

what follow he heard and saw as in a nightmare. 

“in the half light that veil all truth, let us prepare to worship and 
adore,” chant schliemann, who had precede him to the end of the room. 

“in the mists that protect our face before the black throne, let us 
make ready the willing victim,” echo kalkmann in his great bass. 

they raise their faces, listen expectantly, as a roaring sound, 
like the passing of mighty projectiles, fill the air, far, far away, 
very wonderful, very forbidding. the wall of the room trembled. 

“he comes! he comes! he comes!” chant the brother in chorus. 

the sound of roaring die away, and an atmosphere of still and utter 
cold establish itself over all. then kalkmann, dark and unutterable 
stern, turn in the dim light and face the rest. 

“asmodelius, our _hauptbruder_, is about us,” he cry in a voice that 
even while it shook was yet a voice of iron; “asmodelius is about us. 
make ready.” 

there follow a pause in which no one stir or spoke. a tall brother 
approach the englishman; but kalkmann held up his hand. 

“let the eyes remain uncovered,” he said, “in honour of so freely 
give himself.” and to his horror harris then realise for the first 
time that his hand were already fasten to his sides. 

the brother retreat again silently, and in the pause that follow 
all the figures about him drop to their knees, leave him standing 
alone, and as they dropped, in voice hush with mingle reverence and 
awe, they cry softly, odiously, appallingly, the name of the being 
whom they momentarily expect to appear. 

then, at the end of the room, where the window seem to have 
disappear so that he saw the stars, there rose into view far up 
against the night sky, grand and terrible, the outline of a man. a 
kind of grey glory envelop it so that it resemble a steel-cased 
statue, immense, imposing, horrific in its distant splendour; while, 
at the same time, the face was so spiritual mighty, yet so proudly, 
so austere sad, that harris felt as he stared, that the sight was 
more than his eyes could meet, and that in another moment the power 
of vision would fail him altogether, and he must sink into utter 
nothingness. 

so remote and inaccessible hung this figure that it was impossible to 
gauge anything as to its size, yet at the same time so strange close, 
that when the grey radiance from its mightily broken visage, august and 
mournful, beat down upon his soul, pulse like some dark star with the 
powers of spiritual evil, he felt almost as though he were look into 
a face no farther remove from him in space than the face of any one of 
the brother who stood by his side. 

and then the room fill and tremble with sound that harris 
understood full well were the failing voice of other who had precede 
him in a long series down the years. there came first a plain, sharp 
cry, as of a man in the last anguish, choke for his breath, and 
yet, with the very final expiration of it, breathing the name of the 
worship--of the dark being who rejoice to hear it. the cries of the 
strangled; the short, running gasp of the suffocated; and the smother 
gurgle of the tighten throat, all these, and more, echo back 
and forth between the walls, the very wall in which he now stood a 
prisoner, a sacrificial victim. the cries, too, not alone of the broken 
bodies, but--far worse--of beaten, broken souls. and as the ghastly 
chorus rose and fell, there came also the face of the lost and unhappy 
creature to whom they belonged, and, against that curtain of pale grey 
light, he saw float past him in the air, an array of white and piteous 
human countenance that seem to beckon and gibber at him as though he 
were already one of themselves. 

slowly, too, as the voice rose, and the pallid crew sail past, that 
giant form of grey descended from the sky and approach the room that 
contained the worship and their prisoner. hand rose and sank about 
him in the darkness, and he felt that he was being drape in other 
garment than his own; a circlet of ice seem to run about his head, 
while round the waist, enclose the fasten arms, he felt a girdle 
tight drawn. at last, about his very throat, there ran a soft and 
silken touch which, better than if there had been full light, and a 
mirror held to his face, he understood to be the cord of sacrifice--and 
of death. 

at this moment the brothers, still prostrate upon the floor, began 
again their mournful, yet impassioned chanting, and as they did so a 
strange thing happened. for, apparently without moving or alter its 
position, the huge figure seemed, at once and suddenly, to be inside 
the room, almost beside him, and to fill the space around him to the 
exclusion of all else. 

he was now beyond all ordinary sensation of fear, only a drab feeling 
as of death--the death of the soul--stirred in his heart. his thought 
no long even beat vain for escape. the end was near, and he knew 
it. 

the dreadfully chant voice rose about him in a wave: “we worship! 
we adore! we offer!” the sound fill his ear and hammered, almost 
meaningless, upon his brain. 

then the majestic grey face turn slow downwards upon him, and his 
very soul pass outwards and seem to become absorb in the sea 
of those anguished eyes. at the same moment a dozen hand forced him 
to his knees, and in the air before him he saw the arm of kalkmann 
upraised, and felt the pressure about his throat grow strong. 

it was in this awful moment, when he had given up all hope, and the 
help of gods or men seem beyond question, that a strange thing 
happened. for before his fade and terrify vision, there slid, as 
in a dream of light,--yet without apparent rhyme or reason--wholly 
unbidden and unexplained,--the face of that other man at the supper 
table of the railway inn. and the sight, even mentally, of that strong, 
wholesome, vigorous english face, inspired him sudden with a new 
courage. 

it was but a flash of fade vision before he sank into a dark and 
terrible death, yet, in some inexplicable way, the sight of that face 
stir in him unconquerable hope and the certainty of deliverance. it 
was a face of power, a face, he now realised, of simple goodness such 
as might have been seen by men of old on the shore of galilee; a face, 
by heaven, that could conquer even the devil of outer space.
