and, in his despair and abandonment, he call upon it, and call with 
no uncertain accents. he found his voice in this overwhelming moment 
to some purpose; though the words he actually used, and whether they 
were in germany or english, he could never remember. their effect, 
nevertheless, was instantaneous. the brother understood, and that grey 
figure of evil understood. 

for a second the confusion was terrific. there came a great shatter 
sound. it seem that the very earth trembled. but all harris 
remember afterwards was that voice rose about him in the clamour of 
terrify alarm-- 

“a man of power is among us! a man of god!” 

the vast sound was repeated--the rush through space as of huge 
projectiles--and he sank to the floor of the room, unconscious. the 
entire scene had vanished, vanish like smoke over the roof of a 
cottage when the wind blows. 

and, by his side, sat down a slight, un-german figure,--the figure of 
the stranger at the inn,--the man who had the “rather wonderful eyes.” 

* * * * * 

when harris came to himself he felt cold. he was lie under the open 
sky, and the cool air of field and forest was blow upon his face. 
he sat up and look about him. the memory of the late scene was 
still horrible in his mind, but no vestige of it remained. no wall 
or ceiling enclose him; he was no long in a room at all. there 
were no lamp turn low, no cigar smoke, no black form of sinister 
worshippers, no tremendous grey figure hover beyond the windows. 

open space was about him, and he was lie on a pile of brick and 
mortar, his clothes soaked with dew, and the kind stars shining 
bright overhead. he was lying, bruise and shaken, among the 
heaped-up débris of a ruin building. 

he stood up and stare about him. there, in the shadowy distance, lay 
the surrounding forest, and here, close at hand, stood the outline of 
the village buildings. but, underfoot, beyond question, lay nothing 
but the broken heap of stone that betoken a building long since 
crumble to dust. then he saw that the stone were blackened, and 
that great wooden beams, half burnt, half rotten, made lines through 
the general débris. he stood, then, among the ruins of a burnt and 
shatter building, the weeds and nettle prove conclusive that it 
had lain thus for many years. 

the moon had already set behind the encircle forest, but the stars 
that spangle the heaven threw enough light to enable him to make 
quite sure of what he saw. harris, the silk merchant, stood among these 
broken and burnt stone and shivered. 

then he sudden became aware that out of the gloom a figure had risen 
and stood beside him. peer at him, he thought he recognise the face 
of the stranger at the railway inn. 

“are _you_ real?” he ask in a voice he hardly recognise as his own. 

“more than real--i’m friendly,” reply the stranger; “i follow you 
up here from the inn.” 

harris stood and stare for several minutes without add anything. 
his teeth chattered. the least sound made him start; but the simple 
words in his own language, and the tone in which they were uttered, 
comfort him inconceivably. 

“you’re english too, thank god,” he said inconsequently. “these germany 
devils----” he broke off and put a hand to his eyes. “but what’s 
become of them all--and the room--and--and----” the hand travelled 
down to his throat and move nervous round his neck. he drew a long, 
long breath of relief. “did i dream everything--everything?” he said 
distractedly. 

he stare wildly about him, and the stranger move forward and took his 
arm. “come,” he said soothingly, yet with a trace of command in the 
voice, “we will move away from here. the high-road, or even the wood 
will be more to your taste, for we are standing now on one of the most 
haunted--and most terribly haunted--spots of the whole world.” 

he guide his companion’s stumble footstep over the broken masonry 
until they reach the path, the nettle sting their hands, and 
harris feeling his way like a man in a dream. passing through the 
twist iron railing they reach the path, and thence made their way 
to the road, shining white in the night. once safe out of the ruins, 
harris collected himself and turn to look back. 

“but, how is it possible?” he exclaimed, his voice still shaking. 
“how can it be possible? when i came in here i saw the building in 
the moonlight. they open the door. i saw the figures and heard the 
voice and touched, yes touched their very hands, and saw their damn 
black faces, saw them far more plainly than i see you now.” he was 
deep bewildered. the glamour was still upon his eyes with a degree 
of reality strong than the reality even of normal life. “was i so 
utterly deluded?” 

then sudden the words of the stranger, which he had only half heard 
or understood, return to him. 

“haunted?” he asked, look hard at him; “haunted, did you say?” he 
pause in the roadway and stare into the dark where the building 
of the old school had first appear to him. but the stranger hurried 
him forward. 

“we shall talk more safe farther on,” he said. “i follow you from 
the inn the moment i realise where you had gone. when i found you it 
was eleven o’clock----” 

“eleven o’clock,” said harris, remember with a shudder. 

“----i saw you drop. i watch over you till you recover 
consciousness of your own accord, and now--now i am here to guide you 
safe back to the inn. i have broken the spell--the glamour----” 

“i owe you a great deal, sir,” interrupt harris again, beginning 
to understand something of the stranger’s kindness, “but i don’t 
understand it all. i feel daze and shaken.” his teeth still chattered, 
and spell of violent shiver pass over him from head to foot. he 
found that he was clinging to the other’s arm. in this way they pass 
beyond the desert and crumble village and gain the high-road that 
led homewards through the forest. 

“that school building has long been in ruins,” said the man at his side 
presently; “it was burnt down by order of the elder of the community 
at least ten years ago. the village has been uninhabited ever since. 
but the simulacra of certain ghastly event that took place under 
that roof in past days still continue. and the ‘shells’ of the chief 
participant still enact there the dreadful deed that led to its final 
destruction, and to the desertion of the whole settlement. they were 
devil-worshippers!” 

harris listen with bead of perspiration on his forehead that did 
not come alone from their leisurely pace through the cool night. 
although he had seen this man but once before in his life, and had 
never before exchange so much as a word with him, he felt a degree of 
confidence and a subtle sense of safety and well-being in his presence 
that were the most heal influence he could possibly have wish 
after the experience he had been through. for all that, he still felt 
as if he were walking in a dream, and though he heard every word that 
fell from his companion’s lips, it was only the next day that the full 
import of all he said became fully clear to him. the presence of this 
quiet stranger, the man with the wonderful eyes which he felt now, 
rather than saw, applied a soothe anodyne to his shatter spirit 
that heal him through and through. and this heal influence, 
distill from the dark figure at his side, satisfy his first 
imperative need, so that he almost forgot to realise how strange and 
opportune it was that the man should be there at all. 

it somehow never occur to him to ask his name, or to feel any undue 
wonder that one passing tourist should take so much trouble on behalf 
of another. he just walk by his side, listen to his quiet words, 
and allow himself to enjoy the very wonderful experience after his 
recent ordeal, of being helped, strengthened, blessed. only once, 
remember vague something of his reading of years ago, he turn to 
the man beside him, after some more than usually remarkable words, and 
heard himself, almost involuntary it seemed, putt the question: 
“then are you a rosicrucian, sir, perhaps?” but the stranger had 
ignore the words, or possibly not heard them, for he continue with 
his talk as though unconscious of any interruption, and harris became 
aware that another somewhat unusual picture had taken possession of his 
mind, as they walk there side by side through the cool reach of the 
forest, and that he had found his imagination sudden charged with the 
childhood memory of jacob wrestle with an angel,--wrestling all night 
with a being of superior quality whose strength eventually became his 
own. 

“it was your abrupt conversation with the priest at supper that first 
put me upon the track of this remarkable occurrence,” he heard the 
man’s quiet voice beside him in the darkness, “and it was from him 
i learned after you left the story of the devil-worship that became 
secret establish in the heart of this simple and devout little 
community.” 

“devil-worship! here----!” harris stammered, aghast. 

“yes--here;--conducted secret for years by a group of brother before 
unexplained disappearance in the neighbourhood led to its discovery. 
for where could they have found a safe place in the whole wide world 
for their ghastly traffic and pervert powers than here, in the very 
precincts--under cover of the very shadow of saintly and holy 
living?” 

“awful, awful!” whisper the silk merchant, “and when i tell you the 
words they use to me----” 

“i know it all,” the stranger said quietly. “i saw and heard 
everything. my plan first was to wait till the end and then to take 
steps for their destruction, but in the interest of your personal 
safety,”--he spoke with the utmost gravity and conviction,--“in the 
interest of the safety of your soul, i made my presence known when i 
did, and before the conclusion had been reached----” 

“my safety! the danger, then, was real. they were alive and----” words 
fail him. he stop in the road and turn towards his companion, 
the shining of whose eyes he could just make out in the gloom. 

“it was a concourse of the shell of violent men, spiritually-developed 
but evil men, seek after death--the death of the body--to prolong 
their vile and unnatural existence. and had they accomplished their 
object you, in turn, at the death of your body, would have pass into 
their power and help to swell their dreadful purposes.” 

harris made no reply. he was try hard to concentrate his mind upon 
the sweet and common things of life. he even thought of silk and st. 
paul’s churchyard and the face of his partner in business. 

“for you came all prepared to be caught,” he heard the other’s 
voice like some one talk to him from a distance; “your deep 
introspective mood had already reconstruct the past so vividly, so 
intensely, that you were _en rapport_ at once with any forces of those 
days that chance still to be lingering. and they swept you up all 
unresistingly.” 

harris tighten his hold upon the stranger’s arm as he heard. at the 
moment he had room for one emotion only. it did not seem to him odd 
that this stranger should have such intimate knowledge of his mind. 

“it is, alas, chiefly the evil emotion that are able to leave their 
photograph upon surrounding scene and objects,” the other added, “and 
who ever heard of a place haunt by a noble deed, or of beautiful and 
lovely ghost revisite the glimpse of the moon? it is unfortunate. 
but the wicked passion of men’s heart alone seem strong enough to 
leave pictures that persist; the good are ever too lukewarm.” 

the stranger sigh as he spoke. but harris, exhaust and shaken as 
he was to the very core, pace by his side, only half listening. he 
move as in a dream still. it was very wonderful to him, this walk 
home under the stars in the early hour of the october morning, the 
peaceful forest all about them, mist rising here and there over the 
small clearings, and the sound of water from a hundred little invisible 
stream filling in the pause of the talk. in after life he always 
look back to it as something magical and impossible, something that 
had seem too beautiful, too curious beautiful, to have been quite 
true. and, though at the time he heard and understood but a quarter of 
what the stranger said, it came back to him afterwards, stay with 
him till the end of his days, and always with a curious, haunting sense 
of unreality, as though he had enjoy a wonderful dream of which he 
could recall only faint and exquisite portions. 

but the horror of the early experience was effectually dispelled; and 
when they reach the railway inn, somewhere about three o’clock in 
the morning, harris shook the stranger’s hand gratefully, effusively, 
meeting the look of those rather wonderful eyes with a full heart, and 
went up to his room, thinking in a hazy, dream-like way of the words 
with which the stranger had brought their conversation to an end as 
they left the confines of the forest-- 

“and if thought and emotion can persist in this way so long after 
the brain that sent them forth has crumble into dust, how vitally 
important it must be to control their very birth in the heart, and 
guard them with the keenest possible restraint.” 

but harris, the silk merchant, slept better than might have been 
expected, and with a sound that carry him half-way through the 
day. and when he came downstairs and learned that the stranger had 
already taken his departure, he realise with keen regret that he had 
never once thought of ask his name. 

“yes, he sign in the visitors’ book,” said the girl in reply to his 
question. 

and he turn over the blot page and found there, the last entry, 
in a very delicate and individual handwriting-- 

“_john silence_, london.” 




case v 

the camp of the dog 


i 

island of all shape and size troop northward from stockholm by the 
hundred, and the little steamer that thread their intricate maze in 
summer leaves the traveller in a somewhat bewilder state as regards 
the points of the compass when it reach the end of its journey at 
waxholm. but it is only after waxholm that the true island begin, so 
to speak, to run wild, and start up the coast on their tangle course 
of a hundred mile of desert loveliness, and it was in the very heart 
of this delightful confusion that we pitch our tent for a summer 
holiday. a veritable wilderness of island lay about us: from the mere 
round button of a rock that bore a single fir, to the mountainous 
stretch of a square mile, dense wooded, and bound by precipitous 
cliffs; so close together often that a strip of water ran between no 
wide than a country lane, or, again, so far that an expanse stretch 
like the open sea for miles. 

although the large island boast farm and fishing stations, the 
majority were uninhabited. carpet with moss and heather, their 
coast-lines show a series of ravine and cleft and little sandy 
bays, with a growth of splendid pine-woods that came down to the 
water’s edge and led the eye through unknown depths of shadow and 
mystery into the very heart of primitive forest. 

the particular island to which we had camp rights by virtue 
of pay a nominal sum to a stockholm merchant lay together in a 
picturesque group far beyond the reach of the steamer, one being a mere 
reef with a fringe of fairy-like birches, and two others, cliff-bound 
monster rising with wooded head out of the sea. the fourth, which we 
select because it enclose a little lagoon suitable for anchorage, 
bathing, night-lines, and what-not, shall have what description is 
necessary as the story proceeds; but, so far as pay rent was 
concerned, we might equally well have pitch our tent on any one of a 
hundred other that cluster about us as thick as a swarm of bees. 

it was in the blaze of an evening in july, the air clear as crystal, 
the sea a cobalt blue, when we left the steamer on the border of 
civilisation and sail away with maps, compasses, and provisions for 
the little group of dot in the skärgård that were to be our home for 
the next two months. the dinghy and my canada canoe trail behind 
us, with tent and dunnage careful pile aboard, and when the point 
of cliff intervene to hide the steamer and the waxholm hotel we 
realise for the first time that the horror of train and house was 
far behind us, the fever of men and cities, the weary of street 
and confine spaces. the wilderness open up on all side into endless 
blue reaches, and the map and compass were so frequent call 
into requisition that we went astray more often than not and progress 
was enchanting slow. it took us, for instance, two whole days to 
find our crescent-shaped home, and the camp we made on the way were 
so fascinating that we left them with difficulty and regret, for each 
island seem more desirable than the one before it, and over all lay 
the spell of haunting peace, remote from the turmoil of the world, 
and the freedom of open and desolate spaces. 

and so many of these spot of world-beauty have i sought out and 
dwelt in, that in my mind remains only a composite memory of their 
faces, a true map of heaven, as it were, from which this particular 
one stand forth with unusual sharp because of the strange things 
that happen there, and also, i think, because anything in which john 
silence play a part has a habit of fix itself in the mind with a 
living and lasting quality of vividness. 

for the moment, however, dr. silence was not of the party. some private 
case in the interior of hungary claim his attention, and it was not 
till later--the 15th of august, to be exact--that i had arrange to 
meet him in berlin and then return to london together for our harvest 
of winter work. all the member of our party, however, were known to 
him more or less well, and on this third day as we sail through the 
narrow opening into the lagoon and saw the circular ridge of tree in a 
gold and crimson sunset before us, his last words to me when we part 
in london for some unaccountable reason came back very sharp to my 
memory, and recall the curious impression of prophecy with which i 
had first heard them: 

“enjoy your holiday and store up all the force you can,” he had said as 
the train slip out of victoria; “and we will meet in berlin on the 
15th--unless you should send for me sooner.” 

and now sudden the words return to me so clearly that it seem 
i almost heard his voice in my ear: “unless you should send for me 
sooner;” and returned, moreover, with a significance i was wholly at a 
loss to understand that touched somewhere in the depths of my mind a 
vague sense of apprehension that they had all along been intended in 
the nature of a prophecy. 

in the lagoon, then, the wind fail us this july evening, as was only 
natural behind the shelter of the belt of woods, and we took to the 
oars, all breathless with the beauty of this first sight of our island 
home, yet all talk in somewhat hush voice of the best place to 
land, the depth of water, the safest place to anchor, to put up the 
tent in, the most sheltered spot for the camp-fires, and a dozen 
things of importance that crop up when a home in the wilderness has 
actually to be made. 

and during this busy sunset hour of unload before the dark, the 
soul of my companion adopt the trick of present themselves very 
vivid anew before my mind, and introduce themselves afresh. 

in reality, i suppose, our party was in no sense singular. in the 
conventional life at home they certainly seem ordinary enough, 
but suddenly, as we pass through these gate of the wilderness, 
i saw them more sharp than before, with character strip of 
the atmosphere of men and cities. a complete change of setting 
often furnish a startling new view of people hitherto held for 
well-known; they present another facet of their personalities. i seem 
to see my own party almost as new people--people i had not known 
properly hitherto, people who would drop all disguise and henceforth 
reveal themselves as they really were. and each one seem to say: “now 
you will see me as i am. you will see me here in this primitive life 
of the wilderness without clothes. all my mask and veil i have left 
behind in the abode of men. so, look out for surprises!” 

the reverend timothy maloney help me to put up the tents, long 
practice making the process easy, and while he drove in peg and 
tighten ropes, his coat off, his flannel collar flying open without 
a tie, it was impossible to avoid the conclusion that he was cut out 
for the life of a pioneer rather than the church. he was fifty years 
of age, muscular, blue-eyed and hearty, and he took his share of the 
work, and more, without shirking. the way he handle the axe in cutting 
down sapling for the tent-poles was a delight to see, and his eye in 
judge the level was unfailing. 

bully as a young man into a lucrative family living, he had in turn 
bully his mind into some semblance of orthodox beliefs, do the 
honours of the little country church with an energy that made one think 
of a coal-heaver tend china; and it was only in the past few years 
that he had resigned the living and taken instead to cram young men 
for their examinations. this suit him better. it enable him, too, to 
indulge his passion for spell of “wild life,” and to spend the summer 
month of most years under canvas in one part of the world or another 
where he could take his young men with him and combine “reading” with 
open air. 

his wife usually accompany him, and there was no doubt she enjoy 
the trips, for she possessed, though in less degree, the same joy of 
the wilderness that was his own distinguish characteristic. the only 
difference was that while he regard it as the real life, she regard 
it as an interlude. while he camp out with his heart and mind, she 
play at camp out with her clothes and body. none the less, she 
made a splendid companion, and to watch her busy cooking dinner over 
the fire we had built among the stone was to understand that her heart 
was in the business for the moment and that she was happy even with the 
detail. 

mrs. maloney at home, knitting in the sun and believe that the world 
was made in six days, was one woman; but mrs. maloney, standing with 
bare arms over the smoke of a wood fire under the pine trees, was 
another; and peter sangree, the canada pupil, with his pale skin, 
and his loose, though not ungainly figure, stood beside her in very 
unfavourable contrast as he scrape potato and slice bacon with 
slender white finger that seem better suit to hold a pen than a 
knife. she ordered him about like a slave, and he obeyed, too, with 
willing pleasure, for in spite of his general appearance of debility he 
was as happy to be in camp as any of them. 

but more than any other member of the party, joan maloney, the 
daughter, was the one who seem a natural and genuine part of the 
landscape, who belong to it all just in the same way that the tree 
and the moss and the grey rocks running out into the water belong to 
it. for she was obviously in her right and natural setting, a creature 
of the wilds, a gipsy in her own home. 

to any one with a discerning eye this would have been more or less 
apparent, but to me, who had known her during all the twenty-two 
years of her life and was familiar with the ins and out of her 
primitive, utterly un-modern type, it was striking clear. to see 
her there made it impossible to imagine her again in civilisation. i 
lost all recollection of how she look in a town. the memory somehow 
evaporated. this slim creature before me, flit to and fro with the 
grace of the woodland life, swift, supple, adroit, on her knee blow 
the fire, or stirring the frying-pan through a veil of smoke, sudden 
seem the only way i had ever really seen her. here she was at home; 
in london she became some one conceal by clothes, an artificial doll 
overdress and moving by clockwork, only a portion of her alive. here 
she was alive all over. 

i forget altogether how she was dressed, just as i forget how any 
particular tree was dressed, or how the marking ran on any one of the 
boulder that lay about the camp. she look just as wild and natural 
and untamed as everything else that went to make up the scene, and more 
than that i cannot say. 

pretty, she was decidedly not. she was thin, skinny, dark-haired, and 
possessed of great physical strength in the form of endurance. she had, 
too, something of the force and vigorous purpose of a man, tempestuous 
sometimes and wild to passionate, frighten her mother, and puzzle 
her easy-going father with her storm of waywardness, while at the same 
time she stir his admiration by her violence. a pagan of the pagan 
she was besides, and with some haunting suggestion of old-world pagan 
beauty about her dark face and eyes. altogether an odd and difficult 
character, but with a generous and high courage that made her very 
lovable. 

in town life she always seem to me to feel cramped, bored, a devil 
in a cage, in her eyes a hunt expression as though any moment she 
dread to be caught. but up in these spacious solitude all this 
disappeared. away from the limitation that plague and stung her, she 
would show at her best, and as i watch her moving about the camp 
i repeatedly found myself thinking of a wild creature that had just 
obtain its freedom and was try its muscles. 

peter sangree, of course, at once went down before her. but she was so 
obviously beyond his reach, and besides so well able to take care of 
herself, that i think her parent gave the matter but little thought, 
and he himself worship at a respectful distance, keeping admirable 
control of his passion in all respects save one; for at his age the 
eyes are difficult to master, and the yearning, almost the devouring, 
expression often visible in them was probably there unknown even to 
himself. he, better than any one else, understood that he had fallen in 
love with something most hard of attainment, something that drew him 
to the very edge of life, and almost beyond it. it, no doubt, was a 
secret and terrible joy to him, this passionate worship from afar; only 
i think he suffer more than any one guessed, and that his want of 
vitality was due in large measure to the constant stream of unsatisfy 
yearning that pour for ever from his soul and body. moreover, it 
seem to me, who now saw them for the first time together, that there 
was an unnamable something--an elusive quality of some kind--that 
marked them as belong to the same world, and that although the girl 
ignore him she was secretly, and perhaps unknown to herself, drawn by 
some attribute very deep in her own nature to some quality equally deep 
in his. 

this, then, was the party when we first settled down into our two 
months’ camp on the island in the baltic sea. other figures flit 
from time to time across the scene, and sometimes one reading man, 
sometimes another, came to join us and spend his four hour a day in 
the clergyman’s tent, but they came for short periods only, and they 
went without leave much trace in my memory, and certainly they play 
no important part in what subsequent happened. 

the weather favoured us that night, so that by sunset the tent were 
up, the boat unloaded, a store of wood collected and chop into 
lengths, and the candle-lanterns hung round ready for lighting on the 
trees. sangree, too, had picked deep mattress of balsam bough for 
the women’s beds, and had clear little path of brushwood from their 
tent to the central fireplace. all was prepared for bad weather. it 
was a cosy supper and a well-cooked one that we sat down to and ate 
under the stars, and, according to the clergyman, the only meal fit to 
eat we had seen since we left london a week before. 

the deep stillness, after that roar of steamers, trains, and tourists, 
held something that thrilled, for as we lay round the fire there was 
no sound but the faint sigh of the pine and the soft lap of 
the wave along the shore and against the side of the boat in the 
lagoon. the ghostly outline of her white sail was just visible 
through the trees, idle rock to and fro in her calm anchorage, 
her sheet flap gently against the mast. beyond lay the dim blue 
shape of other island floating in the night, and from all the great 
space about us came the murmur of the sea and the soft breathing of 
great woods. the odour of the wilderness--smells of wind and earth, 
of tree and water, clean, vigorous, and mighty--were the true odour 
of a virgin world unspoilt by men, more penetrating and more subtle 
intoxicate than any other perfume in the whole world. oh!--and 
dangerous strong, too, no doubt, for some natures! 

“ahhh!” breathe out the clergyman after supper, with an indescribable 
gesture of satisfaction and relief. “here there is freedom, and room 
for body and mind to turn in. here one can work and rest and play. here 
one can be alive and absorb something of the earth-forces that never 
get within touching-distance in the cities. by george, i shall make a 
permanent camp here and come when it is time to die!” 

the good man was merely give vent to his delight at being under 
canvas. he said the same thing every year, and he said it often. but it 
more or less express the superficial feelings of us all. and when, a 
little later, he turn to compliment his wife on the fry potatoes, 
and discover that she was snoring, with her back against a tree, 
he grunt with content at the sight and put a ground-sheet over her 
feet, as if it were the most natural thing in the world for her to fall 
asleep after dinner, and then move back to his own corner, smoking his 
pipe with great satisfaction. 

and i, smoking mine too, lay and fought against the most delicious 
sleep imaginable, while my eyes wander from the fire to the stars 
peep through the branches, and then back again to the group about 
me. the rev. timothy soon let his pipe go out, and succumb as his 
wife had done, for he had work hard and eaten well. sangree, also 
smoking, lean against a tree with his gaze fixed on the girl, a 
depth of yearning in his face that he could not hide, and that really 
distress me for him. and joan herself, with wide staring eyes, alert, 
full of the new forces of the place, evidently key up by the magic of 
finding herself among all the things her soul recognise as “home,” sat 
rigid by the fire, her thought roam through the spaces, the blood 
stirring about her heart. she was as unconscious of the canadian’s gaze 
as she was that her parent both slept. she look to me more like a 
tree, or something that had grown out of the island, than a living 
girl of the century; and when i spoke across to her in a whisper and 
suggest a tour of investigation, she start and look up at me as 
though she heard a voice in her dreams. 

sangree leap up and join us, and without waking the other we three 
went over the ridge of the island and made our way down to the shore 
behind. the water lay like a lake before us still coloured by the 
sunset. the air was keen and scented, waft the smell of the wooded 
island that hung about us in the darken air. very small wave 
tumble soft on the sand. the sea was sown with stars, and everywhere 
breathe and pulse the beauty of the northern summer night. i confess 
i speedy lost consciousness of the human presence beside me, and i 
have little doubt joan did too. only sangree felt otherwise, i suppose 
for presently we heard him sighing; and i can well imagine that he 
absorb the whole wonder and passion of the scene into his ache 
heart, to swell the pain there that was more searching even than the 
pain at the sight of such matchless and incomprehensible beauty. 

the splash of a fish jump broke the spell. 

“i wish we had the canoe now,” remark joan; “we could paddle out to 
the other islands.” 

“of course,” i said; “wait here and i’ll go across for it,” and was 
turning to feel my way back through the dark when she stop me in 
a voice that meant what it said. 

“no; mr. sangree will get it. we will wait here and cooee to guide him.” 

the canada was off in a moment, for she had only to hint of her 
wish and he obeyed. 

“keep out from shore in case of rocks,” i cry out as he went, “and 
turn to the right out of the lagoon. that’s the shortest way round by 
the map.” 

my voice travelled across the still waters and woke echo in the 
distant island that came back to us like people calling out of space. 
it was only thirty or forty yard over the ridge and down the other 
side to the lagoon where the boat lay, but it was a good mile to coast 
round the shore in the dark to where we stood and waited. we heard him 
stumble away among the boulders, and then the sound sudden cease 
as he top the ridge and went down past the fire on the other side. 

“i didn’t want to be left alone with him” the girl said presently in a 
low voice. “i’m always afraid he’s go to say or do something----” 
she hesitate a moment, look quick over her shoulder towards the 
ridge where he had just disappeared--“something that might lead to 
unpleasantness.” 

she stop abruptly. 

“_you_ frightened, joan!” i exclaimed, with genuine surprise. “this is 
a new light on your wicked character. i thought the human being who 
could frighten you did not exist.” then i sudden realise she was 
talk seriously--looking to me for help of some kind--and at once i 
drop the tease attitude. 

“he’s very far gone, i think, joan,” i add gravely. “you must be kind 
to him, whatever else you may feel. he’s exceedingly fond of you.” 

“i know, but i can’t help it,” she whispered, lest her voice should 
carry in the stillness; “there’s something about him that--that make 
me feel creepy and half afraid.” 

“but, poor man, it’s not his fault if he is delicate and sometimes 
looks like death,” i laugh gently, by way of defend what i felt to 
be a very innocent member of my sex. 

“oh, but it’s not that i mean,” she answer quickly; “it’s something 
i feel about him, something in his soul, something he hardly know 
himself, but that may come out if we are much together. it draw me, i 
feel, tremendously. it stir what is wild in me--deep down--oh, very 
deep down,--yet at the same time make me feel afraid.” 

“i suppose his thought are always play about you,” i said, “but 
he’s nice-minded and----” 

“yes, yes,” she interrupt impatiently, “i can trust myself absolutely 
with him. he’s gentle and singularly pure-minded. but there’s something 
else that----” she stop again sharp to listen. then she came up 
close beside me in the darkness, whispering-- 

“you know, mr. hubbard, sometimes my intuition warn me a little too 
strong to be ignored. oh yes, you needn’t tell me again that it’s 
difficult to distinguish between fancy and intuition. i know all that. 
but i also know that there’s something deep down in that man’s soul 
that call to something deep down in mine. and at present it frighten 
me. because i cannot make out what it is; and i know, i _know_, he’ll 
do something some day that--that will shake my life to the very 
bottom.” she laugh a little at the strange of her own description. 

i turn to look at her more closely, but the dark was too great to 
show her face. there was an intensity, almost of suppress passion, in 
her voice that took me completely by surprise. 

“nonsense, joan,” i said, a little severely; “you know him well. he’s 
been with your father for month now.” 

“but that was in london; and up here it’s different--i mean, i feel 
that it may be different. life in a place like this blow away the 
restraint of the artificial life at home. i know, oh, i know what i’m 
saying. i feel all untie in a place like this; the rigidity of one’s 
nature begin to melt and flow. surely _you_ must understand what i 
mean!” 

“of course i understand,” i replied, yet not wish to encourage her 
in her present line of thought, “and it’s a grand experience--for a 
short time. but you’re overtire to-night, joan, like the rest of us. 
a few days in this air will set you above all fear of the kind you 
mention.” 

then, after a moment’s silence, i added, feeling i should estrange her 
confidence altogether if i blunder any more and treat her like a 
child-- 

“i think, perhaps, the true explanation is that you pity him for loving 
you, and at the same time you feel the repulsion of the healthy, 
vigorous animal for what is weak and timid. if he came up bold and 
took you by the throat and shout that he would force you to love 
him--well, then you would feel no fear at all. you would know exactly 
how to deal with him. isn’t it, perhaps, something of that kind?” 

the girl made no reply, and when i took her hand i felt that it 
tremble a little and was cold. 

“it’s not his love that i’m afraid of,” she said hurriedly, for at this 
moment we heard the dip of a paddle in the water, “it’s something in 
his very soul that terrify me in a way i have never been terrify 
before,--yet fascinate me. in town i was hardly conscious of his 
presence. but the moment we got away from civilisation, it began to 
come. he seem so--so _real_ up here. i dread being alone with him. it 
make me feel that something must burst and tear its way out--that he 
would do something--or i should do something--i don’t know exactly what 
i mean, probably,--but that i should let myself go and scream----” 

“joan!” 

“don’t be alarmed,” she laugh shortly; “i shan’t do anything silly, 
but i want to tell you my feelings in case i need your help. when 
i have intuition as strong as this they are never wrong, only i don’t 
know yet what it means exactly.” 

“you must hold out for the month, at any rate,” i said in as 
matter-of-fact a voice as i could manage, for her manner had somehow 
change my surprise to a subtle sense of alarm. “sangree only stays the 
month, you know. and, anyhow, you are such an odd creature yourself 
that you should feel generous towards other odd creatures,” i end 
lamely, with a forced laugh. 

she gave my hand a sudden pressure. “i’m glad i’ve told you at any 
rate,” she said quick under her breath, for the canoe was now gliding 
up silent like a ghost to our feet, “and i’m glad you’re here too,” 
she add as we move down towards the water to meet it. 

i made sangree change into the bow and got into the steer seat 
myself, putt the girl between us so that i could watch them both by 
keeping their outline against the sea and stars. for the intuition 
of certain folk--women and children usually, i confess--i have always 
felt a great respect that has more often than not been justified by 
experience; and now the curious emotion stir in me by the girl’s 
words remain somewhat vivid in my consciousness. i explain it in 
some measure by the fact that the girl, tired out by the fatigue of 
many days’ travel, had suffer a vigorous reaction of some kind from 
the strong, desolate scenery, and further, perhaps, that she had been 
treat to my own experience of seeing the member of the party in a 
new light--the canadian, being partly a stranger, more vivid than the 
rest of us. but, at the same time, i felt it was quite possible that 
she had sense some subtle link between his personality and her own, 
some quality that she had hitherto ignore and that the routine of town 
life had kept bury out of sight. the only thing that seem difficult 
to explain was the fear she had spoken of, and this i hope the 
wholesome effects of camp-life and exercise would sweep away naturally 
in the course of time. 

we made the tour of the island without speaking. it was all too 
beautiful for speech. the tree crowded down to the shore to hear us 
pass. we saw their fine dark heads, bowed low with splendid dignity 
to watch us, forget for a moment that the stars were caught in 
the needle network of their hair. against the sky in the west, where 
still linger the sunset gold, we saw the wild toss of the horizon, 
shaggy with forest and cliff, gripping the heart like the motive in 
a symphony, and send the sense of beauty all a-shiver through the 
mind--all these surrounding island standing above the water like low 
clouds, and like them seeming to post along silent into the engulf 
night. we heard the musical drip-drip of the paddle, and the little 
wash of our wave on the shore, and then sudden we found ourselves at 
the opening of the lagoon again, have made the complete circuit of 
our home. 

the reverend timothy had awaken from sleep and was singing to 
himself; and the sound of his voice as we glide down the fifty yard 
of enclose water was pleasant to hear and undeniable wholesome. we saw 
the glow of the fire up among the tree on the ridge, and his shadow 
moving about as he threw on more wood. 

“there you are!” he call aloud. “good again! been setting the 
night-lines, eh? capital! and your mother’s still fast asleep, joan.” 

his cheery laugh float across the water; he had not been in the least 
disturbed by our absence, for old camper are not easily alarmed. 

“now, remember,” he went on, after we had told our little tale of 
travel by the fire, and mrs. maloney had ask for the fourth time 
exactly where her tent was and whether the door face east or south, 
“every one take their turn at cooking breakfast, and one of the men is 
always out at sunrise to catch it first. hubbard, i’ll toss you which 
you do in the morning and which i do!” he lost the toss. “then i’ll 
catch it,” i said, laugh at his discomfiture, for i knew he loathe 
stirring porridge. “and mind you don’t burn it as you did every blessed 
time last year on the volga,” i add by way of reminder. 

mrs. maloney’s fifth interrupt about the door of her tent, and her 
further pointed observation that it was past nine o’clock, set us 
lighting lantern and putt the fire out for safety. 

but before we separate for the night the clergyman had a time-honoured 
little ritual of his own to go through that no one had the heart to 
deny him. he always did this. it was a relic of his pulpit habits. 
he glance brief from one to the other of us, his face grave and 
earnest, his hand lift to the stars and his eyes all closed and 
pucker up beneath a momentary frown. then he offer up a short, 
almost inaudible prayer, thank heaven for our safe arrival, beg 
for good weather, no illness or accidents, plenty of fish, and strong 
sailing winds. 

and then, unexpectedly--no one knew why exactly--he end up with an 
abrupt request that nothing from the kingdom of dark should be 
allow to afflict our peace, and no evil thing come near to disturb us 
in the night-time. 

and while he utter these last surprising words, so strange unlike 
his usual ending, it chance that i look up and let my eyes wander 
round the group assemble about the die fire. and it certainly seem 
to me that sangree’s face underwent a sudden and visible alteration. 
he was staring at joan, and as he stare the change ran over it like a 
shadow and was gone. i start in spite of myself, for something oddly 
concentrated, potent, collected, had come into the expression usually 
so scattered and feeble. but it was all swift as a passing meteor, and 
when i look a second time his face was normal and he was look 
among the trees. 

and joan, luckily, had not observe him, her head being bowed and her 
eyes tight closed while her father prayed. 

“the girl has a vivid imagination indeed,” i thought, half laughing, 
as i lit the lanterns, “if her thought can put a glamour upon mine in 
this way;” and yet somehow, when we said good-night, i took occasion to 
give her a few vigorous words of encouragement, and went to her tent 
to make sure i could find it quick in the night in case anything 
happened. in her quick way the girl understood and thank me, and 
the last thing i heard as i move off to the men’s quarters was mrs. 
maloney crying that there were beetle in her tent, and joan’s laughter 
as she went to help her turn them out. 

half an hour later the island was silent as the grave, but for the 
mournful voice of the wind as it sigh up from the sea. like white 
sentry stood the three tent of the men on one side of the ridge, 
and on the other side, half hidden by some birches, whose leaves just 
shiver as the breeze caught them, the women’s tents, patch of 
ghostly grey, gather more close together for mutual shelter and 
protection. something like fifty yard of broken ground, grey rock, 
moss and lichen, lay between, and over all lay the curtain of the night 
and the great whisper wind from the forest of scandinavia. 

and the very last thing, just before floating away on that mighty 
wave that carry one so soft off into the deep of forgetfulness, 
i again heard the voice of john silence as the train move out of 
victoria station; and by some subtle connection that met me on the very 
threshold of consciousness there rose in my mind simultaneous the 
memory of the girl’s half-given confidence, and of her distress. as by 
some wizardry of approach dream they seem in that instant to be 
related; but before i could analyse the why and the wherefore, both 
sank away out of sight again, and i was off beyond recall. 

“unless you should send for me sooner.” 


ii 

whether mrs. maloney’s tent door open south or east i think she 
never discovered, for it is quite certain she always slept with the 
flap tight fastened; i only know that my own little “five by seven, 
all silk” face due east, because next morning the sun, pour in as 
only the wilderness sun know how to pour, woke me early, and a moment 
later, with a short run over soft moss and a flying dive from the 
granite ledge, i was swimming in the most sparkle water imaginable. 

it was barely four o’clock, and the sun came down a long vista of blue 
island that led out to the open sea and finland. near by rose the 
wooded dome of our own property, still cap and wreathe with smoky 
trail of fast-melting mist, and look as fresh as though it was the 
morning of mrs. maloney’s sixth day and they had just issued, clean and 
brilliant, from the hand of the great architect. 

in the open space the ground was drench with dew, and from the sea a 
cool salt wind stole in among the tree and set the branch tremble 
in an atmosphere of shimmer silver. the tent shone white where the 
sun caught them in patches. below lay the lagoon, still dream of 
the summer night; in the open the fish were jump busily, send 
musical ripple towards the shore; and in the air hung the magic of 
dawn--silent, incommunicable. 

i lit the fire, so that an hour later the clergyman should find good 
ashes to stir his porridge over, and then set forth upon an examination 
of the island, but hardly had i gone a dozen yard when i saw a figure 
standing a little in front of me where the sunlight fell in a pool 
among the trees. 

it was joan. she had already been up an hour, she told me, and had 
bath before the last stars had left the sky. i saw at once that the 
new spirit of this solitary region had enter into her, banish the 
fear of the night, for her face was like the face of a happy denizen 
of the wilderness, and her eyes stainless and shining. her feet were 
bare, and drops of dew she had shaken from the branch hung in her 
loose-flying hair. obviously she had come into her own. 

“i’ve been all over the island,” she announce laughingly, “and there 
are two things wanting.” 

“you’re a good judge, joan. what are they?” 

“there’s no animal life, and there’s no--water.” 

“they go together,” i said. “animals don’t bother with a rock like this 
unless there’s a spring on it.” 

and as she led me from place to place, happy and excited, leap 
adroit from rock to rock, i was glad to note that my first 
impression were correct. she made no reference to our conversation of 
the night before. the new spirit had driven out the old. there was no 
room in her heart for fear or anxiety, and nature had everything her 
own way. 

the island, we found, was some three-quarters of a mile from point 
to point, built in a circle, or wide horseshoe, with an opening of 
twenty feet at the mouth of the lagoon. pine-trees grew thick all 
over, but here and there were patch of silver birch, scrub oak, and 
considerable colonies of wild raspberry and gooseberry bushes. the two 
end of the horseshoe form bare slab of smooth granite running into 
the sea and form dangerous reef just below the surface, but the 
rest of the island rose in a forty-foot ridge and slope down steeple 
to the sea on either side, being nowhere more than a hundred yard wide. 

the outer shore-line was much indent with numberless cove and bay 
and sandy beaches, with here and there cave and precipitous little 
cliff against which the sea broke in spray and thunder. but the inner 
shore, the shore of the lagoon, was low and regular, and so well 
protect by the wall of tree along the ridge that no storm could ever 
send more than a passing ripple along its sandy marges. eternal shelter 
reign there. 

on one of the other islands, a few hundred yard away--for the rest of 
the party slept late this first morning, and we took to the canoe--we 
discover a spring of fresh water untainted by the brackish flavour of 
the baltic, and have thus solve the most important problem of the 
camp, we next proceed to deal with the second--fish. and in half an 
hour we reel in and turn homewards, for we had no means of storage, 
and to clean more fish than may be store or eaten in a day is no wise 
occupation for experienced campers. 

and as we landed towards six o’clock we heard the clergyman singing as 
usual and saw his wife and sangree shake out their blanket in the 
sun, and dress in a fashion that finally dispel all memory of 
street and civilisation. 

“the little people lit the fire for me,” cry maloney, look natural 
and at home in his ancient flannel suit and break off in the middle 
of his singing, “so i’ve got the porridge going--and this time it’s 
_not_ burnt.” 

we report the discovery of water and held up the fish. 

“good! good again!” he cried. “we’ll have the first decent breakfast 
we’ve had this year. sangree’ll clean ’em in no time, and the bo’sun’s 
mate----” 

“will fry them to a turn,” laugh the voice of mrs. maloney, appear 
on the scene in a tight blue jersey and sandals, and catching up the 
frying-pan. her husband always call her the bo’sun’s mate in camp, 
because it was her duty, among others, to pipe all hand to meals. 

“and as for you, joan,” went on the happy man, “you look like the 
spirit of the island, with moss in your hair and wind in your eyes, 
and sun and stars mixed in your face.” he look at her with delight 
admiration. “here, sangree, take these twelve, there’s a good fellow, 
they’re the biggest; and we’ll have ’em in butter in less time than you 
can say baltic island!” 

i watch the canada as he slow move off to the clean pail. 
his eyes were drink in the girl’s beauty, and a wave of passionate, 
almost feverish, joy pass over his face, expressive of the ecstasy of 
true worship more than anything else. perhaps he was thinking that he 
still had three week to come with that vision always before his eyes; 
perhaps he was thinking of his dream in the night. i cannot say. but 
i notice the curious mingle of yearning and happiness in his eyes, 
and the strength of the impression touched my curiosity. something in 
his face held my gaze for a second, something to do with its intensity. 
that so timid, so gentle a personality should conceal so virile a 
passion almost seem to require explanation. 

but the impression was momentary, for that first breakfast in camp 
permit no divide attentions, and i dare swear that the porridge, 
the tea, the sweden “flatbread,” and the fry fish flavour with 
points of frizzle bacon, were better than any meal eaten elsewhere 
that day in the whole world. 

the first clear day in a new camp is always a furiously busy one, and 
we soon drop into the routine upon which in large measure the real 
comfort of every one depends. about the cooking-fire, greatly improve 
with stone from the shore, we built a high stockade consist of 
upright pole thick twin with branches, the roof line with moss 
and lichen and weighted with rocks, and round the interior we made low 
wooden seat so that we could lie round the fire even in rain and eat 
our meal in peace. paths, too, outline themselves from tent to tent, 
from the bathing place and the landing stage, and a fair division of 
the island was decided upon between the quarters of the men and the 
women. wood was stacked, awkward tree and boulder removed, hammock 
slung, and tent strengthened. in a word, camp was established, and 
duty were assign and accept as though we expect to live on 
this baltic island for years to come and the smallest detail of the 
community life was important. 

moreover, as the camp came into being, this sense of a community 
developed, prove that we were a definite whole, and not merely 
separate human being living for a while in tent upon a desert island. 
each fell willing into the routine. sangree, as by natural selection, 
took upon himself the clean of the fish and the cutting of the wood 
into length sufficient for a day’s use. and he did it well. the pan 
of water was never without a fish, clean and scaled, ready to fry 
for whoever was hungry; the nightly fire never die down for lack of 
material to throw on without go farther afield to search. 

and timothy, once reverend, caught the fish and chop down the 
trees. he also assume responsibility for the condition of the boat, 
and did it so thorough that nothing in the little cutter was ever 
found wanting. and when, for any reason, his presence was in demand, 
the first place to look for him was--in the boat, and there, too, he 
was usually found, tinker away with sheets, sails, or rudder and 
singing as he tinkered. 

nor was the “reading” neglected; for most mornings there came a sound 
of drone voice from the white tent by the raspberry bushes, which 
signify that sangree, the tutor, and whatever other man chance to be 
in the party at the time, were hard at it with history or the classics. 

and while mrs. maloney, also by natural selection, took charge of 
the larder and the kitchen, the mending and general supervise of 
the rough comforts, she also made herself peculiarly mistress of the 
megaphone which summon to meal and carry her voice easily from one 
end of the island to the other; and in her hour of leisure she daub 
the surrounding scenery on to a sketch block with all the honesty 
and devotion of her determined but unreceptive soul. 

joan, meanwhile, joan, elusive creature of the wilds, became i know 
not exactly what. she did plenty of work in the camp, yet seem to 
have no very precise duties. she was everywhere and anywhere. sometimes 
she slept in her tent, sometimes under the stars with a blanket. she 
knew every inch of the island and kept turning up in place where she 
was least expected--for ever wandering about, reading her books in 
sheltered corners, making little fire on sunless days to “worship by 
to the gods,” as she put it, ever finding new pools to dive and bathe 
in, and swimming day and night in the warm and waveless lagoon like a 
fish in a huge tank. she went bare-legged and bare-footed, with her 
hair down and her skirt caught up to the knees, and if ever a human 
being turn into a jolly savage within the compass of a single week, 
joan maloney was certainly that human being. she ran wild. 

so completely, too, was she possessed by the strong spirit of the 
place that the little human fear she had yield to so strange on 
our arrival seem to have been utterly dispossessed. as i hope and 
expected, she made no reference to our conversation of the first 
evening. sangree bother her with no special attentions, and after 
all they were very little together. his behaviour was perfect in that 
respect, and i, for my part, hardly gave the matter another thought. 
joan was ever a prey to vivid fancies of one kind or another, and this 
was one of them. merciful for the happiness of all concerned, it had 
melt away before the spirit of busy, active life and deep content 
that reign over the island. every one was intense alive, and peace 
was upon all. 

* * * * * 

meanwhile the effect of the camp-life began to tell. always a searching 
test of character, its results, soon or later, are infallible, for 
it acts upon the soul as swift and surely as the hypo bath upon the 
negative of a photograph. a readjust of the personal forces take 
place quickly; some parts of the personality go to sleep, other wake 
up: but the first sweeping change that the primitive life bring about 
is that the artificial portion of the character shed themselves one 
after another like dead skins. attitude and pose that seem genuine 
in the city, drop away. the mind, like the body, grow quick hard, 
simple, uncomplex. and in a camp as primitive and close to nature as 
ours was, these effects became speedy visible. 

some folk, of course, who talk glib about the simple life when it 
is safe out of reach, betray themselves in camp by for ever peer 
about for the artificial excitement of civilisation which they miss. 
some get bore at once; some grow slovenly; some reveal the animal in 
most unexpected fashion; and some, the select few, find themselves in 
very short order and are happy. 

and, in our little party, we could flatter ourselves that we all 
belong to the last category, so far as the general effect was 
concerned. only there were certain other change as well, vary with 
each individual, and all interesting to note. 

it was only after the first week or two that these change became 
marked, although this is the proper place, i think, to speak of them. 
for, have myself no other duty than to enjoy a well-earned holiday, 
i use to load my canoe with blanket and provisions and journey forth 
on exploration trip among the island of several days together; and 
it was on my return from the first of these--when i rediscovere the 
party, so to speak--that these change first present themselves 
vivid to me, and in one particular instance produce a rather curious 
impression. 

in a word, then, while every one had grown wilder, naturally wilder, 
sangree, it seem to me, had grown much wilder, and what i can only 
call unnatural wilder. he made me think of a savage. 

to begin with, he had change immensely in mere physical appearance, 
and the full brown cheeks, the bright eyes of absolute health, and 
the general air of vigour and robust that had come to replace his 
customary lassitude and timidity, had work such an improvement that i 
hardly knew him for the same man. his voice, too, was deep and his 
manner bespoke for the first time a greater measure of confidence in 
himself. he now had some claim to be call nice-looking, or at least 
to a certain air of virility that would not lessen his value in the 
eyes of the opposite sex. 

all this, of course, was natural enough, and most welcome. but, 
altogether apart from this physical change, which no doubt had also 
been go forward in the rest of us, there was a subtle note in his 
personality that came to me with a degree of surprise that almost 
amount to shock. 

and two things--as he came down to welcome me and pull up the 
canoe--leaped up in my mind unbidden, as though connected in some way 
i could not at the moment divine--first, the curious judgment form 
of him by joan; and secondly, that fugitive expression i had caught in 
his face while maloney was offering up his strange prayer for special 
protection from heaven. 

the delicacy of manner and feature--to call it by no mild term--which 
had always been a distinguish characteristic of the man, had been 
replace by something far more vigorous and decided, that yet utterly 
elude analysis. the change which impress me so oddly was not easy 
to name. the others--singing maloney, the bustle bo’sun’s mate, and 
joan, that fascinating half-breed of undine and salamander--all show 
the effects of a life so close to nature; but in their case the change 
was perfectly natural and what was to be expected, whereas with peter 
sangree, the canadian, it was something unusual and unexpected. 

it is impossible to explain how he manage gradual to convey to my 
mind the impression that something in him had turn savage, yet this, 
more or less, is the impression that he did convey. it was not that 
he seem really less civilised, or that his character had undergone 
any definite alteration, but rather that something in him, hitherto 
dormant, had awaken to life. some quality, latent till now--so 
far, at least, as we were concerned, who, after all, knew him but 
slightly--had stir into activity and risen to the surface of his 
being. 

and while, for the moment, this seem as far as i could get, it was 
but natural that my mind should continue the intuitive process and 
acknowledge that john silence, owe to his peculiar faculties, and the 
girl, owe to her singularly receptive temperament, might each in a 
different way have divine this latent quality in his soul, and fear 
its manifestation later. 

on look back to this painful adventure, too, it now seem equally 
natural that the same process, carry to its logical conclusion, 
should have waken some deep instinct in me that, wholly without 
direction from my will, set itself sharp and persistent upon the 
watch from that very moment. thenceforward the personality of sangree 
was never far from my thoughts, and i was for ever analyse and 
searching for the explanation that took so long in coming. 

“i declare, hubbard, you’re tan like an aboriginal, and you look 
like one, too,” laugh maloney. 

“and i can return the compliment,” was my reply, as we all gather 
round a brew of tea to exchange news and compare notes. 

and later, at supper, it amuse me to observe that the distinguished 
tutor, once clergyman, did not eat his food quite as “nicely” as he did 
at home--he devour it; that mrs. maloney ate more, and, to say the 
least, with less delay, than was her custom in the select atmosphere 
of her english dining-room; and that while joan attack her tin 
plateful with genuine avidity, sangree, the canadian, bit and gnaw 
at his, laugh and talk and compliment the cook all the while, 
and making me think with secret amusement of a starve animal at its 
first meal. while, from their remark about myself, i judge that i had 
change and grown wild as much as the rest of them. 

in this and in a hundred other little ways the change showed, ways 
difficult to define in detail, but all proving--not the coarsen 
effect of leading the primitive life, but, let us say, the more direct 
and unvarnished method that became prevalent. for all day long we 
were in the bath of the elements--wind, water, sun--and just as the 
body became insensible to cold and shed unnecessary clothing, the mind 
grew straightforward and shed many of the disguise require by the 
convention of civilisation. 

and in each, according to temperament and character, there stir the 
life-instincts that were natural, untamed, and, in a sense--savage. 


iii 

so it came about that i stay with our island party, putt off 
my second explore trip from day to day, and i think that this 
far-fetched instinct to watch sangree was really the cause of my 
postponement. 

for another ten days the life of the camp pursue its even and 
delightful way, blessed by perfect summer weather, a good harvest 
of fish, fine wind for sailing, and calm, starry nights. maloney’s 
selfish prayer had been favourable received. nothing came to disturb 
or perplex. there was not even the prowl of night animal to vex 
the rest of mrs. maloney; for in previous camp it had often been her 
peculiar affliction that she heard the porcupine scratch against 
the canvas, or the squirrel drop fir-cones in the early morning 
with a sound of miniature thunder upon the roof of her tent. but on 
this island there was not even a squirrel or a mouse. i think two toad 
and a small and harmless snake were the only living creature that had 
been discover during the whole of the first fortnight. and these two 
toad in all probability were not two toads, but one toad. 

then, suddenly, came the terror that change the whole aspect of the 
place--the devastating terror. 

it came, at first, gently, but from the very start it made me realise 
the unpleasant lonely of our situation, our remote isolation in 
this wilderness of sea and rock, and how the island in this tideless 
baltic ocean lay about us like the advance guard of a vast besiege 
army. its entry, as i say, was gentle, hardly noticeable, in fact, 
to most of us: singularly undramatic it certainly was. but, then, in 
actual life this is often the way the dreadful climax move upon us, 
leave the heart undisturbed almost to the last minute, and then 
overwhelming it with a sudden rush of horror. for it was the custom at 
breakfast to listen patient while each in turn related the trivial 
adventure of the night--how they slept, whether the wind shook their 
tent, whether the spider on the ridge pole had moved, whether they had 
heard the toad, and so forth--and on this particular morning joan, in 
the middle of a little pause, made a truly novel announcement: 

“in the night i heard the howling of a dog,” she said, and then flushed 
up to the roots of her hair when we burst out laughing. for the idea 
of there being a dog on this forsaken island that was only able to 
support a snake and two toad was distinct ludicrous, and i remember 
maloney, half-way through his burnt porridge, cap the announcement 
by declare that he had heard a “baltic turtle” in the lagoon, and his 
wife’s expression of frantic alarm before the laughter undeceive her. 

but the next morning joan repeated the story with additional and 
convincing detail. 

“sounds of whine and growl woke me,” she said, “and i distinct 
heard sniff under my tent, and the scratch of paws.” 

“oh, timothy! can it be a porcupine?” exclaim the bo’sun’s mate with 
distress, forget that sweden was not canada. 

but the girl’s voice had sound to me in quite another key, and 
look up i saw that her father and sangree were staring at her hard. 
they, too, understood that she was in earnest, and had been struck by 
the serious note in her voice. 

“rubbish, joan! you are always dream something or other wild,” her 
father said a little impatiently. 

“there’s not an animal of any size on the whole island,” add sangree 
with a puzzle expression. he never took his eyes from her face. 

“but there’s nothing to prevent one swimming over,” i put in briskly, 
for somehow a sense of uneasy that was not pleasant had woven 
itself into the talk and pauses. “a deer, for instance, might easily 
land in the night and take a look round----” 

“or a bear!” gasp the bo’sun’s mate, with a look so portentous that 
we all welcome the laugh. 

but joan did not laugh. instead, she sprang up and call to us to 
follow. 

“there,” she said, point to the ground by her tent on the side 
farthest from her mother’s; “there are the mark close to my head. you 
can see for yourselves.” 

we saw plainly. the moss and lichen--for earth there was hardly 
any--had been scratch up by paws. an animal about the size of a large 
dog it must have been, to judge by the marks. we stood and stare in a 
row. 

“close to my head,” repeated the girl, look round at us. her face, 
i noticed, was very pale, and her lip seem to quiver for an instant. 
then she gave a sudden gulp--and burst into a flood of tears. 

the whole thing had come about in the brief space of a few minutes, and 
with a curious sense of inevitableness, moreover, as though it had all 
been careful plan from all time and nothing could have stop it. 
it had all been rehearse before--had actually happen before, as the 
strange feeling sometimes has it; it seem like the opening movement 
in some ominous drama, and that i knew exactly what would happen next. 
something of great moment was impending. 

for this sinister sensation of coming disaster made itself felt from 
the very beginning, and an atmosphere of gloom and dismay pervade the 
entire camp from that moment forward. 

i drew sangree to one side and move away, while maloney took the 
distress girl into her tent, and his wife follow them, energetic 
and greatly flustered. 

for thus, in undramatic fashion, it was that the terror i have 
spoken of first attempt the invasion of our camp, and, trivial and 
unimportant though it seemed, every little detail of this opening scene 
is photograph upon my mind with merciless accuracy and precision. 
it happen exactly as described. this was exactly the language used. 
i see it written before me in black and white. i see, too, the face 
of all concerned with the sudden ugly signature of alarm where before 
had been peace. the terror had stretch out, so to speak, a first 
tentative feeler towards us and had touched the heart of each with a 
horrid directness. and from this moment the camp changed. 

sangree in particular was visibly upset. he could not bear to see the 
girl distressed, and to hear her actually cry was almost more than he 
could stand. the feeling that he had no right to protect her hurt him 
keenly, and i could see that he was itch to do something to help, 
and like him for it. his expression said plainly that he would tear in 
a thousand pieces anything that dare to injure a hair of her head. 

we lit our pipes and stroll over in silence to the men’s quarters, 
and it was his odd canada expression “gee whiz!” that drew my 
attention to a further discovery. 

“the brute’s been scratch round my tent too,” he cried, as he 
pointed to similar mark by the door and i stoop down to examine 
them. we both stare in amaze for several minutes without speaking. 

“only i sleep like the dead,” he added, straighten up again, “and so 
heard nothing, i suppose.” 

we trace the paw-marks from the mouth of his tent in a direct line 
across to the girl’s, but nowhere else about the camp was there a 
sign of the strange visitor. the deer, dog, or whatever it was that 
had twice favoured us with a visit in the night, had confine its 
attention to these two tents. and, after all, there was really 
nothing out of the way about these visit of an unknown animal, for 
although our own island was destitute of life, we were in the heart 
of a wilderness, and the mainland and large island must be swarm 
with all kind of four-footed creatures, and no very prolonged swimming 
was necessary to reach us. in any other country it would not have 
cause a moment’s interest--interest of the kind we felt, that is. 
in our canada camp the bear were for ever grunt about among 
the provision bags at night, porcupine scratch unceasingly, and 
chipmunk scuttle over everything. 

“my daughter is overtired, and that’s the truth of it,” explain 
maloney presently when he rejoin us and had examine in turn the 
other paw-marks. “she’s been overdo it lately, and camp-life, you 
know, always means a great excitement to her. it’s natural enough. if 
we take no notice she’ll be all right.” he pause to borrow my tobacco 
pouch and fill his pipe, and the blunder way he fill it and 
spill the precious weed on the ground visibly belie the calm of his 
easy language. “you might take her out for a bit of fishing, hubbard, 
like a good chap; she’s hardly up to the long day in the cutter. show 
her some of the other island in your canoe, perhaps. eh?” 

and by lunch-time the cloud had pass away as suddenly, and as 
suspiciously, as it had come. 

but in the canoe, on our way home, have till then purposely ignore 
the subject uppermost in our minds, she sudden spoke to me in a way 
that again touched the note of sinister alarm--the note that kept on 
sound and sound until finally john silence came with his great 
vibrate presence and relieved it; yes, and even after he came, too, 
for a while. 

“i’m ashamed to ask it,” she said abruptly, as she steer me home, 
her sleeve roll up, her hair blow in the wind, “and ashamed of 
my silly tear too, because i really can’t make out what cause them; 
but, mr. hubbard, i want you to promise me not to go off for your long 
expeditions--just yet. i beg it of you.” she was so in earnest that 
she forgot the canoe, and the wind caught it sideways and made us roll 
dangerously. “i have try hard not to ask this,” she added, bring 
the canoe round again, “but i simply can’t help myself.” 

it was a good deal to ask, and i suppose my hesitation was plain; for 
she went on before i could reply, and her beseech expression and 
intensity of manner impress me very forcibly. 

“for another two week only----” 

“mr. sangree leaves in a fortnight,” i said, seeing at once what she 
was driving at, but wonder if it was best to encourage her or not. 

“if i knew you were to be on the island till then,” she said, her face 
alternate pale and blushing, and her voice tremble a little, “i 
should feel so much happier.” 

i look at her steadily, wait for her to finish. 

“and safer,” she add almost in a whisper; “especially--at night, i 
mean.” 

“safer, joan?” i repeated, thinking i had never seen her eyes so soft 
and tender. she nod her head, keeping her gaze fixed on my face. 

it was really difficult to refuse, whatever my thought and judgment 
may have been, and somehow i understood that she spoke with good 
reason, though for the life of me i could not have put it into words. 

“happier--and safer,” she said gravely, the canoe give a dangerous 
lurch as she lean forward in her seat to catch my answer. perhaps, 
after all, the wisest way was to grant her request and make light of 
it, ease her anxiety without too much encourage its cause. 

“all right, joan, you queer creature; i promise,” and the instant look 
of relief in her face, and the smile that came back like sunlight to 
her eyes, made me feel that, unknown to myself and the world, i was 
capable of considerable sacrifice after all. 

“but, you know, there’s nothing to be afraid of,” i add sharply; and 
she look up in my face with the smile women use when they know we are 
talk idly, yet do not wish to tell us so. 

“_you_ don’t feel afraid, i know,” she observe quietly. 

“of course not; why should i?” 

“so, if you will just humour me this once i--i will never ask anything 
foolish of you again as long as i live,” she said gratefully. 

“you have my promise,” was all i could find to say. 

she head the nose of the canoe for the lagoon lie a quarter of a 
mile ahead, and paddle swiftly; but a minute or two later she pause 
again and stare hard at me with the dripping paddle across the thwarts. 

“you’ve not heard anything at night yourself, have you?” she asked. 

“i never hear anything at night,” i reply shortly, “from the moment i 
lie down till the moment i get up.” 

“that dismal howling, for instance,” she went on, determined to get 
it out, “far away at first and then get closer, and stopping just 
outside the camp?” 

“certainly not.” 

“because, sometimes i think i almost dream it.” 

“most likely you did,” was my unsympathetic response. 

“and you don’t think father has heard it either, then?” 

“no. he would have told me if he had.” 

this seem to relieve her mind a little. “i know mother hasn’t,” she 
added, as if speak to herself, “for she hear nothing--ever.” 

* * * * * 

it was two nights after this conversation that i woke out of deep 
sleep and heard sound of screaming. the voice was really horrible, 
break the peace and silence with its shrill clamour. in less than 
ten seconds i was half dress and out of my tent. the scream had 
stop abruptly, but i knew the general direction, and ran as fast as 
the dark would allow over to the women’s quarters, and on get 
close i heard sound of suppress weeping. it was joan’s voice. and 
just as i came up i saw mrs. maloney, marvellous attired, fumble 
with a lantern. other voice became audible in the same moment behind 
me, and timothy maloney arrived, breathless, less than half dressed, 
and carry another lantern that had gone out on the way from being 
bang against a tree. dawn was just breaking, and a chill wind blew in 
from the sea. heavy black cloud drove low overhead. 

the scene of confusion may be better imagine than described. question 
in frightened voice fill the air against this background of 
suppress weeping. briefly--joan’s silk tent had been torn, and the 
girl was in a state border upon hysterics. somewhat reassure by 
our noisy presence, however,--for she was plucky at heart,--she pull 
herself together and try to explain what had happened; and her broken 
words, told there on the edge of night and morning upon this wild 
island ridge, were oddly thrill and distressing convincing. 

“something touched me and i woke,” she said simply, but in a voice 
still hush and broken with the terror of it, “something push 
against the tent; i felt it through the canvas. there was the same 
sniff and scratch as before, and i felt the tent give a little 
as when wind shakes it. i heard breathing--very loud, very heavy 
breathing--and then came a sudden great tear blow, and the canvas 
rip open close to my face.” 

she had instantly dashed out through the open flap and scream at 
the top of her voice, thinking the creature had actually got into the 
tent. but nothing was visible, she declared, and she heard not the 
faintest sound of an animal making off under cover of the darkness. 
the brief account seem to exercise a paralyse effect upon us all 
as we listen to it. i can see the dishevelled group to this day, the 
wind blow the women’s hair, and maloney crane his head forward to 
listen, and his wife, open-mouthed and gasping, leaning against a pine 
tree. 

“come over to the stockade and we’ll get the fire going,” i said; 
“that’s the first thing,” for we were all shake with the cold in 
our scanty garments. and at that moment sangree arrive wrap in a 
blanket and carry his gun; he was still drunken with sleep. 

“the dog again,” maloney explain briefly, forestall his questions; 
“been at joan’s tent. torn it, by gad! this time. it’s time we did 
something.” he went on mumble confused to himself. 

sangree grip his gun and look about swift in the darkness. i 
saw his eyes flame in the glare of the flicker lanterns. he made a 
movement as though to start out and hunt--and kill. then his glance 
fell on the girl crouch on the ground, her face hidden in her 
hands, and there leap into his features an expression of savage 
anger that transform them. he could have face a dozen lion with a 
walking-stick at that moment, and again i like him for the strength of 
his anger, his self-control, and his hopeless devotion. 

but i stop him go off on a blind and useless chase. 

“come and help me start the fire, sangree,” i said, anxious also to 
relieve the girl of our presence; and a few minutes later the ashes, 
still glowing from the night’s fire, had kindle the fresh wood, 
and there was a blaze that warm us well while it also lit up the 
surrounding tree within a radius of twenty yards. 

“i heard nothing,” he whispered; “what in the world do you think it is? 
it surely can’t be only a dog!” 

“we’ll find that out later,” i said, as the other came up to the 
grateful warmth; “the first thing is to make as big a fire as we can.” 

joan was calm now, and her mother had put on some warmer, and less 
miraculous, garments. and while they stood talk in low voice 
maloney and i slip off to examine the tent. there was little enough 
to see, but that little was unmistakable. some animal had scratch up 
the ground at the head of the tent, and with a great blow of a powerful 
paw--a paw clearly provided with good claws--had struck the silk and 
torn it open. there was a hole large enough to pass a fist and arm 
through. 

“it can’t be far away,” maloney said excitedly. “we’ll organise a hunt 
at once; this very minute.”
